THE RED PAGE .
Some Bad Verse.
THE desire of man to be a poet is, like the
vermiform appendix, the survival of some-
thing 1 which probably had its use one day.
Ifc is also a desire which has survived in most
cases the capacity for its fulfilment. Probably nine
persons out of ten attempt “ poetry ” of somd sort
at some age, infancy, amoraney, or dotage. Fortu-
nately a natural modesty—a very proper instinc-
tive feeling that one’s poetry is a sacred thing, not
to be exposed before profane eyes—keeps the great
bulk of poetic effort to a limited and appreciative
circle of one. Another great mass is timidly ex-
hibited only to a lover or an intimate. There re-
mains as a residue the “ poetry ” which is sent in
to the papers. A little, marvellously little,
of that justifies the desecration of the writer’s
mental hearth, the profanation of the inmost
domesticities of his soul, and is added to the
golden store of the world’s accepted poetry. Much
more is graceful verse, the interesting disclosures
of the results of flirtation with the Muses, and it
helps an occasional newspaper to be readable.
Very much more is the ludicrous expression of
bathetic ineptitude, and the stale re-hash of
butchered thoughts. Of this the quantity sent to
The Bulletin (and probably other papers) is
enormous.
Sometimes bad verse is really pathetic in its
badness linked with its obvious sincerity ; some-
times it has a real humor in its unconscious
comedy; most often it is maddening for its
sliameless plagiarism or its wearying common-
placeness.
There js only one justification for attempting to
write “ poetry a feeling that one must sing,
that the emotion is too strong for prose, and that
the heart-beats must find response in rhythm
beats. Leaving out of the question “poetry/’ a
writer may also justifiably drop into verse if'he is
reasonably skilful in the use of words, if be has
some ear for music, and if he has an idea which is
best expressed in numbers. Most bad “poetry”
is written because of the misconception that the
bald statement of circumstances in rhyming lines
makes poetry. And that sort of bad stuff is
usually .not even interesting enough to earn a place
m this column of Bad Verse. But occasionally, in
really naive hands, it acquires a certain amount
of humor. This, for instance, from a “poem”
sent in last month—
WHEN DAD GOES FISH IN.
I often chuckle to niyself ,
when dad takes down the rod
And sez I’m goin to the creek
to try and hook a cod
you see him rippin up the ground
where worms are hidden deep
I often wonder that their gosts
dont haunt him in his sleep
and here and there along the Creek
he lifts up bark and logs
If ever you are up our way
come and watch dad ketch frogs
I mostly have to go with him
to carry lines and bait
And I suppose I shouldent kick
it is the hand of fate
but then I often wish that dad would get another mate
I lay beneath a wattle tree
no anglin for me
but I watch dad he lifts the line
so carfully and free
And Just as I am dozing off
I know there something crook
When dad yells out come here, you brat,
Me pants has caught the hook
I rush unto his rescue,
for I sympathise with dad
and when I say you’ve hooked a Shark
my word don’t he get mad
I quickly release the hook
but ere I’ve time to steer
out of his way I mostly get
a beauty on the ear
but dad he makes another start
with worms and nasty things
and I quietly go to sleep again
to dream of angels wings,
and. Just as I’m admiring
visions rangin past in white
I hear dad swearin pretty loud
because the fish wont bite
etc., etc.
Here is a specimen of didactic and scientific
poetry which was sent to this office with evident
great earnestness:
If we persist in violating nature’s laws
Forcing on sea and sky an office which the earth should
perform
As surely as an effect follows a cause
So surely might we expect famine inundation or de-
vastating storm.
If the purification of matter where unpent
Should cause the earth to tremble beneath a blazing sky
What would be the effect if ocean leagued with the air to
resent
Having thrust upon them such an office, the filth of
mortals to purify.
Such a compact entered into amid the booming thunder
of the skies . ,
The moon responsive on her charge relaxing control
The tides released from bondage above their margin rise
And swallow up offending cities whole.
When man usurps the earth’s wondrous power
Is it likely that minor achievements can only prove vain
The earth which transforms the vilest rottenness into the
odor of the flower
Or into the tempting fruit and life supporting grain.
Yet another sample—a bald statement of facts
boldly labelled as “a poem” :
POEM: WHEN YOU ARE “ON THE WALLABY.”
Now tramping it is not the game
That it is cracked up to be,
But you’re not bound down like city life,
That’s consoling—you are free ;
But it ain’t all beer arid skittles,
As many people think ;
For you’ll often walk for miles
Without a drop to drink.
When old Sol shines fiercely on you
With his rays so fierce and strong,
And your tired legs and feet ache
’Till you can’t drag them along,
You welcome night and water,
' For you know here ends your tramp ;
And you set the billy boiling
While you pick a place to camp.
All these have at Jeast the merit of being
ludicrous. The rea,lly maddening samples of bad
verse are those which, unexceptional in rhyme,
metre and grammar,, say nothing new, suggest
nothing new, but, with deadly complacency,
repeat old ideas in old and palsied phrases, An
example (not one of the worst: the worst are so
exasperating that, they can’t be permitted to
remain in the office even for a week):
VOICES OF THE NIGHT.
The moon rides o’er a cloudy sky,
The weary world seems sleeping,
Save for the mopoke’s lonely cry
Its midnight vigil keeping,
k*H?u one w h°se heart is crushed with pain,
Whose soul with sorrow’s burning,
Who’s sought on earth for peace in vain,
To God for pity turning.
It dies away, and all is still,
clouds the moon are veiling)
B hush ! a sound comes from the hill—
The curlew’s mournful wailing.
Like cry of one with grief distrest,
Who mourns a friend departed,
nni? v^en khc world has gone to rest
Then weep the broken hearted.
Now from the gully over there
A loud and weird-like howling
Proclaims the fox has left his lair,
And in search of prey comes prowling.
That cry I hear full many a time,
When shades of night are falling,
As though ’twas speaking some dread crime
And was for vengeance calling.
Oh wild bush cries, how dear to me,
The midnight hath awoken .
For they e’er seem in sympathy
With my own heart nigh broken.
One grateful fact is that the Bad Poet is
generally meek. The good poet, even the good
versifier, has the courage of a lion, the vanity of
a peacock and the skin-resistance of a hippo-
potamus. But the Bad Poet mostly accepts his
rejections with a chastened pity for the ignorance
of the man who is so lacking in sense as to decline
his work. 1 here are exceptions, and occasionally,
when our ideas of politics or of poetry don’t suit
some reader, this sort of thing comes to hand (a
genuine sample received last month):
THE BOSS OF THE BULLETIN.
If you want a low down Liar
i Who lies for the sake of tin
No matter how vile
If it answers his while
That’s the Boss of the Bulletin.
If .vou want a crawling Liar
Who stoops to the lowest thing
If it sells his foul rag
And swells out his bag
That’s the Boss of the Bulletin.
Ciiorus :
If you want a damned imposter
A disgrace to the name of man
Whose glory is shame
Foul slander his game
That’s the Boss of the Bulletin.

Representative Australians,” by Charles
JNuttall ; a series of very fine lithographs of
Australian public men, and of various Governors
and their wives—who are hardly “Australians,”
representative or otherwise. Mr. Nuttall’s work
is generally excellent, and the printing and bind-
ing idl that can be desired. From G. Robertson
and Co.
“ T he Australian Baby,” by Mrs. Everett Ellis
(Ward, Lock and Co.) This is the age for
children ; the books devoted to them are number-
less. Mrs. Ellis' work is intended to instruct
mothers in the feeding and care of the child from
birth to school-age. Its precepts are sound and
common-sensible.
** The Tale of Peter Rabbit,” by Beatrice Potter
(I. Warner and Co.), a daintily printed and
bound booklet for very young children, with a
not-too-obtrusive moral.
“ Just-so Stories,” by Rudyard Kipling, illus-
trated by himself (Macmillan and Co.). Mr.
Kipling should certainly turn his back resolutely
on any temptations to sing again the war-paeans
of Jingoism, and devote himself to story-telling of
a non-political kind. These “Just-so Stories”
are vivid and humorous, enjoyable by the adult
as well as by the child. The illustrations show
that Kipling could have won a more than respect-
able place in black-and-white art if he had chosen,
(k rom Geo. Robertson and Co.).
l Tide,” by Sarah Greene, another book
ot the vasty of the Basins” type; very well
done m a quiet, religious tone. But interest in
this class of work is dying out; the first tillage in
anew held bnngs the rich harvest which comes
from virgin soil ; subsequent efforts must have
poorer results. (From Geo. Robertson & Co.)
‘The Wings of the Dove,” by Henry James, a
lengthy work m his recent Mereditbian style;
everything said roundaboutly, and, on a final
judgment, the roundaboutness is not justified.
It s all very well to go through a maze to find a
hne castle, but not to come across a villa. (From
Geo. Robertson & Co.) ,

Louise Mack’s “An Australian Girl in London ”
is, on the whole, disappointing. That the writer
has insight and a vivid power qf description is
shown occasionally ; there is also shown more than
occasionally a flurried effort to be picturesque •
and the sum total of new impression to be gleaned
doesn t justify a book. Images and-similes are
sometimes painfully strained and artificial Of
gums.
I lay with my face against their white kid trunks
smooth as gloves.
Of egos
The ego aches as if it were in a cramped sardine box.
It wants to go off by itself sometimes, in the queer wav
egos have. But the sardine box holds it tight All it
can do is to kick a little.
Of Australians—
,,MJ' countrymen are splendid beings. I love the
thought of them, in their big, grey, felt hats, with their
deep quiet Australian eyes, full of plains and snakes and
great silences.
But there is much of acute observation and many
a bright word-picture to atone for what comes
perilously close to nonsense.
Of riding in a rickshaw in Ceylon :
You feel like a queen. You have a man—a, flesh-and-
blood man—running in harness between the shafts of
your tall black perambulator with two big wheels.
How valuable is the glimpse this gives into
woman’s real soul ! That’s the best feature of
Louise Mack’s work—its candour. Our copy is
from Geo. Robertson & Co.
“Is England to be Ruined?” is an effective
pamphlet for Protection by Robert Shapland, of
London. He points out
We buy or import £415,000,000 worth or thereabout
and export £226,000,000. How can this last ? Such a
state of business could never continue if these were the
only agents at work ; but as I previously stated, interest
on foreign loans comes in and fills the gap. Take this
interest or income from foreign investments away, Eng-
land’s trade would then be found rotten and ruinous.
In other words, England is now living on her fat
-—the fat she accumulated when she was pro-
tectionist. The only remedy, it is argued, is
a prompt resource to Protection.
Herman Merivale, in his “Bar, Stage and
Platform,” sums up Buckle, the man who wrote
the “History of Civilisation” as being about the
most insufferable literary bore he ever had to listen
to “who thundered solemn misstatements by the
hour.”
Jt
One G. S. Guest, a novelist of no importance
himself, talks like a man of common-sense con-
cerning the Corelli class of book writers :
No one seems to write quietly and with consideration
about the great successes—l mean your 100,000 copies,
ladies and gentlemen. Probably the figures bother people,
irritating some so that they can be barely civil, and fasci-
nating others so that they drown themselves in eulogy.
But I should like the great successes to be temperately
explained to me. After all it can hardly be that a book
which is read by several millions has no interesting
quality about it, literary or otherwise . . . Will not
some one, leaving the seat of the scornful, or rising from
the floor of abasement, tell me quickly and coherently
what it all means. My appeal is to the 100,000-copy
writers. One of these successful novels was an attack,
and not a skilful attack, on the Reyn an Catholic Church.
The latest introduces an important -statesman and per-
sonages more illustrious. You or I might attack the
Church or introduce statesmen and illustrious personages
till we were black' in the face, and no one would take
any notice. lam tired of scoffers. I want to understand.
An art so sure of its effects cannot be altogether uncon-
scious. Won’t he or she explain ? .
G. S. Street voices the sentiment of many who,
possesssing enough of cultivated intelligence and
critical faculty to put them beyond the pale of
Marie Corelli’s public, are nevertheless very tired
of the little literary scoffers. The Scoffers pride
themselves on their dislike for the stuff which
millions of less superior persons—“educated,” as
the term goes—can i greedily devour. Yet these
readers of Corelli, Boothby ar.d Co. are for the
most part the very “fools” to whom the scoffers
address their little fictions with the vain hope
of scoring a popular success. “The Sorrows
of Satan,” for instance, caught the fancy
of people not incapable of appreciating the
recognised “ masterpieces ” of English fiction,
although it was intolerable nonsense —on
account of its pretentiousness—to those who
want artistic verisimilitude in any fiction
which has nothing of humor to recommend it.
Mane Corelli and co. possess a certain power of
imposition unattainable by the ordinary novelist.
Is an entire absence of humor essential to these
popular literary impostors, or does the secret of
their art lie in their superior knowledge
0 ... things that • the general public
will, or will not, take seriously ? By some
means dr other they escape the ridicule
of the 100,000 who buy their books, and the
millions who read them. The educated minority,
including their jealous rivals, snort at them, point
out the transparent absurdities in their work, but
don’t attempt to analyse the literary dust which
those successful tricksters throw into the fairly
clear-sighted eyes of ordinary novel readers. The
Corelli an dupes are not so very foolish as the
scoffers affect to think them. They are even
critics themselves sometimes. But they are weak-
minded, short-sighted, or what you will—where a
dozen or two popular novels are concerned. Why ?

viewed in “ B.” 30/10/’O2) contains an ode by Will
Op lvie - But his address is “Kelso, Scot-
land. Some of the specially Australian grovel
over the Coronation :
Australia greets her King to-day.
The digger fills his pipe with pride,
The squqtter takes a lordlier stride,
And every maiden’s heart is gay.
Notable, also, that 1084 odes were sent it; that
Ist prize went to Rev. Watt, 2nd. ter Rev. Wat-
kins, 3rd to Rev. Wood ; but that Lucy Eveline
Smith, of Dunedin, was bracketed 3rd. Possibly
the very worst of the 73 odes for publication is
that by the Rural Dean of Wollongong, N.S.W. :
England, Scotland, Wales and Ireland
Joined in love are here as one,
With the other members on whose
Empire never sets the sun.
His idea of an Ode is a detailed description of the
scene in the Abbey-
Now the Archbishop, in the words prescribed,
Speaks to the King, who makes rep v, then kneels
And, placing on the Book his hand,'declares : '
“ The tilings which I now here have„protmsed
I will perform and keep.” . . .
And ail the Peers and Peeresses put on
Their coronets, the Bishops don. their caps,
The trumpets blare, drums sound, and guns are heard.
There is a curious-insistence on the idea that a
king is not really a king until the formal corona-
tion ceremony lias been gone through.
Edward is crowned, all’s well with Britain now,
says .someone from Malay. Most curious of all,
however are the requests for Divine aid in smash-
ing up the other fellow.
While in faith we follow Thee, do Thou, the waves divid-
ing, ...
Make the pathway clear for us, but lead our foes astray !
So says a bard whp talks the sentiments of Oliver
Cromwell in the accents of Kipling. But another
couplet might have been Cromwell’s own :
Let all our guns be shotted with Thy word,
And in their throats Thy voice alone be heard 1
“Titus Salt” writes :
Was reading, the other day, the “ Mysterious Mr
Sabin,” ,by Oppenheim—at least I think it was Oppenheim
but it doesn’t matter. It reminded me considerably of
“Pro Patria,” arid some half-a-dozen other works of a
new class of fiction that has sprung up lately. In this
class the British empire is ever tottering to its fall
Somebody has discovered the special, solitary, particular'
button which, if pressed, will wreck the whole show
The button .hqs been there all the time, but nobodv
noticed it before, else the empire would have fallen lono-
ago. Somebody has ascertained that the empire is
balanced on a hair, and he has located the hair. Or else
there is a document somewhere which, if found, will blow-
all Britannia kite high. And a dark foreign emissary is
rushing at the button, or making a bee-line for the hair,
or is half-a-mile ahead of everybody else in the race for
the document. The enemy isn’t going to raise a huge
army and fleet and crumple up Britain in the usual way
by a long war ; when he finds out the weak spot, and
puts his finger on it, things will simply crumple up by
themselves. And the trouble is that, after the novelist
has lashed his readers to frenzy for 200 pages—after the
empire has tottered frightfully, and the nation has
realised its peril and its helplessness, and has
been unable to relish its beer owing.to the greatness
of the crisis—a time comes when it is necessary to
explain what the mystery was. And the novelist never
has an adequate mystery to offer. After: working up all
this frenzy about how, the empire was balanced on a hair
and the foreign miscreant was going straight for the hair
with a pair of scissors, and how, if he got there, one-sixth
of the globe was going to fall in a heap and be so helpless
that any foreign alderman might smack it on the ear with
impunity, he can’t invent a feasible hair. In “ The
Mysterious Mr. Sabin ” the horrid secret is about to be
sold to Germany, and when Germany gets it Kaiser
William is going to come over and reign in London, and
give Edward VII. a job as sentry at his palace gate. The
secret is hidden in the house of a deranged admiral, and
round that house rage conspiracy, intrigue and the devil-
ment of 47,000 devils. And after all the secret only
amounts to some plans of coast defences -and particulars
about a number of defective ironclads.
In another work of the same character the empire is
tottering to its fall,.and the cause of the totter proves to
be an attempt to drive a tunnel from France to England.
In a third, it is a flying machine ; in a fourth, a scheme
for a great invasion of Chinamen, who are going to invert
their joint and several vegetable basket over Britain and
smother it. Whether this hysterical variety of literature
is a sign of national decadence or not is hard to say; but
it is very weary stuff to read—very weary and cheap and
crude. In the horrid and twopenny variety of melo-
drama the fate of the hero usually depends on a lost
document. This is the same thing in a swollen form,
and it is also horrid and twopenny. Perhaps it would be
more truthful to say that it is half-penny.
Mary Gilmore:—
In regard to the Literary Woman directly using her
own personal experiences as copy, it is a matter entirely
regulated by her sense of honor and her feeling of per-
sonal dignity. The things intimately one’s own, and in-
timately one’s self, are not for the public, as a rule. Of
course, the utilitarian woman, the notoriety-loving
woman, and the impulsive woman, all have characteris-
tics which war with the natural human reticence which
we call modesty; and What they may do can only be
gauged by the weight of these qualities in the totality of
their make-up. But the over-plus of these qualities is
abnormality. Indirectly, I dare say, all that happens
to one works in somewhere and somehow, leavening the
whole. Without experience, from the infant’s first sense
of cold, to the last link of thought we have, man would
be less than a jelly fish.
Rita Sunyasee on the Woman Writer question :
Seeing that ideal matrimony should be based on Love
(a little further removed from mutual admiration), and
Love, analysed to its essentials, means, roughly put, the
sinking of many idiosyncrasies on both sides for a perfect
consummation to be expected—the bias of common-sense,
based on the ethics of domesticity, is against a happy,
much less a satisfactory, uniSn betwen men and women
writers. Like the circus-girl, actresses, and other units
of womanhood, who, quite worthy in themselves of per-
petuating the best traditions of their particular sphere,
are quite beyond the pale of domesticity by reason of
Bohemian existence, and further by the exigencies of
their callings, the Literary woman, in a greater degree
than the man-writer, must subdue her mental activity and
be prepared to face the music of the domestic hearth
rather as it is than as her fancy hears it ! The average
school inarm, typewriter, woman bookkeeper or clerk,
aye ! the up-to-date, dainty, devising milliner is straying
from the ways and means of domesticity—and the woman
who rises with the daily expectation of having her cook-
ing, washing and womanly tasks generally performed for
het- by others is not angelic enough, on the airy founda-
tion of a woman’s love dream, to sink her individuality,
not to say ink-pot inclinations, and turn energies long
dormant (if she ever possessed them) in the ways of
motherhood and true womanhood generally.
“ R.A.” writes :
“ Prooshan ” (Red Page, 22/11/’O2) gives rather poor
consolation to Henry Lawson—an Australian Crown as
the price _ of padding through Hell’s region with
“ Matilda ” up. “Prooshan” says: “ There is more to
win than mere pecuniary remuneration as a ready hand
on magazines in London.” Given rest, Lawson will do as
good work as ever he has done. Among free-lances the
“ Australian Crown ” has long been held up to contempt
as not being an asset to the extent of providing a reliable
barrier against starvation. If Lawson be advised by
experience, he will use his bit of bay leaves to boil the
pot in London, and abandon the Australian Crown with
the glory of fitting it on the head of “ Matilda” in the
awful bush. One “mere pecuniary remuneration ”in
the stomach is worth two Australian Crowns in the
bush.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London Times, as an epitaph for the
British nation
Here lies a people whose rare gifts of energy, good
sense, and integrity raised them above all other peoples
when those qualities were all in all. But they lacked
intelligence and despised it; and, lacking intelligence,
they fell from their high estate as soon as the world’s
prizes began to be awarded only to those who brought a
trained and disciplined intelligence to the understanding
of its laws—moral, economic, and physical.
The “Greatest Kentry on Uurth ” not
only takes first prize for fancy religions, but also,
apparently, for wildly-weird friendly societies.
At the recent annual convention, at Milwaukee,
of the Concatenated Order of Hoo Hoos, elections
were made of Snark of the Universe, Senior Hoo
Hoo, Junior Hoo Hoo, Bojum, Scrive-noter,
Jabberwock, Custocation, Arcanoper, and Gur-
deen.
A Message.
You will come back !
Because the quick pulsating breath of Spring,
The pungent scent of wild flow’rs burgeoning
Will bring you memories of a time like this
When langorous West winds made you crave to
kiss ;
And I was there with crimson tempting mouth
To quench your thirsting, yet increase your
drouth.
Oh, heart of mine ! You cannot stay away
When Spring dawn lengthens into summer day,
When all the mating instinct’s in the air
You will remember that my face is fair,
No pillow softer, whiter, than my breast,
No heart so true as where your head may rest.
Oh, warm winds, send him thought of days to be !
Oh, Springtide, make his pulses thrill for me !
Oh, wild-flowers, blazing by the bridle-track,
I’ll love you if you bring my lover back !
NELLIE M. FIELD.
The Gift of the Wind.
She came as an amorous wind,
Dew-sweet with the incense of flowers,
And swept from a lust-weary mind,
The dust of inglorious hours;
She fell as a dusky-red rose
Athwart the worn path to my heart,
And swift from the desert arose
Fairy blooms of a sovereign art.
Love dreamed in the shade of the palms,
Love woke where the waterfalls gushed.
Dream, love, in the warmth of my arms
Until the wild heart-beat is hushed.
So let the tense gaze of black eyes
Sink deep to the earth-swelling soul,
Then cease the low plaint of the sighs
Still breathed for the freshness I stole !
O witching the generous night,
And wondrous the song-haunted day,
Adrift when forbidden delight
Lures all men’s senses astray !
Adrift when the pulses of earth
Beat fast with the glories of love,
And all that of passion has worth,
Is wafted by gods from above !
A week and a night—we were glad,
Merged deep in an exquisite bliss,
A week and a day—we were sad,.
Clasped close in a soul-searing kiss'!
The wind-witch when soughing from south,
Sweet-lipped with red cedar and pine,
Blows kiss after kiss on my mouth —
Ah, love, that these kisses were thine !
THE HERMIT.
To Arms.
“ To Arms ! ” —Love’s eager battle-cry
Rings bell-like through the night.
The patrol-hearts beat swift, and by
Go lip-battalions bright.
“ To Arms ! ” —the sweet kiss-cavalcades
Ride down through vale and glen.
The picket mouths of glowing maids
Meet those of sentry-men.
“ To Arms ! ” —sweet arms of laughing Love—
White squadrons of the stars
Bret in their barrack-yards above
And long for like red wars.
*
“To Arms ! ” the hearts’ fierce cohorts come,
All hotly marching on;
Prince Passion beats his battle drum,
Or waves his gonfalon.
“ To Arms ! ” like ruthless-veined hussars
Men hearts in squadrons go ;
With champing bits and bridle-bars,
Their souls ride out a-row.
“ To Arms ! ” —the tender Panoply—
The stirrup-stars of Love,
Like sabre-gleams of ecstasy
Flash in the darkled grove.
“To arms ! ” Sweet women, hear the call
Ring past your bosom’s gate !
Reach down your hearts from hook and wall,
And meet man’s mouth elate.
“To arms ! —To arms ! ” Love’s battle-cry
Is ringing thro’ the night;
The vanguard-hearts march beating by,
The phalanxed lips shine bright.
GRANT HERVEY.

A Torturing Liver.
“KN EXPERIMENT."
The Best Bargain I Ever Made.
The Case of K|rs. B. LAWUIV
(by a reporter).
The inventions of paper and the press
have put an end to all the restraints with
which our forefathers were fettered, and
they have not only made everyone a writer,
but have also enabled every mind to pour
itself into print and diffuse itself over the
whole intellectual world. The consequences
are inestimable, as, if the slow and laborious
operation of writing on parchment was still
in existence it would be difficult to acquaint
all our readers with such important matters
as are spoken of by Mrs. Bridget Lawler,
of Northwood-street, Camperdown.
“ I was once stricken with a terrible
malady,” began that lady. “It was an
attack of rheumatism which started in my
feet and worked its way into my hands, the
pain being so great that the joints felt as if
they were being screwed round in a vice. I
was very stout when I was first taken ill,
but four years’ intense suffering took all the
flesh off my body, besides making me very
weak and irritable. Some days the pains
were not so bad, while at other times they
were so severe that I was a regular cripple
through them, and could not get out of bed.
At the best my limbs were almost useless,
for I had to go from one room to another
on my hands and knees, so that will tell
you how sorely pressed 1 was.”
“ Did any complications arise?” queried
the newspaper man.
“ They did indeed—a'ny amount of them,
for my system seemed to be upset right
throughout. One of the chief causes of my
weakness was through my food coming back
almost as soon as it was taken, and I hardly
got five minutes’ respite from retching both
day and night.s>No wonder, then, that my
eyes felt swollen and that my sight was bad,
and that I used to lie in a state of exhaustion
for hours, with my head pierced by sharp
pains as if rheumatism was there also. Oh !
it was a wretched time for me, and one
which I would not go through again for
thousands of pounds. I thought the vomit-
ing was caused through food being taken
against my inclination, but I was bound to
eat a little sometimes, even though my
appetite was gone, or I would have collapsed
altogether. A heavy kind of pain in the
chest was very troub'esome, too, as it
seemed to weigh me down to such an extent
that I could not get my breath properly,
and thoughts of smothering often entered
my mind, although I was not afraid of
doing so in my sleep, because I very rarely
got any. It was quite an extraordinary
event for me to sleep three hours at a time,
and only happened when I was so exhausted
that even my tortures could not keep me
awake any longer ; but for all that I did
not feel rested afterwards, as the nasty
dreams I had seemed to take away all the
refreshing influences of sleep. When my
eyes opened again I still felt languid and
heavy and so low spirited and desolate that
all brightness seemed to have vanished from
my life. After taking any necessary
nourishment my stomach filled out with
wind, thus bringing into force another dis-
comfort, which frequently developed into
pgins like the cramps, and lasting till every-
thing, as usual, was vomited. About the
shoulders, too, I had reason to complain
owing to irritating pains getting a hold
there, while nothing could be more un-
welcome than the sickly taste I had in my
mouth every morning, besides having a
whitish coating on my tongue. These
symptoms made me understand very clearly
that my liver was deranged to a serious
degree, and I suppose my doctors knew that
as well, but still I did not benefit myself in
any way By remaining under their treat-
ment.”
“ How many doctors did you have ?
“ It would be very hard for me to say
exactly. Indeed, I had so many that I
cannot remember them all, and I have no
reason to keep them in mind, because not
one of them did me any good. The more I
went to the worse I got, and then I started
taking advertised pills and medicines, one
particular kind of which having such an
injurious effect upon me that after taking &
certain quantity I was almost paralysed on
one side of my body. There was very little
medicine I had not taken more or less of,
but I was now afraid to resort to any of
them again. I went on in misery for a
while without anything, but, being so help-
less that I could not even handle a cup, I
thought I would never get better that way,
and as I had tried everything but Clements
Tonic it would not do much harm to see
what that medicine was like.”
“ Was your experiment attended with any
better results ? ”
“It was the best bargain I ever made in
my life when I bought some Clements Tonic,
for there was no uncertainty or indecision
about its aciion or my internal organs,
which felt its curative propensities much
more readily than I had deemed possible.
For a long time past my nerves had been
very weak, and I was upset and tremulous
at all sorts of trifling incidents, but that
was one of my troubles which Clements
Tonic remedied straight away. My nervous
system was greatly invigorated after taking
three or four bottles, and then I ceased
meeting difficulties half-way, for I was so
much brighter in spirits and more hopeful
through receiving such benefit from
Clements Tonic that I thought I might yet
leave my sufferings behind if I gave that
medicine a fair trial. And thus when each
bottle was finished I got another, the
returns for my outlay being a good healthy
appetite and a steady falling off in my
pains. My headaches did not last long
against Clements Tonic, and all the time
my liver symptoms were losing their in-
tensity. 1 slept well at nights, because my
rheumatic pains were less violent, whilst
those about my shoulders and chest had
left altogether, leaving me to breathe with-
out the least distress. I got up with my
mouth free from its objectionable taste, and
after each meal of the day there was an
absence of flatulence and consequent pains ;
but to describe each healthful blessing
which came from every bottle of Clements
Tonic would, perhaps, take up too much of
your time. I must content myself by say-
ing that in a few weeks my internal com-
plaints were quite obliterated, and I was
able to get about my work with a cheerful
heart and a contented mind. The
strengthening and disease-removing powers
of Clements Tonic simply astounded me
after the many experiences I had with other
medicines, and I would be very ungrateful
indeed if I kept this knowledge to myself.”
“ Did your various pains leave as quickly
as the rest ? ”
“ No ; but although it took some time to
get me well and strong again in every
respect there is none the less credit due to
Clements Tonic, as it was the only medicine
which was able to bring me back to health,
and I am willing to let you publish my story
in any way you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Bkidubt Lawler, of North wood-street,
Camperdown, Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that 1 have
carefully read the annexed document, consisting
of four folios, and consecutively numbered from one
to four, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way ill}' statements which I give voluntarily, and
without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Camperdown, this
twentyflfth day of March, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
ROBERT ELSTUB, J.P.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 6, 1902.*
More Transcontinental Railway
Literature.
The Bulletin receives this letter (among
several others) from the JfiNKiNS-ridden city
of Adelaide :
Dear Bulletin.— You are very sarcastic at the expense
of South Australia because of the authorisation, of the
construction of the Transcontinental Railway to Port
Darwin on the land-grant principle under certain specified
conditions. So is Sydney D.T., which, however, shows
its hand by urging that Sydney alone should be the
terminus of the line to the Territory, and that Adelaide
is an impudent hussy for daring to contemplate making
use of its geographical advantages. But is not New South
Wales worse than South Australia in that the senior State
sells its lands for cash which it transfers to revenue,
and then borrows money in London to construct
its lines which, practically, like those of the rest
of Australia, belong to English capitalists ? The South
Australians have spent many millions on railways. They
have attempted to push the line through to Port Darwin,
and have constructed hundreds of miles of the connection
out of borrowed money. The Pine Or-eek railway (165
miles southward from Port Darwin) does not pay' working
expenses. The land between the two termini is practi-
cally without settlement save for the overland telegraph
stations, and brings in no revenue. If it were sold to
raise the money to build the remaining sections of the
railway, millions more acres would have to be alienated
than it is proposed to give under the land-grant scheme.
Yet that method would be considered legitimate by many
opponents of the present proposal. What is the differ-
ence between so constructing-the line and arranging with
capitalists to build and to work it in return for certain
specified blocks of territory ? Iq ( the first instance, the
Govt, would own the line, but would have to bear the
responsibility of working it for the benefit of the private
owners whose territory abutted on it, and whose land
would increase in value with the success of the line.
In the second instance, the grantees would build
the line and take all risks in regard to it, while
the conditions of contract would prevent them from
fleecing the public in any way, apart from the fact that
the Govt, would retain possession of the terminal portion
of the line in the South at any rate, even if they parted
with the Pine Creek line. The case of the Trans-
continental railway is distinct altogether from that of
lines such as those built by syndicates in AVest Australia,
Tasmania and Maoriland, which undoubtedly ought to
have been built by Govt. It is more of the kind of the
Canadian Pacific railway (which has done wonders for
the Dominion), or the lines which pushed over the Sierra
Nevada mountains and joined New York to San Francisco
and which would have been delayed for years had
they waited for the initiation of the Govt. —Yours,
Adelaide, Nov. 23,1902. G. R. Wilton.
P.S. —South Australia cannot afford to construct the
line herself, and she sees clearly that if the Territory
were handed over to the Commonwealth, all the influence
of the Eastern States would be exerted to deflect the
railway, so that it would run obliquely to Sydney or
Brisbane instead of to Adelaide. If the Commonwealth
would undertake to build a line from Oodnadatta to Pine
Creek within the next ten years, and to accept the
responsibility for the present debt on the Territory
(which S.A. has kept free from colored labor at great
expense for many years in the interest of a White Aus-
tralia), this State would willingly hand over her rights in
the Territory to the Federation.
The above letter states the case for the
Transcontinental land-grant syndicate to
the best advantage—so far as there is
any best advantage. The only real point
in the syndicate’s favor is the greed
and arrogance of a small gang of paro-
chialists in Sydney, whose one and only
idea of Australian progress is to bring every-
thing to Sydney, and add still further to the
congestion of traffic in the bottle-neck where
the trams run over people regularly at the
railway end of George-street. This party
demands, in the columns of Sydney Tele-
graph, that the Transcontinental line be
abandoned altogether in favor of a line which
will bear towards the coast and bring the
Northern Territory trade to Sydney. Such
an idea means building 300 or 400 miles
more railway; it means making several
hundreds of miles of line already
built by South Australia practically
worthless ; it means laying extra burdens
upon Australia (of which S.A., in addition
to losing all hope of making its partially
finished Transcontinental line payable, would
have to bear a part) for the building of use-
less mileage ; and it represents the abandon-
ment of the whole purpose with which the
line was started and the leaving of Australia
with no more real Transcontinental railway
than it had before. Also Sydney is 300
miles further from Port Darwin than Ade-
laide is, so the idea further involves a
permanent loss in the shape of extra freight
charges from port to port. Bub the proposed
scheme would bring the Territory trade to
Sydney, and in the eyes of a certain small
and narrow-minded party that covers every-
thing.
* • # *
If this party amounted to anything serious,
then the action of the Jenkins Iscariot
Government in endeavoring to get its Trans-
continental line built by a syndicate, or by
anybody who would build it on almost any
terms, might have some kind of justification.
Bub the party doesn’t amount to anything—
or at least it amounts to very little. Still,
the Australian Government should certainly
endeavor to set the matter at rest by offering,
subject to the consent of Parliament, to take
over the Territory with all its assets and
debts, and to complete the Transcontinental
line within 10, or at most, 15 years. But it
should also be stipulated 4 that South Australia
shall sell at a reasonable value the line from
Adelaide to Oodnadatta, so that the Trans-
continental shall be Federal property all
the way. And the Australian Government,
by way of giving national enterprise
an equal show with private enterprise,
should endow its railway department with
90,000,000 acres of the best available land in
the Territory, the rents to go towards sub-
sidising the railway revenue, and the pro-
ceeds of sales to go towards paying off the
railway debt. There is no need that there
should be any debt whatsoever on the new
line, for the Australian Government could
find £5,500,000 in 10 years out of taxation,
but doubtless there will be a debt from
force of habit. And if the Jenkins
Iscariot Parliament refused this offer,
which it possibly would do, seeing that
national enterprise, unlike private syndi-
cafcism, doesn’t provide boodle, tip, commis-
sions, preference shares, and the like, then
the Australian Parliament should impose a
graduated land-tax on a scale which would
make the 90,000,000-acre syndicate utterly
impossible, and which would also destroy
the other big land steals with which the
Territory is being so liberally dotted. The
Barton Government should approach
Jenkins Iscariot with a generous offer in
one hand and a club in the other, and it
should be equally ready to use either of
them.
• • • m
If the Jenkins Ministry does not come to
terms with the Australian Government, the
prospects of the Port Darwin line coming to
Sydney in the end will be better than they have
ever been before. Supposing the Australian
Parliament kills the 90,000,000-acre syndi-
cate with a graduated land-tax, as it probably
will, then that dismal public swindle will be
quite dead., and the Australian Parliament
will not be committed, as an equivalent for
its decease, to build the Transcontinental
line as a national work. The way will be
open for the Sydney agitators, and the
general irritation caused by the attempt
of S.A. to hand over such an important
enterprise to foreign speculators will be a
weapon in their hands. And if the Austra-
lian Parliament does not interfere with a
graduated land-tax the Jenkins syndicate
will probably linger for a few years and then
perish with its work less than half done.
The average railway syndicate which has its
headquarters in London is unspeakably
wasteful in its methods. The only successful
Australian private railways that the writer
can recall have been locally owned. Boodle,
commission, promoters’ profits, preference
shares, large sums paid to middlemen who
obtain the “ concession ” and re-sell it, huge
directors’ fees, office expenses, the refusal to
put up sufficient capital, and the raising of
debenture money at 5 and 6 and 7 per cent,
eat up everything. The “success 1 ' of the
"Canadian Pacific line or the United
States transcontinentals has no bearing on
the case. The S.A. line hasn’t a vast
area of rich, well-watered country to
traverse, nor has it the richest goldfield
in the world for its objective point. S.A.
can’t plank down millions in cash in addition
to the land grant to help private enterprise
out of the holes it is always falling into. It
isn’t going to make the co. a present of the*
existing line from Adelaide to Oodnadatta to
begin with, as Canada did when its great
burst of private railway enterprise started.
And it can’t prop up its syndicate perman-
ently with grants and aids, as Canada is
doing, to some extent, even yet. If the
Australian Parliament doesn’t interfere, S.A.
will probably find itself, ten years hence,
with an unfinished railway on its hands, a
huge lawsuit, a large amount of new debt
incurred in propping up the syndicate, a
great loss of land, a broken heart, and a
vanished Jenkins.
• • • •
As to whether S.A. could build the line
itself as a State enterprise, that is another
question. It has never tried to raise the
money by not only pawning its own credit
but by also setting aside 90,000,000 acres of
the best Northern Territory land, with the
assurance that the rents obtained from it
will be used exclusively to pay interest on
the railway debt, and the money derived
from sales be applied to the extinction of
that debt. Probably if it tried that experi-
ment it would find that it could get the
money in Paris or New York at a less
price than thesyndicate will pay. Theabstract
relative morality of land grant railways
and loan railways is too vast a subject to
discuss at length just now. N.S.W. and
other States sell their land, and spend the
money, and build their railways out of 3|
per cent. State loans, for which the people
have to find the interest. S.A. proposes, in
the present case, to give away the land, and
get a syndicate to build the railway with
debenture money borrowed at 5 or 6 per
cent., the people finding the interest, if it is
found at all—at least that is the usual course
in such cases. Also the Act provides that
the State may repurchase the railway with-
out getting back the land, in which case it
will just be an ordinary instance of State
ownership by means of loans—with the loss
of 90,000,000 acres thrown in. Under all
the circumstances, and all the possible frauds,
the best thing the Australian Government
can do is to approach Jenkins early with a
generous offer in one hand and the weapon
already alluded to in the other.
Premier Barton on Preferential Trade.
I am not one of those who agree with the quaking
utterances of some of the people in the press in saying
that by doing the same we shall do ourselves injury with
France or Germany. I do not fear that. Take Canada :
She at first made a preference in favor of Great Britain
of 25 per cent., then 33 1-3 per cent., and she is about to
do a little more. (Cheers.) Germany turned round and
withdrew from Canada the most-favored-nations clause.
That might have killed Canada—(laughter)—but it did
not. Canada is quite well, thank you—(laughter)—and
she is exporting more to Germany than she did before.
It is said that if we followed the example of Canada,
Germany would retaliate on our wool. Weil, take that
view. Germany at present gets her wool from the
Argentine, from the Cape, from Maoriland, and from
ourselves. She makes all her finer fabrics from merino
wool which we grow. If she did not get that wool from
us, where would she get it? (Hear, hear.) I will say
something more about that in a few days, but I will say
this now—that I am one of those who are not afraid of
other nations in the matter of doing for ourselves what
■other nations have been doing for themselves. (Cheers.)
And whether we make a reciprocal agreement with Great
Britain or take the first step ourselves by giving a pre-
ference to Great Britain, which I am prepared to do, we
shall be strengthening the Empire, and in that way we
shall be strengthening ourselves. (Cheers.) While in
England, I may add, I, as your representative, was treated
with the greatest kindness, consideration, and generosity,
■which I accepted as not intended merely for myself,
but for the people I represented.— Premier Barton at
Maitland.
In his Maitland speech, Premier Barton made a
limp and lame and sorry defence of his scheme for
■establishing a preferential tariff in favor of Great
Britain. Part of the defence was based on a great
apparent ignorance of trade conditions, both here
and in Canada. Part of it consisted in an assu-
rance that he had been hospitably received in
England, and well lodged, and liberally victualled,
and lavishly drinked, and decorated with a title—
the implication being that Australia, which got
none of this free board and lodging and refresh-
ment, and which wasn’t made a knight, but re-
mains plain Bill as aforetime, should pay for
these attentions. Australia doesn’t see the point,
but Premier Barton appeared quite seriously to
put the British hospitality which he received
among the reasons why Australia should give
Britain the trade preference which Chamberlain
■demands.
* * • *
As Premier Barton truly observes, Canada
Fas, during the last few years, charged lower duties
■on goods imported from Great Britain than on those
bought from other countries, and it has lately in-
creased this preference. The fact that Canadian
-exports to Germany dropped from 2,220,000
■dollars worth in 1899 to 1,716,000 dollars worth
in 1900 (no later figures are at present available)
may, or may not, have been one of the results of
this policy; but that is a side issue. The main
point is that Canada had every right to grant
great Britain a customs preference, while the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth has every right not to. As
a rule, a State which wants to push an export
trade, if it gives a preference at all, gives it to its
good customer —not its bad one. It gives a pre-
ference to the country to which it sells much, and
from which it buys little; and it discriminates
against the countries which buy little from it
while selling much to it. The idea is obvious
•enough ; it seeks to so arrange things that it will
transfer its buying orders to the country to which
it sells largely, on the ground that such a country
■deserves encouragement.
• • * «
Taking its trade returns in its own coinage,
Canada, when it realised the advisability of giving
a trade preference to Great Britain, found that its
■commercial position was about as follows :
Canada Canada Balance in
sold to bought from
Britain .. .. 104,998,000 32,500,000 72,498,000
dollars. dollars. dollars.
Balance
against
Canada.
'Other countries.. 59,154,000 107,823,000 48,669,000
dollars. dollars. dollars.
Naturally enough, Canada decided to give Great
Britain a special preference in the matter of
customs duties. It would have been a most
unwise country to do otherwise. It found
that Britain was willing to buy almost
•everything it ■ had to sell, while Canada
bought comparatively little from Britain in
return. On the other hand, the United States,
Germany, France, &c., were willing to sell
Canada goods to any amount, but were not willing
to buy from it. Canada decided, like any other
sensible trader, to give whatever extra encourage-
ment it could to such a good customer as Britain
had proved itself. If Britain demanded, in the
first place, that Canada should reduce its customs
duties on British goods so as to encourage its
people to use more British and less Yankee or
German goods, Britain had every right to so de-
mand. For every £1 worth of goods which
Britain bought from Canada the Dominion only
bought about 6s. worth in return, and Britain
could fairly say that it wasn’t a fair deal.
No evidence exists to show that loyalty, or de-
votion, or sentiment of any kind entered into the
matter. The average State would give special
concessions to its worst political enemy if its
worst enemy were so good a customer as this.
* • • •
But the situation in which we are requested by
Premier Barton to impose lower duties on goods
bought from Britain so that we may buy more
British goods, and to levy higher duties on foreign
goods so that we may buy less foreign goods, is
something quite different. Here are the latest
figures:—
Common- Common- -
wealth wealth Xmmon
sells to buys from wea , th<
Foreign Countries £23,032,945 £15,382,299 £7,650,646
Balance
against
Common-
wealth.
Britain .. .. 25,194,928 25,237,032 42,109
Premier Barton’s theory, therefore, is that,
because Canada gave a preference to its good
customer (Britain), we should give a preference to
our bad customer (Britain). That, because Canada
discriminated against its bad foreign customers
we should discriminate against our good foreign
customers. That because Canada gave special
terms in order to induce greater imports of
British goods when the balance of trade was
so shamefully against Britain that the latter
country had every right to demand special terms,
we should give special terms to induce larger
British imports into Australia when the balance
of trade is already against us, and if anyone has a
right to demand special concessions we are the
individual. That because Canada, when it only
bought £7,500,000 of goods per annum from
Britain, made a special effort to increase its pur-
chases, therefore Australia, which, with a far
smaller population, already buys £25,000,000
worth of British goods per annum, should also
make a special effort to increase its purchases. All
these are wildly absurd theories, yet they are
Premier Barton’s theories to the letter. Canada
found that, in trade matters, it fairly owed
Britain some £14,000,000 a year, and it made an
honest effort in the way of doing something
towards paying Britain. Premier Barton holds
that we should make a special effort to pay
Britain, though it owes us £42,000 a year, or some
like trifle.
* • • *
And Premier Barton advances as a reason —at
least the remark is so unfortunately placed that it
seems to be advanced as a reason—that he was
well treated in England, that he took the victuals
and the drink and the lodging and the title as
special compliments to Australia, and that Aus-
tralia, in consequence, should pay for them. Un-
fortunately for this line of argument Australia
looks on the victuals and the other advantages
which Premier Barton personally received as
Premier Barton’s personal matter. Also,
it regards the bill which Premier Barton
has brought back as an utterly outrageous
price to demand for that purely persona]
hospitality, even supposing it is regarded as a fair
thing for a host to present his invited guest with
any bill at all. In the circumstances, all the elo-
quence that can be scattered around re Canada’s
splendidly loyal example falls flat. Canada was
loyal when it paid Canada to be so, and when it
gained the additional satisfaction of administering
a lesson to greedy foreign customers who sought
to sell it everything and buy little or nothing in
return. It suited its own convenience both ways.
We are asked to be splendidly loyal on the same
lines when we are almost certain to lose by
it, and to make ourselves grossly unpleasant
to foreign customers who buy much from
us while we buy little in return. In trade
it is one thing to insult the man who buys from
you (as we are asked to do), and quite another
to insult the man who wants you to buy from him
(as Canada did). A seller can he insulted with
much more impunity than a buyer. Whether
Canada would have done as it did in our circum-
stances we have no means of knowing, but the
chances against it are about a thousand to one.
• » • •
There remains, of course, Premier Barton’s
assurance that our export trade with foreign
countries wouldn’t be damaged, no matter what
special disabilities we placed on these countries—
that foreign countries dare not retaliate because
they must have our wool under any circumstances.
But Australia does not live by wool alone;
in 1901 its entire wool exports were only worth
£15,000,000, and as foreign countries alone took
£23,000,000 worth of goods from us it is
evident that they bought many things of which
the off-handed Barton takes no account. In fact,
it doesn’t appear as if wool constituted half the
trade. In any case, Australia is no longer the
boas wool-growing country of the world, and it
can be dispensed with much more easily than it
could a dozen years ago. In view of that fact,
and the impossibility of deciding how far our
rivals may develop or what the future may have
in store, it is unwise to give way to needless
excitement or undue swelled head. Australia is
doing remarkably well out of commerce with
foreign countries —Germany, France, and Bel-
gium in particular. It is taking a picturesque
vengeance on the pro-Boer and other remote
characters by extracting from them, by a one-
sided process of trade, the money which paci-
fies its British creditor and prevents Bull-
Cohen rising up with Australia’s dishonoured
p.n. in his hand and calling it accursed. , A very
unwise person is the person who doesn’t know
when to let well alone, and who will * tread
on the sleeping dog, merely to see what size the
animal is when it stands up. Moreover, Premier
Barton is under suspicion of being an unreliab.e
witness in the case. He got his facts and argu-
ments from an interested party. He has just
come back from dining with an interested party.
And lie is altogether too enthusiastic about the
dinner that the interested party gave him.
Of Labor Parties.
“ In the N.S.W. Parliament the Labor party proved by
no means unreasonable over the Western Lands Bill wnen
they were made to understand the necessity of the case.
But there the nettle was grasped boldly. In South Aus-
tralia of late years the Labor party have given reasonable
consideration to pastoral relief measures. But in Sheens-
land, with a Government professedly favorable, ana a


larrre majority behind it, pastoralists have more difficulty
in getting a profitable tenure than in any other State.
“ What will the Labor party say ’ to this or that is the
first thing Ministers seem to think of, whereas the Labor
party would probably say nothing very dreadful if the
Government straightforwardly said what in their hearts
they believe, and stuck by it.”—Tiib Pastoralists
Review —the organ of militant Toryism m Australia.
' l l still vote for the Labor party. It’s all right,
but there is very little Labor about it now said
aN.S.W. working-man of the “old school, the
other day, with more than an atom of truth, when
speaking on the election of a delegate to the
now-sitting Interstate Political Labor Confer-
ence. In truth, “Labor parties” in Australia
have outgrown their name in so much as it
represented a class idea or the intolerance of what
is contemptuously dubbed “respectability by
those who rejoice in being “horny-handed.
• * ' > * *
A little consideration i\*ill show that that was
inevitable. A “Labor Party,” in the old school
sense of the word, a party of the horny-handed
for the horny-handed, honestly narrow, and nar-
rowly honest in its principles, jealously intolerant
of any “toff” element, may exist in a country like
England for the next century, or until a Revolu-
tion comes. There, class distinctions are
strongly entrenched; the so-called gentleman
regards himself as of a different class of
being to the laboring man; and Providence
is credited with keeping on hand for the
creation of humanity, meerschaum as well as
common clay. But such ideas have never been
much held in Australia. There are snobs, certainly
—hundreds, thousands of them —more offensive
snobs, perhaps, than could be found in England,
but thero is no class which is accepted as an aris-
tocracy because it was born in certain beds, and
whose ohildren will form an. aristocracy for the
same poor reason ; and there is no general body of
ignorance and superstition and reverence, to
encourage the growth of such a class. Here the
laboring man of to-day may be the well-to-do
“ gentleman ” to-morrow, the son of the shop-
keeper be a fashionable doctor or barrister, and
the “gentleman’s” son have cause to bless the
seven-shillings-a-day system on Government
works. There is, in short, not a sufficiently stable
system of social inequality to justify a “Labor”
party, in the horny-handed sense, in Australia.
• * * *
But undoubtedly many of the pioneers of the
Labor political movement thought that there was,
and laid the foundations of their party under that
misconception; but they builded better than they
knew, and about the only “class” feature of the
Labor party of to-day is its name. It is neither
composed exclusively of laborers, nor considers
exclusively laborers. It is a Reform Party, the
representative, as a rule, of most that is sincere
and earnest in State and Australian politics,
attracting support from all classes. Generally,.
too, it is more conspicuously representative of
political ability than the other parties, and secures,
therefore, somewhat more influence than the
extent of its support warrants. The hobbledehoy,
the blucher, the Tom Bent element is more con-
spicuously absent from it than from the organi-
sations to which it is opposed. More sincere,
more able, better educated, with just as much re-
gard for the graces of life as other parties, an
Australian “Labor Party” is certainly named a
little curiously.
• * * *
The title is the survival of the pioneers of the
movement who gave to the party the benefit of
their solid earnestness and sense of grievance.
They were (and are, for many survive) fine men,
those pioneers, if a little ludicrous in their narrow-
ness. Fine linen, black coats and kid gloves they
looked upon as marks of the Tory beast. (Nowa-
days a N.S.W. Labor member holds the
record as the best-dressed man in Parliament.)
Horny hands they insisted upon as the only true
marks of democratic grace (Labor members to-day
are often merchants, school-teachers, engineers,
barristers, journalists—parsons even). And from
this class-spirit at the dawn of the political
reform movement came the label ‘ ‘ Labor
Party.” It is not absolutely necessary that it
should be changed. Granted that the “ Labor
Party” has broadened until it has become
the Reform Party, has evolved into the “Edu-
cated Party,” has shown year by year an increas-
ing tendency to attract to itself all that is best,
most truly liberal and progressive in politics, the
old pioneers may yet be left the gratification of a
name which, if insufficiently descriptive, is
genuinely dignified. No one needs to be ashamed
of being a “ labor member,” though it would not
be good to be called a loafing member.
• * * *
Looking back on the past, the Labor parties of
Australia, assembled at their first conference after
practical experience in a Pan-Australian Parlia-
ment, may well congratulate themselves. They
have revolutionised the franchise conditions, set
in train a long series of measures destined to
ameliorate the lot of the working population, and
the while won for themselves a high personal
reputation for ability, thoughtfulness, sobriety,
and sincerity. Even from their enemies, reluctant
iiraise has been wrested, and grudging acknow-
edgment of their sweet reasonableness gained.
When the Pastoralists’ Review is forced to some
sort of commendation, it shows the general prac-
tical benefit of Labor party effort. But the suc-
cesses of the past do not justify a contented lazi-
ness for the future. What has been done is
splendid. Still greater work yet remains.
* * * *
That work groups itself naturally under two
headings—the one to an extent idealistic and senti-
mental, the other practical and business-like —fos-
tering and crystallising the national, as opposed to
any outside sentiment, and straightening the
national finances. These are the two great reform
tasks of future politics, mutually inter-dependent,
and necessarily conjoined. The first is impossible
without the second, the second incomplete with-
out the first. The Australian Labor Party—which
is the Reform Party, and the Educated Party-
must recognise that “My Country” which is
“ my country ” with the reservation that the title
is in pawn to the foreign money-lender is a
meaningless phrase. That section of it which
represents the cause of reform in the Australian
Parliament seems to see now, partly at any rate,
the necessity of an absolute revulsion from the
present Pawn system of finance; the State Labor
members have not progressed so far as yet,
but it is morally impossible for them to re-
fuse the lead of the Federal Labor members
if that is strongly enough given. The conference
now in session—which represents in a fashion the
whole political Labor movement as it finds ex-
pression in municipal, State and Federal politics—
might, with great advantage, give special con-
sideration to this matter. The point to be kept
in mind is that the Labor party can afford to
neglect no reform of value. Its existence in the
past has depended, and its existence in the future
will depend 1 , on its having a monopoly of the
earnest advocacy of Progress. That’s its business.
Neglect it, and room will have to be made for
another and more progressive organisation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tho Balmain Election.
Balmain South (N.S.W.) State bye-election is
absolutely unimportant so far as the personal
issue is involved. Mr. S. .T. Law or Mr. Beyers—
neither is of the least moment. But interest is
given to the struggle by the fact that it is the first
appearance of the recently-organised sectarian
movement before an Australian constituency ; if
Mr. Law retains the seat it will represent a
triumph of Orangeism and an endorsement of the
miserable attempts now being made to divide the
people of N.S. Wales into two hostile religious
camps. The facts that the electorate is one
particularly amenable to the influence of Dill-
Mackyism; and that Mr. Law as the recently
sitting member (and a “good local member” to
boot) has much in his favor, point with some
certainty to a victory for him. Yet there
could, at the present stage of N.S.W.
politics, be no more unfortunate happening,
for to win the first engagement will much
encourage the sectarian fanatics. Since the Bal-
main electors are given the chance to vote against
the “Yellow Pup,” without in any way endorsing
the “ Scarlet Woman,” they may, and, it is to be
hoped, will, take the sensible course of discouraging
one brand of sectarianism without encouraging the
other. The return of Mr. Beyers would mean
that. The solidarity pledge of the Labor party i s
involved in the issue also. Mr. Law has found the
present aconvenient opportunity todeclarethat his
conscience will not permit him to remain a pledged
Labor member. Mr. Beyers stands as the candi-
date of the “ Solidarity Labor Party.” Whilst not
swallowing whole the N.S.W. Labor party plat-
form (chiefly because of its omissions), The Bul-
letin regards that party as the most hopeful
organisation in State politics. It is a very dis-
tinct advance in education, in ability, and in
liberal views on the John See party or the
Carruthers party, and from it there may evolve
the reform administration which will do for
N.S.W. what Ballance did for Maoriland ; there
is no hope of such an evolution from the other
parties. A necessary condition of the Labor
party’s existence is its “solidarity pledge.”
Without that it would soon disappear. Conse-
quently, in the Balmain South contest, Mr. Law
stands as the enemy, Mr. Beyers as the friend of
the Labor party. If this were the main issue,
the result would be in no doubt, for Balmain
South is a labor electorate. But the miserable
religious squabble seems to be the point relied
upon to carry the constituency for Mr. Law.
The real contest is between Sectarianism and
Labor.

The New Queensland Land Bill Again.
A little over 12 months ago the Q. pastoralists
approached the Government and laid the tale of
their woes and demands before the Philpocracy.
An extension of ten years added on to the unex-
pired term of lease (five or six years in some cases,
15 or 16 in others) for half the lease, in cases of
good sheep country, was, in the eyes of pastoral
lessees, a fair thing. Now that the general elec-
tion is over and things look fairly secure again,
the desires of the sheep - lords have waxed
amazingly. Much of the best country in W.Q.
has an unexpired term of 15 or 16 years to run. If
this land be classified as 11. class it receives an
extra 21 years (the shortest extension possible
under the new Bill), making a sum total of 36 or 37
years altogether. Re-assessment can only take
place twice in the whole term, so that in many
cases a re-assessment of rent will only occur once
in 18 to 20 years. As to the possible resumptions,
56 per cent, of the land cannot be touched till the
expiration of the extended term (say about 1940
in some cases—l93o in others) —so that the Q.
sheep-kings may fairly assert that in the Bill now
before the Assembly they have a very permanent
thing indeed. The most marvellous feature
about this measure is its cool, tfnbridled auda-
city. Hitherto aggregation has been denounced
in the Q. Assembly as an evil. Pastoralists
have waxed eloquently indignant on the theme,
regardless of the great and damning fact that the
lessee himself has been, in most cases, the aggrega-
tor; legislation prohibiting dummying has been in-
corporated in the statute-book —and now, all of a
sudden, the clauses against dummying areabolished
at one fell swoop, and the lessee is left at liberty
to take up a selection on the resumption of his own
holding, and to get all his friends and relations to
dummy the remainder of the resumption at plea-
sure. It is alleged (by pastoralist advocates) that
abundance of land will be left for selection under
the new scheme. This statement, considering
that land has been selected in Q. for the last seven
years at an average of about million acres per
annum, will scarcely hold water; but, even sup-
posing it were true, the damning fact remains that
there is no provision in the new Bill to ensure that
the resumptions shall be kept for legitimate selec-
tion. Surely Minister O’Connell does not
imagine that, whereas he couldn’t prevent aggre-
gation when dummying was illegal, he will see it
discontinued now when all restrictions are re-
moved and dummying is made a respectable and
reputable practice. This practical permission to
dummy and to aggregate is the worst feature
of all in a Bill abounding in bad features.
If the lessee is to be permitted to select
and aggregate the resumption, then it must
be abundantly evident that the old squatter has
simply become the new aggregator, and that the
change of occupation is merely a nominal one.
The financial institutions are to be congratulated
on the introduction of this measure ; it does credit
to their engineering skill. It will now be possible
for the best lands in W. Queensland to be held
under lease for 40 years from the present date ;
and lands on the Darling Downs and in the Burnett
district, adjacent to the coast, may also obtain a
big extension of tenure —a thing hitherto flouted
as absurd by every man who understands the Q.
land question. Moreover, under the clause
abolishing nearly all restrictions on the sale of
Crown Lands, the very eyes will be picked out
of the western country by big monopolists,
and the future of towns such as Longreach,
Barcaldine and Hughenden utterly blighted.
Since 1859 Queensland has passed Jd Land Bills —
an average of about one per annum. If this Bill
pass in the present form there will be little need
for more land legislation, for with more than half
the land locked up for 30 or 40 years, with the
resumptions dummied by the lessees, and the pick
of the land sold in large blocks to financial insti-
tutions, tli£ hapless Queenslanders won’t have any
public estate left to legislate for.
