How Somebody Ought to do Something.
The iniquities of the immovable and irreconcilable
and indissoluble and utterly Tory Victorian House
of Landlords roused Melbourne Age the other
day to another of its vague articles about the
necessity of Somebody doing Something. This is
its appeal:—
Ordinarily the Legislative Council is immovable. It
tand9 buttressed by the terras of the Constitution Act so
that it may resist the whole united will of the people.
There are no regular means of making it conform to
reason and good sense. But Ministers with resolution
and knowledge may find means, in an appeal to the
people, of overcoming this stumbling block in the path of
Constitutional government. It is always open to a
Ministry which realises the full responsibility of office to
seek from a direct referendum to the people such direction
as may be its warranty for extraordinary actions. There
are some evils too deeply seated to be remediable by
regular means. The Legislative Council has long
been such an evil. It seems to be culminating
in its career of tactless obstruction. Some of
its members have thrown out warnings that it will
treat Constitutional reform in the immediate future as
it has treated woman suffrage and plural voting abolition
in the past. Ministers have hoped almost against hope
for the best. But the experiences they have received
from the dealings of the Council with the Factories Act
have prepared them for the worst. They have come to a
crisis in which they must either carry forward the
mission they have deliberately undertaken or sink into
deserved contumely and ridicule. . . . The Irvine Go-
vernment has shown a spirit as firm as that of its pre-
decessor was flabhy. We might well wish to see the
mandate of the State peaceably vindicated in its policy ;
but if that be found impossible, in consequence of the
contempt of the Legislative Council, we may hope for a
resoluteness of procedure worthy of the trust of confiding
people.
The Age has published dozens of such articles, in
which it declared that the Upper House was im-
movable, but that Somebody should do Something
to move it. It has never explained what is the
Something that should be done, probably because
it has no settled idea of its own on the subject,
but it has been very wrath against the Somebody
for not doing it. The Bulletin, which reckons
that the kind of reform agitation that consists
in demanding that Somebody not clearly specified
shall do Something not specified at all is a mere
policy of bagging the wind, again offers its
theory:—
That the Victorian House of Landlords should be
abolished and Victoria run with one State House of
Parliament, as most of the Canadian States are run.
That, as one alternative, the Bill which provides for the
abolition should also set forth that the 48 M.’sL.O. shall,
on their abolition, become entitled to a life pension of
£lOOO a year each in recognition of their valuable services
—the latter clause being sarcasm. That proviso would
probably make the 48 Landlords pass the Bill and retire
amid general public contempt. It would cost Victoria, at
most £48,000 a year, and the expense would diminish
rapidly. With the House of Landlords out of the way an
amended Land Tax Act which would bring in another
£lOO,OOO a year would become possible.
That, as another though less promising alternative, the
Assembly, when the Council rejects the Bill, should go on
strike, refuse to pass any estimates, and let the police,
the magistracy, the public service and everything else
disband, and let the strike continue till the House of
Landlords consents to its own abolition.
Will the allegedly democratic Age— the paper
which is supposed to run Victoria —call on the
Ministry to try either of these devices, or find a
workable device of its own ? The bald statement
that Somebody should do Something is weari-
some.
Relief for the Distressed Farmers.
Premier See’s conference, which was to devise
means of relieving the distressed N.S.W. farmer,
duly met, and devised and departed. It met
without much hope, and it separated without
much result. It conceived five ideas for helping
the distressed settlers in a small way out of the
Treasury ; four ideas for helping them in.a larger
way out of borrowed money ; and two ideas for
passing the responsibility on to someone else —11
ideas in all. They may be enumerated thus :
(1) That relief be granted in authenticated instances of
destitution.
(2) That public works be built in the drought areas, the
bill, of course, to be paid for out of loans.
(3) That a large number of public.works—bill, pre-
sumably, payable out of loans —be put in hand. (This is
a curious duplication of No. 2.)
(4) That the Advances to Settlers department make
advances up to £l5OO.
(5) That seed-wheat be lent to farmers.
(6) That rents and land instalments be suspended
where required. , , ,
(7 and 8) That the Australian Government be requested
by the N.S.W. Parliament to suspend the food and fodder
duties for 12 months.
(9) That reduced railway rates be charged on fodder
and starving stock.
(10) Ditto on wheat and flour.
(11) That water conservation works—paid for out of
loans—be pushed on vigorously. (This is, to some
extent, Nos. 2 and 3 over again.)
Some of these ideas are probably about the best
in the way of temporary relief that the case
admits of. Some are an evidence of the incurable-
ness of the borrowing habit. Two of them, how-
ever—those dealing with the suspension of the
food and fodder duties—show a great deal
more evidence of a mean, grudging desire
to create difficulties for the . Australian
Government than to afford relief to the
distressed settlers. The State Parliament
should help itself in this matter—it would help
itself if it had any real desire for the alleged
assistance indicated, unless, its. helpless attitude
merely arises out of a constitutional objection to
do anything that can, by any possibility, be left to
someone else to do. If the N.S.W. Parliament;
decided to import sufficient grain to supply its own
State, or to pay the duty on private importations
for the use of its own State, it would thereby get
all tho relief that it could get by a general
suspension of the duties; it would pay the
duties and get the money back again, which is
just the same as not paying them at all.
And the State Parliament is in session and
able to act. The Australian Parliament
is not in session, and it can’t be gathered
together from the far ends of the continent
at short notice. Therefore the conference really
demands, and the N.S.W. Assembly demands,
that the Australian Government should put
itself into a most dangerous hole by illegally
suspending the food and fodder duties on
its own authority. Also that it should suspend
them in six States on the demand of one State
only—a high-handed proceeding which might
gratify some parochial politicians by enabling
N.S.W. to figure as the State which runs the
whole Commonwealth, but which would assuredly
lead to a lot of trouble. If this proposal did not
arise out of sheer malice, then it was a most ex-
traordinary case of weariness and shirk and
avoidance of personal responsibility.
The alleged strong and spineful Irvine Govern-
ment, which was to carry everything before it like
Bent’s stomach, met with less than the average
amount of luck over the Factories Bill, after all.
The House of Landlords having knocked most of
the essentials out of the temporary renewal
scheme, members of the two Houses met in con-
ference. Premier Irvine and Treasurer Shiels
represented the Government with the Spine, and
put their feet down manfully. Then they
gathered their feet up again. The Bill, as passed
by the Assembly, provided that the new Act
should hold good till December, 1904. The House
of Landlords altered the date to September, and
the conference of the two Houses decided on Octo-
ber. The difference was thus split, but it wasn’t
split in the middle, and the Assembly got the small
end. . The Assembly’s proposal was that all past
decisii ns of the Wages Boards should hold good
while the temporary Act lasted, but that the
Wages Boards should do nothing further till the
whole matter was reconsidered at the end of next
year. The Council proposed to cancel a whole
lot of Boards and decisions and to remove a
number of trades from the control of the anti-
sweating law. Here again there was a compro-
mise. The decisions which the Council objects to
are cancelled, but if the boards care to re-enact
them by seven votes against four (where the board
consists of eleven members), or by four votes
against three (where the board number six mem-
bers), and providing in the latter case that two of
the four are employers, these decisions shall again
have the force of law. This puts the power wholly
in the hands of the employers, so the Council
scores again. When it depends wholly on the
employers’ delegates whether any Wages Board
decision shall be cancelled, it is just as much a
victory as if the employers’ delegates in the House
of Landlords cancelled it at once. Possibly any
ordinary man would have managed no better than
Ikvine did—but then Irvine was to prove quite
unlike any ordinary man. When that alleged
tremendous and overwhelming person, who was
going to allow no interference with the Factories
Act, comes back to the Assembly with a tatter of
the Bill trailing behind him, and says he has
rescued this much from the dog with great diffi-
culty, he gets down to the level of the common
herd.

Brisbane Courier, in answer to Drake, who had
been demonstrating to Brisbane that a huge
amount of white labor had been engaged last
season in doing plantation work hitherto deemed
impossible to white men :
It is true a rebate of £18,918 has been paid, but it is
quite wrong- to assert that the cane was grown by white
labor. The proviso of the Commonwealth Qovt. was that
the rebate should be payable on sugar made from cane
worked by white labor since Ist March last. It was im-
material "whether white or black labor had been em-
ployed on it up to that time.
The unfortunate Courier in this simple paragraph
puts the whole case clean away. It has always
been conceded by the planters themselves that
white men could do all the necessary plantation
work—except the trashing and cutting or har-
vesting. But this is precisely the work the white
gangs have been engaged upon since March last—
and engaged upon (as witness amount of rebate
paid) with conspicuous success. The Courier
goes on further to deprecate this success by
alleging that the sugar crop was only small and
there was an exceptionally large supply of surplus
labor. But this is apart from the question. If
the work be impossible to white men (as alleged)
then the large supply of surplus labor isn’t going
to perform any miracles. The fact is—the
Australian Govt., by its white labor policy, has
just successfully demonstrated how many lies have
been told about this sugar industry, and some of
the liars don’t like it.

Maokinnon, of Prahran, is the only member of
the Victorian Assembly who has had the courage
to ask the straight question whether the Mallee
country is worth saving. It is his opinion that
instead of spending a million, as the Iceberg
Reform Government proposes to do, in taking a
miserable little stream of water to the mallee
settlers, Victoria should spend three millions in
transplanting them to a portion of the State
where there is a sufficient rain-fall. It cannot be
denied that if the Mallee possessed the water-
supply proposed by the Irvine Government at
the present time it would not prevent the disaster
of the drought. Without grass, that can not be ob-
tained save through a rainfall, the Mallee farmers
would be as helpless as they are now. A com-
petent man who has just completed a trip through
the drought area asserts that if the Government
wants to save the Mallee settlers a sum of £200,000
of public money must be loaned to them on the
security of land already loaded up to its full
mortgage values. Then the recent dust-storms in
the Mallee have raised another serious question.
After the storms had passed it was found that
two-thirds of the tanks and channels had been
filled up with shifting sand. The channel to take
a dribble of water to the Mallee from the Goulburn
river will cost £500,000 to construct. What will
it cost to clean out when it is filled with sand,
perhaps a year after its completion ?
N.S.W. Government docks don’t pay. Returns
regularly indicate that, and the reason why they
do not is often a harassing mystery. Calico
Jimmy, from his place in Parliament, always puts
it down to the fact that the Government has in-
terfered with private enterprise. Here is a little
fresh light on the subject. The Public Works
Committee was examining Mr. Keele, Principal
Engineer, Harbor and Rivers Branch, about a
proposed graving dock at Newcastle :
Mr. M'Gowen : What I want to know is, have the
Imperial authorities in New South Wales a right to go
into Sutherland Dock, and only to pay you just the actual
cost of wages?—Yes.
And practically 75 per cent, of the vessels docked belong
to the Imperial authorities?—Yes.
And our own vessels have to give way ? —Yes.
Have any cases occurred where the Government has
been put to extra expense through being compelled to
shift ?—Well, not lately, but cases have occurred.
And does this kind of thing apply to the Fitzroy Dock ?
—Yes, to either.
Is it any wonder, now, why these Government docks
don’t pay ?—Not at that rate.
The Imperial authorities, then, have the right to go into
these docks, turn out your own State boots, and do not
allow you a penny for wear and tear?—Yes ; that is what
has been done.
Will Calico Jimmy paste that evidence in his hat?
And will the Government forthwith revise those
unbusinesslike and absolutely crooked arrange-
ments with the Imperial authorities ?
»
The attitude assumed by Victorian Premier
Shiels in replying to a deputation of printers,
who complained about prison competition, seems
hard and unsympathetic, but there is no possible
doubt that it is the only logical attitude. It is
impossible to keep prisoners idle, unless they are
to go mad in solitary confinement, or else hang
about in droves in the gaol-yard, and learn to be
confirmed loafers, and spend their time planning
new crimes, and come out ten times worse than
they went in. To employ them on absolutely use-
less labor is demoralising, and teaches them
nothing in the way of earning an honest liveli-
hood—also, it prevents them earning anything
towards their own maintenance. To set aside
one or two trades as exclusively prison
trades would mean that the means of live-
lihood the prisoner learned in gaol would
be of no use to him when he came out.
If prisoners are employed at any occupation that
can possibly help them into the paths of honest
industry when they are set free, they must com-
pete with outside labor in some way. The
printers recognised this, and suggested that instead
of prisoners competing against them they should
take to some other line of business and compete
against somebody else —which is strictly human
nature. But, after all, the goods made for outside
use in all the prisons in Australia are probably
not worth £lOO,OOO a year, while imported goods,
which are equally detrimental to local industry,
represent £42,000,000.

These few remarks are all that this paper
feels equal to making about the Sydney Royal
show this week. That commendable but, in some
respects, rather ridiculous play is still on deck,
and the Constable’s armor shines like a new tin
billy, but the days of its glare are numbered. It
will cease to clatter on Friday, after a season of
two weeks only. Sydney doesn’t really want to
see any show for more than two weeks, unless it
is musical comic idiocy or melodrama of the
Anderson type —“ The Most Horrible Female in
Greece,” or some such production. “If I Were
King ” has done good business during its short
season, but, it not being a melo-shriek with a
locomotive and a horse-race and a dog-fight, the
management wisely refrains from pushing the
luck too far.
* * * *
Melba gives the first of her three Sydney
operatic performances at the Royal on Saturday—
to consist of two acts of “Faust” and one of
“Rigoletto.” On Wednesday (10th) she gives
three acts of “ La Traviata,” and the next Satur-
day (13th) two acts of “Traviata” and two of
“Faust.” A good many people—writer among
them—would prefer a whole opera to scraps. The
same two acts from “Faust” will be given on both
Saturdays. On the 10th there are to be given the
first, second and last acts of “Traviata,” and on
the 13th the first and second acts of “ Traviata,” so
in three nights the Sydney public is only to see
Melba in less than three operas—about 2J, in
fact. This is going byJast Monday’s advt. Pre-
vious Saturday’s advt. said that the programme
for the 10th was to be three acts of “ Traviata ”
and the mad scene from “Lucia”; and that the
one for the 13th was to be one act from “ Tra-
viata,” one from “Faust,” and the mad scene
from “Lucia.” What future advts. may say the
writer doesn’t know but one man who wanted to
see “Lucia,” and who bought tickets on the
strength of it, is already calling the management
most violent names. Monday’s Telegraph advt.,
by the way, was slovenly enough to tell Sydney
that “ the Melbourne public will doubtless feel
grateful,” &c. Nothing makes Sydney madder
than to be called “the Melbourne public.” t
* * * *
At time of writing there were mfire or
less credible reports of a re-opening of Sydney
Palace with a Sardou play, and a strong cast.
The play (if it is played) deals with Divorce, and
should be a typical Christmas attraction.
* * * *
“ The White Heather,” revived at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday night, has lost none of its
brightness in the course of time, and its unusual
coherence strikes one with quite a shock. It is
about the only play that deals successfully with a
lost marriage certificate. The document is Scotch
(old Scotch), and can, therefore, be reasonably the
only one of its kind; so when Baker repu-
diates his wife (Harrie Ireland) at a shooting
party, it is a sensible bit of villainy, seeing
that the record of her respectability is shut
up in an iron box in the foundered White
Heather, and that one witness is dead and the
other lost. So the earls and duchesses who have
just finished taking lunch out of doors with their
feet on the tablecloth, repudiate Harrie Ireland for
her alleged immoral conduct, and in the next act
attend a rehearsal where a noble damsel appears
as Lady Godiva, while Bland Holt shows Mrs.
Bland how to hover round her as the spirit of
purity. The play then negotiates successfully
some excellent scenery, including the Great
Ocean Scene and never behaves absurdly
till the last act, when Harrie Ireland’s good
lover brings the missing document into a
fancy-dress ball and forgets to mention the casual
circumstance that her husband is drowned.
There are so many titled men in the drama that
the loss of one or more in the sea is evidently a
matter of no consequence. The story concludes
abruptly with the good lover telling the repu-
diated lady that hope shines ahead (or something
like that) as a graceful way of breaking it to her
that she is a, widow. The dim, weird scene where
the two divers grapple for the proofs of the
heroine’s respectability goes without a hitch. It
needs only the addition of a tame shark or a
length of sea-serpent to make the realism perfect.
* * * *
The acting in “The White Heather”
is up to Bland Holt’s usual high level, ex-
cept that the Scotch accent of some of the
characters is a little variable and apt to go
off in moments of excitement. Miss Harrie
Ireland makes the most of her part as the lady
who didn’t look after her marriage certificate, and
Arthur Styan produces some very good results as
her good lover. Bland Holt and Mrs. Bland are
in their element, and Baker, as a cold, aristocratic
villain, is wholly satisfactory and much better
than he often is as a hero nowadays. Albert
Norman’s death-scene deserves a special mention.
* m * *
“A Trip to Chicago,” revived at Sydney
Criterion, is that desolate old friend, the mother-
in-law joke, supported by the usual personages
who are of so little importance when J. F.
Sheridan is present. There is, as usual, a Con-
siderable amount of dialogue without much
sinking in act 1, and a good deal of variety
business without much else in act 2. The plot
consists mostly in Mrs. Parker’s mother, who is
vulgar, meeting Mr. Parker’s mother, who is
refined, and in nothing particular happening
afterwards, except that Johnny remembers his
Irish ancestors of some hundreds of years ago, and
shows a considerable amount of ankle in con-
sequence. The wild chase of Johnny’s pink-
slippered _ feet after the troublesome son-
in-law is interesting in a rather unin-
teresting way. The Romeo and Juliet duet,
in which John is again himself (another self) as
the type of bashful damsel that is met with on the
burlesque stage, is a better item. Leon Roche is
to be commended for a really good sketch of a
wooden-legged major, and Thomas Curran and
Heba Barlow are appreciated for their duet, “ I’ve
Noticed It.” Miss Celia Mavis takes the leading
part, and the other characters make the usual
hazy impression, so that after they are gone it is
difficult to remember which was which, and which
wasn’t.
* * *
Continental Carnivals have been started
as a private enterprise at Sydney Cricket Ground,
but at the first, which occurred last Monday, the
principal item of the Continental continental is
absent —that is the beer garden. Refreshments
were obtainable at the usual place, the basement
under the grand stand. But you couldn’t
sit at a little table with the girl of your
heart near you, a foaming tankard in front
of you, and waiters running round it to stir up the
froth on it; all under the stars. Band music and
fireworks there were, and a cinematograph
display of a prize fight, which, curiously
enough, delighted the women—perhaps because
the terriflio blows hit a phantom and there
was no gore in sight. If the committee could
see its way to introduce the steeplechase, one of
the strongest attractions of Coney Island and
other American continentals, they would do a
wise and profitable thing. Horses on this steeple-
chase track are wooden, but that only adds to the
gaiety of the carnival. The programme of song-
and-dance and trapeze performance was witnessed
by a great and rather silent audience.
* * * *
Sydney Tivoli gives a cheery show just
now on revivals. The Nawns, the Schenks and
the Harbecks, and those boxing comedians, the
Harvey Boys, make up the chief part of the
programme. Successful music-hall people nowa-
days are always “The Something” in the plural
number.
* * * *
Geoi’ge Musgrove’s suspension of his ordi-
nary Melbourne press-list* during the Melba
season has revived the old, old question as to why
theatrical managers don’t deal with their “dead-
head” grievance in a reasonable, precautionary
way. Under existing circumstances the press-list
is an imposition which managers suffer in silence,
except on the rare occasions when they kick
against it. Musgrove’s kick, in the Melba case,
was honestly contemptuous, whereas he might
have made it mildly apologetic. Every manager
has cause for being embittered against the crowd
of (to him) valueless representatives of newspaper
privilege whose names are “on the door” at his
theatre, but no manager attempts to establish a
system whereby he could ensure fair treatment
from the press on the cheapest possible
terms as regards free admissions. The present
system is chiefly abused by the very men
and women to whom the “courtesy due
to the press ” is not due at all. A
manager can get only one notice of a first-
night performance from one newspaper, large or
small, and that one notice is desirable to him,
otherwise he wouldn’t recognise the existence of
the paper that publishes it. But on a crowded
first night, when every seat in the d.c. is worth
its price, there will be as many as ten or a dozen
deadheads representing, say, one daily-and-weekly
newspaper office’s free-list, and each of these
brings a lady companion, and they all want seats.
The small weeklies are proportionately almost as
bad as the tyrannous dailies. They put six or
more names “on the door,” and the owner of the
name that represents the forthcoming notice in the
paper—the only member of the little staff who is
there on business—is probably the only one who
shows consideration for the management by not de-
manding seats for self and lady. That s how
the imposition goes in an ordinary way. Managers
truckle to the press by sanctioning a ridiculously
lengthy free-list. They want all the notice-
writers and puff paragraphists they can get
to help them. Nevertheless they despise the
press because its genuine theatrical representa-
tives are only about 10 per cent, of the
dead-heads for whom that mighty power is
responsible. Every self-respecting journalist who
knows the facts of the theatrical free-list, sympa-
thises with the managers, and yet blames them
for not making the list a free, fair, and honorable
thing instead of a cause of constant grievance on
the one side and occasional grievance on the
other. Mr. Musgrove showed his business
opinion of free-list pressmen as a class
by advertising the fact that the list
was suspended for the series of Melba opera
performances. Consequently, some of the little
weeklies, with just as much claim to a little con-
sideration as the bigger papers, were not repres-
ented at all. Musgrove didn’t send them a
ticket for one bona-fide reporter, He didn’t send
them a tioket for any of the Melba concerts.
Practically he told those little journals that he
thought he could afford to treat them with con-
tempt in the Melba case, and if they wanted to
advertise these special performances by publish-
ing notices of them they (the “ suspended ” news-
papers) must pay for the privilege. But if a paper
is not worth a free admission, for one person at
least, at all times, why is it ever granted the right
of six free admissions? And by what rule of
reasoning does a manager arrive at a policy of
permitting a whole host of deadheads—3o to 40,
sometimes more—to be imposed upon him by one
newspaper office ?
** m *
“A Country Mouse” will probably be
Musgrove’s Xmas show at Melb. Princess’s. A
muddle-headed public may need instruction on
the point that “A Country Mouse” is a different
play from “Mice and Men.”
** * *
Melba, in the youthful parts of Gilda and
Marguerite, was a bright revelation to the Mel-
bourne that had only seen her as an impressive
platform figure. Melba in “La Traviata” was
astounding. The first, second and fourth acts of
Verdi’s opera require the prima donna to get
inside the emotional character of Camille, or at
least to seem to be inside it. Canaille is at
her sentimental best in those three acts. She
loves; she sacrifices her love at the instiga-
tion of an old gentleman in a grotesque garb
of respectability ; and she dies like a gentle,
gilt-edged sinner. Melba’s artistic perform-
ance was astounding, not so much on its
honest merits as because it couldn’t be reconciled
with the old Australian belief in her alleged cold-
ness and amateurishness. This country had swal-
lowed a hundred and one disparaging remarks
upon Melba’s dramatic powers. It remained for
the great singer (nobody ever found any flies on
her musical celebrity) to come along and show her
native land that her fortune wasn’t all in her
voice and her “ business ability.” We have heard
too much about Melba’s business ability from
people who seem to forget that she is an idol of
the Public in Europe, the Public that only wor-
ships the artist for her art. Melba, as Camille in
the opera, is, firstly, a mighty fine woman;
secondly, a graceful, tearful, absolutely natural
impersonator of a warm-hearted character,
and all the time a thrilling singer. Not
loud and thrilling. Just Melba, suiting the
expression of her voice to the scene, and looking
uncommonly charming as she pours out the
melody. Last week’s one performance included
the Mad Scene from “ Lucia.” Melba sang the
larger half of the “Lucia ” item at one or other of
her concerts. The whole of the scene, as she sings
it in the traditional nightgown, with angel sleeves
and Ophelian smiles and gestures, is a brilliant
effect, but it seems to be pretty much at the
mercy of the flautist who plays the obligato. On
the Wednesday night flautist Griffith’s accom-
paniment was a bit “ off.”
** * *
Musgrove’s dramatic co. is running an
eccentric course. It started its “Moths” at a
Saturday matinee, and terminated the unhappy
flutter the next Saturday night; another romantic
drama, “A Royal Rival,” being appointed to
take place at the Princess’s for the first Australian
time on Monday of this week. Dramas which
introduce themselves to Melbourne on a Monday
can only appeal to the interest of The Bulletin’s
next issue by telegraph-wire, and this particular
drama did not appeal. It is irreligious for a show
to get born on a Monday, except at Easter.
m# * *
“The Mikado ” is going for a second week
at Her Majesty’s, but “Paul Jones” is waiting to
seize the floor. After a four years’ absence (the
advt. said six years), “ Mikado ” was rather more
new than usual. Also, it is at least a brighter
show now than last time, when Lauri played the
boss potentate, and other people, bar Bathurst,
were different from the present co. Lauri, asKo-Ko
instead of the reigning Jap, imparts a new flavor
to the tit-willow ballad, and Florence Young’s
Katisha is a weird, bloodthirsty spinster, with a
tragic leer and a tall top-note. On Saturday next
(6th) Miss Young will change the character of her
smile, and explain what happened to Jones, the
marine apostle.
** * *
At Fitzgerald’s Circus the ruminative cow,
the genial elephant, and a large assortment of
men, horses, and things, continue to chase the
fugitive venison through a wet expanse of water
every evening and two matinees a week. A stag
hunt which embraces an elephant is an aquatio
novelty, anyhow, but it might be improved by the
introduction of Pagel. The Strongest of all
Strong Men should tie his raging lion to a big
dumb-bell, drop it into the 22ft. of water, and
pull it out by the tail. Or he might swim the full
length of the tank with the pony seated on his
back. Pagel is a man of infinite possibility, and
his legs loom larger than ever.
On Saturday night, the Royal patrons
recognised among other old friends in the cast of
“A London Arab” a foreign adventuress, who
marries a dotard baronet (Sterling Whyte) for his
money, and billets on the premises as “brother”
and secretary, her irregular French husband
(H. 0. Willard), also another equally wicked
Mossoo, one Ferdinand (J. L. Lawrence). Un-
fortunately, as it turns out, the much-married
Madame’s first and only lawful husband, Jasper
Hardacre (why not Harddrinker ?), as imper-
sonated by George P. Carey, also chances to be
staying at Yilliers Hall in the private apartments
and wearing the clothes of his old college chum,
Alfred Yilliers (R. Inman), the bart.’s only son,
who is curing Jasper of drunkenness and vaga-
bondage. Three husbands in one house are sure
to fall out. Jasper recognises his wicked Toinette
in the present Lady Villiers. He attempts to
kill her, but his bullet finds a billet instead
in one of the other husbands, the senile
Bart., who, seeing his murderer in sonnie’s
clothes, promptly accuses the luckless Alfred
of parricide, and dies off before he can be
contradicted. With one husband in the grave
and another —viz., .Jasper—immured in a
London den, allowed no beverage but brandy,
Toinette and Mossoo (Willard) look forward
to high jinks. But though Alfred (Inman),
the accused heir, has fled to Johannesburg, his
wife and child are still in England, which means
that before the unrighteous foreigners can enjoy
their ill-gotten gains, abduction, infanticide, and
numerous other crimes must be committed. They
commit them, of course, or try to, but in the end
their sin finds them out, and police, handcuffs, and
gallows embitter their “ closing march, their last
march to the grave.” Bert Bailey as an Irish
policeman, “a proper broth of a bhoy of aleadhin’
Oirish family —a family'that’s always led pigs and
oows and things about "—does the very best possible
in the part. Gladys Hughes, as the London Arab,
also makes a brave attempt to impersonate an
urchin with the heart of an angel, the spirit of a
hero, the appearance of a gutter-snipe, and the
appearance of a stage costermonger. As the
child of the diabolical Toinette and the broken-
down swell Jasper, the youngster must have “ cast
back ” to some extra-special ancestor for his many
excellences. Temple Harrison, as a comic page,
Maud Gwynne, as the persecuted hero’s tearful
wife, and Pattie Hughes, as the child of last-named,
all deserve praise. Rural England, London
Bridge and the African veldt all have more than
justice done them in really beautiful scene-
settings.
** * *
“ Little Lord Fauntleroy,” smartly-staged
at Melbourne Bijou, seems well worth a revival
for the first time in 13 years. The tone of Mrs.
Burnett’s story is rather painfully feminine as
regards “ birth ” and “ station,” yet the authoress
illustrates her reverence for the British Peerage
with a truthful picture of a deplorable old earl.
Although the comedy preaches the greatness of
family titles, pedigrees, and ancestral vaults, it
permits one person of middle-class extraction to
be treated, in the last act, as a suitable widow
of a real earl’s youngest son, and a quite
presentable mother of the next heir' to the
title and estates. Also Hobbs, the grocer, and
Dick, the shoeblack, figure as deserving.specimens
of plebeian ignorance. But this concession to the
lower orders strikes one as being due to the fact
that Hobbs and Dick are ardent admirers of His
Little Lordship. If they didn’t love society they
would not belong to the deserving class. The
only character in the play who treats society with
scorn is Minna, and she turns out to be a bigamut
of the worst description.
* » * *
“Little Lord Fauntleroy” lias to stand,
or fall, by the performance of Sydney Halliday
in the name part. It stands. The Fauntle-
roy whom Melbourne remembers at the Prin-
cess’s of yore was a girl, or rather, two girls—
Olive Berkley and Grade Hopkins. They played
the part alternately. One was less tall and round
than the other, but neither of them was a boy that
any observant mother could possibly accept as
genuine. Now, Syd. Halliday is a boy after every
woman’s own heart. He wears no ringlets, and he
puts on no frill, and is a little brick of a lad, who
probably beats the record as a 9-year-old actor.
All things considered, little Syd.’s bright, inter-
esting performance on Saturday night was a won-
derful feat. Hawtrey and co. give the star
kiddie some good enough support, though they
might be considerably better in spots. Oily
Deering is a cheerful, extravagant Hobbs,
and Hawtrey will, no doubt, develop into
a. first-class noble grandfather, in spite of
his nasal accent. Gregan MacMahon and Heggie
play a couple of character parts capably,
and Miss Nicnolls, as “ Dearest,” is very satisfac-
tory in the first act. In the last act she is other-
wise. Minna (Miss Hilda Wright) is a restless,
improbable sort of adventuress, and the footman
at Dorincourt Castle ought to be discharged
without a month’s wages,
** * m
Maggie Moore has turned up at Adelaide
Royal after long absence, asa “Widowfrom Japan.”
She also plays the same old “Chinese Question,”
and is much the same plump Sam Lee Loo as of
old. The management has wisely inaugurated
summer prices—3, 2 and 1. At the Tivoli,
Howard Vernon (who ran up against a heat-wave
at starting) has boldly got down to 2,1, and |.
‘ 1 The Parisians ” proved to be a version of Robert
Maeaire’s adventures, helped out with miscel-
laneous music. Its topical allusions are of the
Hopetoun and Federation and Pretoria era; and
Victor Prince, as principal support, introduces
mother-in-law and whisky ballads into Parisian
high-life as freely as he did into the Katskill
Mountains.
In the old black-lettered pages of the dead and
buried ages you may chance to read a story of
how France lay desolate ;
How King Louis’ scared commanders saw the
motley hordes of Flanders like crows and"
vultures gathered at their city’s crumbling
gate ;
How the city’s heart was sinking, and the aged
king was drinking with a hangman in a house
where malefactors used to dine ;
He heard his country calling, and he heard his
kingdom falling, and he saw the flames of
ruin reflected in his wine.
In disguise he sat there thinking—this aged king,
and drinking, while outside the hordes of
Flanders like swarms of demons raved ;
And his soul was dark with curses, but just then
some martial verses were recited by a poet,
who said that France might yet bo saved.
And King Louis dropped his beaker, and King
Louis spilled his liquor, and he stood up in
that tavern (so runs the old romance) ;
His act was rather sudden, and his motives some-
what hidden, but that poet (who wasn’t sober)
he made Constable of France.
Then the raging hosts of Flanders, and their
banners and commanders, that poet (when he
got sober) blew to all the winds of earth ;
And the king, no longer feeling that the throne
of France was reeling, remarked unto the
hangman that this was a bard of worth.
The tale is fair in seeming, and it set this writer
dreaming of something that niight happen in
these dull, prosaic days—
And he dreamt the Boers had risen, and Roberts
was in prison, and Kitchener was dead, and
all the land was in a blaze.
The empire fast was falling, and for counsel all
were calling, and some said we should fight,
and some, we should negotiate ;
From the Shetlands down to Surrey there was
chaos, rush and hurry; there was frenzy on
the Mile-End Road and wrath in Billingsgate.
All were crazy, saint and sinner ; and the King
had had no dinner, for the cook had gone
demented and the kitchen fire was out,
And King Edward in disguise was eating mutton
pies in a restaurant when, casually, a rumor
went about,
That in a room adjoining, Victor Daley then was
dining, and while he dined was writing out a
poem on a slate,
Then the king burst in disordered, and there and
then he ordered him to bring along his poem,
and they yet might save the State.
But a ghostly king sat thinking when they were
gone, and drinking; and a ghostly hangman
faced him where the bard had sat that night,
And their souls were full of curses as they pondered
o’er the verses, for they were better far than
those that Villon used to write.
But through the muddy highways, and by short
cuts through the bye-wavs, the poet and the
monarch ran like furies to the camp,
Where the army, few in numbers, lay uneasy in
its slumbers, and they read the poem to it by
a dim and flaring lamp.
And it stated with a roar, if it had heard these
lines before, it would have knocked the
Dutchmen endways when first they chanced
to meet.
Then, refreshed, it took its gun, at the rising of
the sun, and trod the base invader in the dust
beneath its feet.

The Gardner Electric Rock Drill which has
been lately introduced into Australia is being very
largely adopted in the mines of the United States, and is
giving great satisfaction. This electric drill is filling a
long felt want, now that electricity is being so largely
adopted for all mining purposes. The Gardner Electric
Drill is very simple and easily handled, and drills as fast
as the air drill, and costs much less to instal in a mine,,
especially in out of the way places. It requires only one
fifth of the power required to operate an air drill doing,
the same work, so the saving in fuel soon pays its
cost. The Gardner Drill at the North Gippsland
Proprietary mine, Victoria, has been in use for eight
months, and has done all the drilling for a tunnel 1500 feet
in very hard country, and the Manager speaks highly in
praise of it. The Tarcoola Blocks Company, South
Australia, has ordered a complete equipment for 6electric
drills and lighting plant, for the mine to be worked by
an oil engine of 16 horse power. The Sole Australian
Agents, the Austral Otis Engineering Co. South
Melbourne, will supply catalogues and estimates on
request.*

Wm. Logan, chief vendor of the Ida H., Laver-
ton (W.A.), to “Boulder King” Waddington, has just
left for London, to beard the lion in his den. His
grievance is that his scrip is unsaleable, as the directors,
for obvious reasons, omitted to apply to the Stock
Exchange Committee for a special settlement for the
vendors’ scrip, without which application no dealings on
’Change are sanctioned or recognised. Same game was
played in the case of the Windsor Consols, with the
result that vendor, in ignorance of this custom, found
himself and family stranded in London.

When the last list was published in this column (14th
June, 1902) it showed three non-dividend-paying banks ;
since then, the Bank of New Zealand has got back on to
the dividend list and reduced the blanks to two. As
regards their relative positions, the two Maorilaud banks,
the Queensland National, Australasia, Westralian Bank,
Royal of Melbourne, E.S. and A., Commercial of Sydney,
City of Sydney, and London occupy the same position on
the list as they did six months ago. The Union, N.S.W.,
Commercial of Tasmania, Ballarat Banking Co., National
(Melbourne), Victoria, Royal of Queensland, and Aus-
tralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank stand higher on the
list than they did ; while the others are iower. Six
months ago the Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
was last, and the A.J.S. second last. Now the positions
are reversed.

• * • •
The Queensland Government is never behind-
hand at borrowing, however it may lag in other respects.
Treasurer Cribb proposes to float a £600,000 loan at 4 per
cent., repayable in 10 years. Of this, £431,939 is to cover
last year’s deficit, and the other £168,061 is on account
of the deficit which Philp and co. expect to have next
June. The Treasurer who borrows money on account of
the deficit he has is common enough, but the one who
borrows on account of the shortage he is going to have
next June, is a rare and “ forehanded ” person. Treasurer
Cribb ascribes his troubles largely to the Australian
Govt.’s diminished returns in the way of customs revenue,
but seeing that a leading feature of the Federal compact
was that inter-State customs were to be abolished and the
inter-State revenue lost, the Philp Government surely
foresaw something of the sort. The fact that it is only
now beginning to do a little in the way of imposing new
taxes to take the place of those abolished, with its full
concurrence, a year ago, is to its everlasting discredit.
• * • *
Last financial year Tasmania paid, in all forms
of taxation, about £2 15s. Bd. per inhabitant, of which
10s. went to make up the railway deficit, leaving £2 ss. Bd.
per inhabitant for general purposes, or about £1 or £1 ss.
per head less than the tax rate in England. After it had
paid interest on public debt, the whole island was run —
education vote, justice, prisons, police, public works,
Parliament, Governor, and all —for £361,000. It is the
most cheaply-governed State in Australasia, by many
miles. But the abolition of inter-State customs duties
leaves a deficit of £122,000, and the local press is almost
unanimous in its opinion that this amount should be
saved out of the £361,000, without damaging the efficiency
of the public service, in order to obviate new taxation.
Getting a quart into a pint jug is a fool to this.
* * * *
“ W.R.P.” (Queensland): Most investing shares
are down at present because the price of money has gone
up. The bad harvest, losses of stock, Government
deficits, and a dreadful fear that Bull is going to stop
lending have sent many reasonably good stocks down
enormously. And ordinary shares (which, of course, will
get no dividend if profits fall so that there is only enough
to give the preference shareholders their 5 per cent.) are
never likely to bevery high. Still you get 10 per cent.
Call 5 per cent, interest on your money. Apply the other
5 per cent, to write down the value of the share's in your
books till they stand at nil. After that everything you
get is clear profit.
• « f «
B. Water Supply, Ltd., has 16 years more in
which to get back its capital ; the whole conoern then
falls in to the State without compensation. Though the
supply is quite insufficient for the mines and town, even
the Owe’Sullivan is not inclined to spend a lot of money
on a brand-new scheme which may knock out what in a
few years’ time will be the Government’s own property.
As a compromise, it is therefore proposed that the
State should advance the £3OOO or so said to be necessary
to supplement the present supply by tapping another
creek which, like Stephens Creek itself, runs for an hour
or two perhaps twice in three years, the co. paying
interest until it gives up possession of the entire show.
Or, as an alternative, it is suggested that the co. put its
reserve into the supplementary works on the Govt, payuig
not on a valuation, but the original cost, when it telMs
them over.

The reserves consist of reserve fund, £18,500; dividend
equalisation account, £3500; forfeited capital, which
isn’t added to any fund but straggles by itself in the
balance-sheet, £2310; and profit balance, £2091. It
would certainly be much more business-like to consoli-
date these miscellaneous sums, and have a reserve-fund
of £26,000, and carry forward £4Ol. The Intercolonial
is one of the most prosperous of the Australian land com-
panies, and its earnings represent over 14 per cent, on
capital; but its way of investing everything it can lay its
hands on looks a trifle risky. The assets total £90,313, of
which £90,199 consists of property and mortgages, £lO5
is furniture, and £9 cash. The latter item seems to be
all that is held in the way of liquid assets against £26,018
of miscellaneous liabilities (bank overdraft, deposits, &c.).
The policy of keeping no money lying idle or invested in
low-interest-bearing liquid securities doubtless pays in
ordinary times. Whether it will pay all the time is an-
other question.
* • • *
N.S. Wales Government is finding, to its sur-
prise, that a great part of what it has done in the way of
cheap railway freights for “ starving stock ” and for
fodder has benefited nobody but speculators and scow-
bankers. The Railway Commissioners, for instance, have
noticed that “this has been looked upon as such a
matter-of-fact arrangement that a produce-agent has had
a rubber-stamp prepared and consignment-notes en-
dorsed, “to be used to keep starving stock alive.’’ In
another case “ a considerable quantity of maize was con-
signed under this description, whereas, as a matter of
fact, it was retailed in the ordinary way of business by a
storekeeper, and was not used for starving stock at all."

The North Queensland Insurance Co. (Syd-
ney) closed a very successful year on 30th September,

The North Queensland Insurance Co., after a long period
of comparative leanness, has had a series of remarkably
good years; out of its reserves of £45,564 no less than
£28,129 (considerably more than half) have been accumu-
lated in the last three years. The investments look
especially good, being mostly Government securities and
bank deposits. The income from investments last year
was £3400 —equivalent, in itself, to rather more than a
6 per cent, dividend. In addition to the reserves shown
above, an unusually large amount (£38,800) has been set
aside to meet known or possible claims, which is a further
good point in the co’s character. As an evidence of the
expansion of the business, these are the gross premiums
collected in various years :
1892 .. £47,828 I 1898 .. £107,712
1895 .. 71,695 | 1902 .. 158,007
This is something unusual in the way of expansion. At
the same time the profits, large as they have been during
the last three years, have not been so consistently large
over the last 11 years as those of some concerns which do
business in a more restricted area. The North Queens-
land Insurance Co. trades in Bombay, Calcutta, Colombo,
Madras, New York, Natal, San Francisco, Singapore,
China, Japan, Manila—in fact it spreads over a very large
section of the earth, and that kind of business is generally
unprofitable in the end. The North Queensland is the
most cheaply-managed insurance co. in N.S.W., and one
of the most cheaply-managed in Australia, which is the
whole secret of its success. If its expense rate had been
equal to the average rate of N.S.W. offices, instead of
adding £38,000 to its reserves in the last 10 years it would
have had to cut down its dividends heavily to avoid
showing a loss.
• * * *
Last year, the ordinary shareholders of Farmer
and Co. received 13J per cent, dividend, and those of
Robert Reid and Co. and D. and W. Murray and Co. 10
per cent. The importer-men make fine large profits, as a
rule, which profits mostly go to English shareholders,
and they employ comparatively little Australian labor,
and what they do employ is none too well paid. The
manufacturer employs, in proportion to his output, five
times as much labor, pays it better as a rule, seldom
screws more than 6 per cent, dividends out of it, and in
most cases spends his profits in Australia. Yet the im-
porter, with his 10 and 13J per cent, dividends, is never
tired of wailing about the manufacturer’s “ rapacity.”
« * * *
This (to Sept. 30) is not B. H. Block 10’s worst
half-year; but it is so close to it that you couldn’t tell
the difference without looking hard. Back in ’94 and ’95
the co. had some very bad times (that was before it got
its sulphide mill going); but since then there has been
none worse, and only one as bad. Its career since the
former turn in the tide came has been as follows :

Latterly the “ net profit ’’ shown has really been gross
profit and a bit over sometimes. Nothing has been
written off for wear and tear of mine plant since the end
of ’99, and nothing for the exceedingly heavy deprecia-
tion of the ore-dressing plant since the end of 1900.
Comparing the last two half-years the result for that
ended March was rather better than it looked; for that
ended September rather worse. The £5386 remained
after refunding £2459 overpaid on a zinc-tailings’ sale in
’9B; the £1284 is arrived at by adding to the working
profit (£107) interest on the co.’s reserves and £564
refunded by the Income Tax Commissioners. Practically,
on actual mining, the co. just made ends meet with silver
averaging 2s 0 3-16 d. and lead £ll ss. 9d. And, as the
present quotation for silver is Is. 10§d., and for lead
£lO 155., the co. must be losing money now; although
against this loss must be set several things—(l) it is
accumulating zinc products which, later on, may be of
value (they contain more than half of the silver, nearly
all the zinc, arid three-tenths of the lead); (2) it is spending
a fair amount in shaft-sinking and other developmental
work ; and (3) it is filling up worked-out places which, for
the mine’s safety, would have to be filled up, anyway.
About the best hope for Block 10’s future, apart
from the recovery of metal prices, lies in the fact that
it, so far, does not recover anything like half the metal
contents of the ore. It doesn’t recover any at all of the
zinc, if it can help it, and of the other metals the extrac-
tions for some periods past have been :

All the Broken Hill mines do this sort of thing ; Block 10

that it cost more than it was worth to get out the last
unit or two. And, then, he isn’t interested in showing
that the plant can do clean work ; on the contrary, he
has condemned it, and a new mill is coming. Certainly
there is plenty of room for better results —a plant that
lets 60 per cent, of the silver and 32 per cent of the lead
slide is several streets away from perfection.
* * • •
B.H. Block 14 has been “resting” for most of
the half-year (to Sept. 30), and its report is chiefly a
pretty picture of the fine things it is going to do when
the good times come again and steam is turned on.
“ Recent operations have shown that smelting can be
satisfactorily carried on with but one-third of the pro-
portion of carbonate ore formerly used, and alterations
can readily be made favoring such cheaper working con-
ditions and improved metal recovery as will soon repay
the cost.” Which seems to indicate that the direc-
tors are still bound to the fatuous policy of run-
ning smelting plant on the co.’s own account.
Only a phenomenal mine can do that economically,
and' Block 14 isn’t a phenomenal mine—in fact, it
isn’t even a third-rater. Though it confined itself prac-
tically to the best of its ores, picking out only those
most cheaply smelted without any preliminary treat-
ment, it last half-year dropped £3097. Its financial posi-
tion is about as follows Liabilities: Sundry creditors,
£24,125. Liquid assets : Stores (coal, coke, &c-)> £7lBB ;
ore, &c., £2258; sundry debtors, £465 ; cash, £4602 ;
total, £14,513. Deficiency, £9612. It is to clean the slate
and put a little by that the co. is now undergoing still
one more reconstruction by the issue of 100,000 pref.
shares at 6s. ; of this fresh capital £2557 is shown in this
balance-sheet, and is presumably included in the “ cash ”
mentioned above.

What are the Broken Hill mines going to show
at the deeper levels ? The lode has been tested now at
800 ft. in the South, at 1015 ft. in Block 10 (approximately
half way along the line), and at 1037 ft. in the Junction
North, at the other end. The South’s developments have
been remarkably satisfactory. Some of the stopes are
over 145 ft. wide, all solid good-grade ore, assaying about
18 per cent, lead and 9oz. silver, and, though hard, suit-
able for milling. At 1015 ft. Block 10 is now working at
five points ; and it is worth while setting out the average
of last half-year’s assays from each

In the Junction North, the lode, where tested at 1037 ft.
by crosscut was 25£ft. wide, for 21-Jft. assaying lOoz.
silver, 17T per cent, lead, and 6‘4 per cent. zinc. Along
this ore body a drive 50ft. long has proved the stuff uni-
form. It has, however, been found that much of the ore
at the deep levels in all the mines is extremely hard, in-
creasing the cost both of mining and of milling, and
largely counterbalancing the improvements which have
been made in the processes. Excluding, perhaps, Block
14 and the British, in which the deeper exploration has
not given average results, it is nevertheless pretty near
the mark to say that down to the deepest levels yet ex-
plored the sulphide bodies show no serious, if any,
deterioration from the average quality of the upper sul-
phide bodies mainly worked in hitherto. The 'Broken Hill
shareholder’s concern, then, is not so much with the
mines as with the metal markets and metallurgical
developments. Taking Block 10 again, on its present
diminished output a movement of Id. in silver and £1 in
lead means just over *6OOO in the half-year ; and in two
years there has been a movement of sd. in silver and
£6 10s. in lead! Though this has been rather sudden,
such movements have always characterised the lead
market. In 1798 the price averaged £l5 10s. ; in 1806,
£35 155.; in 1825, £25 55.; in 1830, £l2 2s. 6d. From ’53
to ’77 it kept steadier at from £24 to £lB 2s. 6d. In the
25 years since 1876 it has been below £lO (average) two
years, £lO to £ll, two ; £ll—£l2, three ; £l2—£l3, five;
£l3—£l4, five ; £l4—£ls, three ; £ls—£l6, one ; £l6
£l7, two; £l7—£lB, one; and £lB—£2o, one. As to
silver it is no use giving such figures, because the whole
position of the silver market has been altered by mone-
tary changes, and there doesn’t seem to be any particular
reason why in its downward career it should stop where
it is.
The recent slump in the tin market (nearly £4
in a week) is probably related in some way to the slump
in silver; they are most likely connected through that
elderly but highly-respectable bore, now happily living in
retirement, the Medium of Exchange. Most of the tin is
got in the Straits Settlements by yellow persons who use
white money ; and the boss yellow person who sells his
tin for gold and pays his men in silver, can afford to sell
his tin for less the more silver he can get for the gold
that he gets for his tin. This isn’t very lucid, but that is
the inevitable result of dragging the Medium of Exchange
into an argument.
• • • •
Though nominally there was no change in the
standard wage at Broken Hill the keen struggle for work
has brought down the average earnings of contract miners
in Block 10 from 10s. 6fd. at the end of last year to 9s. 3d.
in the half-year just finished.
* * * *
Mr. W. J. Barnett, accompanied by Mr. A. S.
Joseph, has been busily engaged for some weeks past in
making an examination of the Great Cobar and other
properties on behalf of the English syndicate to which
they are under offer. Mr. J. D. Kendall, a recognised
American copper mining authority, who has been selected
to report on the proposition, is now on his way to Sydney.
Apropos, big metallurgists get big fees. The man who
was originally asked to report on Great Cobar refused to
come for 7000 guineas—it wasn’t enough, he said (for the
work which, with the sea-travelling thrown in, would
have covered a period of, say, five mouths in all).
• * * *
The Great Australian Quicksilver Co. is making
extensive preparations to test its mine at Yugilbar,
Clarence River, N.S.W. A complete treatment-plant has
been purchased, and is now in course of erection. The
plant has been designed by Dr. Elliott, who is taking an
active interest in the management of the show. The
vendors have every faith in the enterprise, and have
shown this by agreeing to lock up their scrip for a long
period. It is thought that quicksilver will be produced
within three months. Arrangements have been made for
the purchase of the whole of the output on what are con-
sidered satisfactory terms.
Burragorang silver-lead field, near Picton,
N.S.W., is being vigorously worked despite the low
prices of metals, and if the several private parties who
are operating are meeting with anything like the success
of the one registered c.o. the field must be prosperous.
Yerranderie co., during the past half-year, has declared
four divB. of 6d. each and started a reserve fund with
£lOOO. The local lodes are comparatively small, but very
rich in fairly long shutes.
* • • *
The picturesque mining camp of Mt. M‘Donald
(N.S.W.) is now almost deserted, the Hongkong co’s.
having abandoned the field. Three years ago there were
hundreds of men employed, and, to outward seeming, it
was one of the most prosperous camps in the State.
Everyone had plenty “Ohinkies’ money” to spend, and
extravagance ran riot in all quarters in the belief that the
“ Chinkies’ ” purse and patience were like the wizard’s
bottle. Unfortunately it was not Chinese money that
was lavished at the outset, but that of British
speculators, who deserved better luck and treatment.
The three co’s which operated there must have lost fully
£lOO,OOO. Only one mine, Oliver’s freehold, made any
respectable showing. It crushed 22,498 tons for 12,0660 z.
gold, worth £45,000, or an average value of £2 per ton of
ore, yet no profit was returned, which indicates that there
must have been several screws loose.

The secretary of the Island Eureka S.M. Co.
(W.A.) describes as misleading a Bulletin paragraph of
of 22nd Nov., with reference to his co. “The battery,”
he says, “is still running full time, and has done so from
the start, with the exception that the original five-head
battery was stopped a short time ago for two days for the
purpose of attaching new cams. The lode is not flat, and
its westerly underlay carries it in the opposite direction
of the Golconda leases, which are situated on the Eastern
and Northern boundaries of the Eureka. There is nothing
to indicate exhaustion of, or falling off in the value of, the
ore reserves.”

SOCIETY
Writes “ Prooshan” :
Simply sickening is this maudlin sympathy for
murderess Sangal and for she-slayers generally. It’s easy
to get petitions signed by people who didn’t see a blood-
stain or hear the splash at the bottom of the well.
Moreover, “it’s a pity to hang a woman ” ; and, “Tisler
was hanged— surely that's enough?" “And then the
little child, you know!” Now, I believe that these
sympathisers, every one, would hang Selina Sangal high as
Haman had she murdered a relative —or even an
acquaintance—of theirs. But, as it’s the other fellow, it
doesn’t matter. It’s largely the impersonality that counts
in.these cases.
Which is all very well, so far as 2tgoes. But
the level-headed section of the community, though
it didn’t wish to have Mrs. Sangal hanged, hadn’t
the faintest “sympathy” with her. Mrs. Sangal
was reconi mended to mercy by the jury—perhaps
on the “silly-sentimental” ground that she is a
woman and was about to bear a child. But nomatter;
that recommendation deserved respect. If it
was not respected, you would have jurors—one
juror out of a panel of 12 has the power—refusing to
convict any woman of a capital offence. Had Mrs.
Sangal been hanged the next lot of juries would
not merely recommend their murderesses to mercy
—they would themselves dispense mercy, and that
would not be merciful to the community. It’s the
community which The Bulletin is worrying
about, not Mrs. Sangal and her like. If the law
takes too much in any one case it will get nothing
at all in some future cases, and it is certainty of
punishment, not severity, which is society’s first
desideratum.
V * • •
A lady, well-known in Sydney society,
describes in the local D.T. the agonies she endured
while awaiting at the local electoral-office the issue
of her voting-right:
We all had to line-up outside like suppliants for charity,
While two gentlemen in the front rooih sat in state and
solitude “ k deux,” and dispensed the tickets of citizen-
ship to all and sundry. But we were not allowed to tres-
pass in the sanctuary unbidden. We were ordered in one
by one, and sometimes two by two, like the animals in
the Ark. I waited my turn for about 20 minutes, but the
atmosphere of the room drove me to seek the outer air
and abandon my quest. Now, as our Premier is a cour-
teous and kindly gentleman, I am sure he will be willing
to provide decent accommodation to women desirous to
enrol themselves as electors. Why not have a separate
room for women, with a waiting-room attached, so that
we could sit down while waiting our turn to be attended
to.
Quite right. If Premier See is any kind of a
man, he will see that there are at that office several
waiting-rooms fully furnished with afternoon-tea,
puff-boxes, curling-tongs, hand-mirrors, hatpins,
and all the other etceteras.
» » * •
There is a strong similarity between the
case of the Kenniffs and the ’97 trial at
Aramac (0.Q.), of Barker, charged with the
murder of his mate, Ackroyd, at Greyrock, on the
Aramac-Clermont road. The pair camped to-
gether on their way to Clermont, and next morn-
ing a’roo shooter heard shots in the direction of
their camp, but did not investigate. Later on,
Barker was met travelling back, having with him
Ackroyd’s dog and horses, which he afterwards
sold. Ackroyd was never seen again, but, months
after, another ’roo shooter found in the ashes of a
fire, several charred pieces of human bones, a
portion of a skull with a tomahawk-mark in it,
and a purse-clasp, identified as having been Ack-
royd’s. As in the more recent case, the difficulties
were the absence of an actual body, and the bare
possibility that Ackroyd might still be alive.
Anyhow, the charge broke down, though Barker
{a notorious criminal), got a long sentence on
another charge. Throughout the trial the case
received little notice from the press (except
locally), which seems to show that big head-line 3
in the leading dailies go far towards securing for
a man the privilege of a drop-kick.
* • * •
The vagaries of people who win money in
“Tatt.’s” sweeps are many. The story of the
policeman who got through £20,000 in a year is
well known; but the money frequently goes to
more thrifty quarters. One big winner (on the
N.S.W. railways) took a half-day off work, and
after banking the money indulged in the excite-
ment of a trip to Manly. An elderly couple at
Bathurst promptly invested their win in house
property, while a prize of £lOOO spread among
five girls in a city tailoring factory resulted in as
many marriages a few weeks later. A Redfern
(N.S.W.) man who won £6OO invested it in an
accommodation-house and grounds by the Hawlces-
bury, and is now worth the £6OO many times over.
A winner in Newcastle district who drew a big
prize invested it soundly, and now lives on the
interest without doing a stroke of work; his life
is that of the usual coal-miner except that he
never goes to the pit.
* • * *
The editor of a Dalby (Q.) paper, desirous
of showing up the exorbitance of the local
butchers, bought a frozen carcase of mutton
recently, hung it up outside his office, carved it
with the assistance of one of his staff, and sold it,
allegedly at a profit, to the public at 2d. a lb.
less than the local butchers were charging. Dalby
rejoiced accordingly—but won’t that man get a
heap of butchers’ advertisements in future?

Maoriland begins to waken to the possi-
bilities of cheap electric power to be obtained
from its many and turbulent rivers. Christ-
church, and indeed all Canterbury, has long felt
the severe handicap on its industries en-
tailed by long distance from the coal-
fields, and it is now proposed to har-
ness the Waimakariri and light the
streets and run the factories by
electricity. The Railway De-
partment begins to see the
futility of spending thou-
sands and thousands in
dragging coal from the
uttermost ends of the
State when inexhaustible
rivers never more than
ten miles apart oan sup-
ply electric power at a
fraction of the cost.
Every important M.L.
town has a considerable
stream of water in close
proximity: Invercargill
has the Oreti; Dunedin,
‘ e Leith, with the Taieri not too far
v \ way ; Christchurch, the Waimaka-
► riri; Blenheim, the Wairau; Welling-
ton, the Hutt; and Auokland, the
Waikato. And with all this power
running to waste coal is dragged
round the coast from Westland at a
shocking expense. In some respects Maoriland
is fast enough ; in some others, a caterpillar could
give it ten yards’ start and win easily.

“ I have not seen a white woman for nine
years,” wrote a Far Northern telegraph-operator,
recently, to his new boss, Senator Drake. He
was appealing for a transfer, and it is pleasing to
learn that he got it.
• * • •
Something is to be done at last about the
removal of the George-st. Asylum, Parramatta.
The buildings at Rook wood are to be much
enlarged, and the old men drafted thither, and
the present George-st. building abandoned. Any-
thing must be better than allowing the hundreds
of helpless inmates to remain where they are, to
be burnt alive in the long run ; but it is not alto-
gether a good idea transferring so many of them
to Rook wood. As a matter of fact, N.S.W.
Public Works Committee strongly objected to
making Rookwood the central pivot of the asylum
system—first, sentimentally, because of the prox-
imity of the cemetery on the one side; and second,
practically, because of the closeness of the water-
supply of the city on the other. In view of the
existence of this report, it is strange how the
Chief Secretary has managed to sanction the
present scheme. Reports of committees and other
public bodies always have one fate—the pigeonhole.
The duties of an Australian Premier are
multifarious. As an instance, the venerable Dr.
Ross, of N.S.W. Assembly, demands that John
See will find out from the Government Medical
Officer whether it is healthy to sleep with the
window open at night. Mr. See isn’t a strikingly
patient man, and this question,
which is one of about two
dozen, should be about the
last straw.
BEYOND RUBIES.
“Ah! true love cannot be bought."
“No, but one can sometimes get a satisfactory
imitation for cash."
SYDNEY, 1902.—AT A
LODGE DISPENSARY.
THE DOCTOR (to the man of all work):
“ Well, Sam, what is it?”
SAM : “ Please, Doctor, the meter man has been
here about the waste of water. He saw the
labels on your physic-bottles about the mixture
being taken three times a day in water. He
wants to know if yer can't let the sick folk
take it neat till more rain comes.”
“The grand Coronation display” of
N.S.W. public school children, ostensibly in aid
of the Mt. Kerabla and Martinique disaster funds,
but much more really for the glorification of cer-
tain smug officials of the Education Department,
was held in Sydney on Nov. 26—a blazing day.
A heat-wave made the city gasp and wilt; never-
theless 12,000 children were mercilessly assembled
on the shadeless cricket-ground, and put through
circus business which, in many cases, amounted
to positive cruelty. For instance, 5000 girls and
boys were put through dumb-bell exercise, and
600 boys set to “maze-running” at a time when
the sun was at tropical heat; trashing cane north
of Bundaberg was not comparable with this as a
tax on human energy. The Sydney daily papers
seem to have been prevented by a con-
temptible timidity from saying what should have
been said about the show. Thus Sydney D.T., as
though it were recording the necessary labor of
some domestic animals instead of an absolutely
useless tax on young citizens :
Another popular, not to say fascinating, item was the
running maze by 600 boys. It was the most trying event
of the day to the competitors on account of the heat,
which was at its worst about the time the event came on.
“These boys are trained to do three miles,” said some-
body. They seemed willing to go on till they dropped,
and left the ground with the perspiration ‘practising
rivulet races down their faces. The movements were
cleverly and artistically arranged.
S.M. Hekald, on the same point with a lady-like
diffidence:—
Then 600 boys ran “mazes,” and showed that they
possessed extraordinary stamina. Under the heat condi-
tions this item might have been somewhat curtailed.
Asa matter of truth, the display from first to last
was barbarous; and the charity pretence of it
makes it none the less condemnable. The real
reason shows out quite plainly in the long list
given by the Herald of the officials of the day,
beginning with “ the Hon. J. Perry, Mr. J. 6.
Maynard and Mr. Bridges,” and in the smug
statement that the Dept, “at the instance of
Mr. Bridges outrivalled itself.”
N.S.W. Parliament should have prompt speech
with the Government on this children’s display
question. If it were only that the circus business
interfered with the educational purposes of the
department and the health of the children and
wasted much valuable time, it would be bad
enough. But in addition to that disorganising
effect, there are the objections that teachers and
scholars are submitted to harassing and practically
compulsory calls for the cost of costumes, etc.
Parliament should say firmly that this display
business is to stop right now. Let the vanity of
Mr. Perry and Mr. Maynard and Mr. Bridges and
other “ officials of the day ” find some other out-
et.

N.S.W. Parliament House, which
the Owe’Sullivan boasts is “the most
comfortable in Australia,” is truly a
luxurious place. There are smoking
rooms, whisky-rooms, lounge-rooms,
come -and - let - us - have-it-out-rooms,
bowling greens, tennis courts, cards,
dominoes, and wonderfully compli-
cated bath arrangements (plunge,
shower, squirt, douche, etc.,
etc.) About the only thing
lacking is golf-links, and the
Domain may yet be resumed
'' for that purpose. By the way,
there is also a library, and a
refreshment room where you
may feed as cheaply as in a
Sussex-st. hash-shop, and a long
sight better.
A nautical man writes re the Elingamite
wreck and similar misadventures:—
Seems to me the travelling- public are themselves much
to blame for such happenings. As long as they require
to be landed in a certain place on a certain date owners
will continue to expect shipmasters to take all kinds of
chances with that object. Apparently the Elingamite
experienced some 36 hours of fog previous to the
disaster, but the skipper, trusting, no doubt, to his former
experience and to the courses steered, kept on in the
hope that he would flounder past the danger. But no
man knows enough of the world’s currents to be able to
predict in what direction'they will set him for the next
12 hours. In thick weather one of two things should be
done. If in anchoring ground put your “ hook ” down
whilst you have water under your vessel; but if not in
soundings stop —which is perhaps what the Elingamite’s
skipper should have done. But this is just what hardly
any skipper will venture to do when there is an arrival date
to be kept, and a “carpeting” from the directors loom-
ing in the distance. In my opinion the first duty of a
shipmaster is to keep his own end up, and, incidentally,
that of his brethren. He must abstain from taking any
risks in order to save shipowners’ time— i.e., money ; and
all hankering after the reputation of a “ smart man ” he
must eschew as he would the devil. The shipowner
usually views these matters from a totally different
standpoint from the skipper’s because the vessel is
usually well insured, whilst no insurance co. has ever
“ written a line ” on the master’s “ parchment.”

The Art Society of N.S.W. is desirous of
obtaining a royal charter giving it the right to
call itself henceforth the “Royal” Art Society.
To this end a deputation has waited on Jingo
Education Minister Perry who promises to attend
to this matter without delay. The Royalising
deputation included citizen Collingridge, who with
so much verve musically declaims “ The Marseill-
aise” at Art Society functions.
* « • •
That sharp young man, Sydney solicitor
Chen hall, was struck off the rolls, last week, after
a valorous fight against several charges. Mr.
Chenhall came to the front in the gent.-one busi-
ness with astonishing rapidity, and acquired a
large practice; over-smartness was the cause of
his downfall. There is a notable absence in Aus-
tralia just now of young men who look likely to
develop into the quiet, irreproachably honest,
absolutely reliable “family solicitor,” of the old
pink-cheeked, white-haired type. The dear old
family solicitor who knew everything and told
nothing, and always wore black clothes and was a
second father to “Miss Dorothy” and “Miss
Mary,” is becoming as extinct as the dodo; the
legal profession gets more sharkish every year.

A Sydney firm of undertakers has issued
and is circulating a reprint of a hygienic leaflet of
the National Health Society of London, entitled,
“ Accidents and Emergencies; or What to do till
the Doctor Gomes.” This is indeed humor !
* * * *
It would be hard to find a meaner thing
than that Newcastle (N.S.W.), church-committee
which has allowed its clergyman to get into the
Insolvency Court through guaranteeing an ad vance
of £3404 from a bank. According to the unlucky
insolvent’s story he had been personally opposed
to borrowing, but was outvoted by the parochial
council, and was induced to sign the guarantee
with three others, two of whom are since dead
(the other has left). “ Only a matter of form,” the
poor man was assured when he signed the
guarantee. The money was borrowed to build a
church, and the church was built, and, to all
appearance, nobody bothered about how the debt
was to be paid. The bank, by the way, seems to
be left. When the godly borrow from the ungodly,
the ungodly generally fall in.
* * • *
Melbourne Board of Health is doing its
best to drive the local population out into the bush
to eat berries and yams like its far-back ancestors.
Last week it had a field day in showing up the
true inwardness of the public’s food. Raspberry
vinegar, almost wholly the drink of children, was
shown to be (40 out of 41 brands in the market) a
compound of deleterious chemicals; 75 per cent,
of the city restaurants were reported as absolutely
insanitary and likely to disseminate ptomaine
poison among their patrons. In many of the cigar
factories it was customary to finish off cigars with
the saliva of the operative—possibly a sufferer
from cancer, tuberculosis or syphilis; and, as a
bonne bouche, came the description of the oyster-
shops, in one of which the invariable practice was
to sprinkle those oysters that looked at all stale
with water from the latrines !
* * * •
In connection with the disposal of the
body of veteran mariner John Rees, who, before
dying at Sydney the other day, asked that his
remains be taken out to sea and dropped over-
board—four tug-boat proprietors were consulted
ere a crew could be guaranteed to undertake the
work. Sailor superstitions die hard, and this one
was freshened up by the case of the steamer which
lately came to grief off the M.L. coast with the
bones of 500 Chinese on board.
* * * •
The Eddy (N.S.W. railway employees’)
Orphanage Bill, after encountering strong opposi-
tion in the Leg. Council, has met a violent death
at the hands of a Select Committee. Premier See
took the measure up, as a matter of form, and his
representative in the Council, who tried to recon-
cile the opposition to it, was painfully ignorant as
to the merits Of the case. The exponents of the
benefits of the boarding-out system were particu-
larly strong against the orphanage idea, and the
Ministry, rather than battle over the Bill, have
thrown it up.
* • * •
The brutal Mrs. Drake, of Wellington,
M.L., who had so inexplicable a hatred for her
youngest child that she ultimately beat it to
death, seems to have a prototype in England.
A Mrs. Penruddoclte, wife of a man of
great wealth, and lord of manors and “ moated
granges,” has just been fined £SO at the Old
Bailey, London, for cruelly ill-treating her
youngest (six-year-old) daughter. The case
coincided with the M.L. one in almost every par-
ticular. In evidence, it was shown that the
mother had a strong feeling of hatred for that one
particular child from its birth, and former ser-
vants told (truly or untruly) of horrible sufferings
and brutalities inflicted on the unfortunate mite.
Medical evidence shewed the child to be a mass
of bruises caused either by a whip or stick, whilst
others were unmistakably caused by kicking.
The fining of this woman, the wife of an ultra-
