* * * *
At Invercargill (M.L.) the three-fifths
majority required to carry total prohibition didn’t
eventuate —-but it was only nine votes short. Nine
more votes would have deprived the saddest-
looking, wettest-looking, grayest-looking town in
all Maoriland of its last remaining shred of conso-
lation. The writer was at Invercargill once. Only
a timely whisky saved his life while waiting for
the protracted snail-express to take him to
Dunedin. To be a sober stranger at Invercargill
is much the same as being a bookmaker in Green-
land, or trying to sell a fire-escape to the
Esquimaux.
Thank goodness there are in Sydney some
people of instinctive Dignity. For quite two
years the aesthetic soul of Mr. E. A. Nichols has
revolted against the appellation of “ Town” Hall
and “Town” Clerk, but he could arouse no
enthusiasm in the matter —the inartistic municipal
authorities went on their own beastly way. Now
the advent of the Lord Mayorship has moved Mr.
Nichols to make one final appeal to the new
council (when it is elected) at its first meeting to
recognise the honor the King has bestowed upon
the collection of lanes, dog-boxes and insanitary
dwellings, known as Sydney City, by re-naming
those municipal appurtenances the. City Hall and
City Clerk respectively. Mr. Nichols is quite
right, but doesn’t go far enough. The Town
Hall should be called the Mansion House, and the
Town Clerk the High Beadle. There’s something
very majestic about the term Beadle, and if
Mansion House is not sufficiently high sounding,
the building might be styled The Beadlery.
* * * *
Victorian State Premier having complained
that the “ Lording ” of Melbourne’s Mayor was at
the recommendation of the Federal, and not of the
State Government, Mr. Barton, in reply :
Do they take us for a parish council ? I suppose we
can recommend anything we like.
The danger is that the Australian Govt, will be
taken for a parish council by meddling, not by
refusing to meddle, with “Lord-Mayor” matters ;
that emphatically is the province of the State
Governments —until the municipal counoils or the
J’s.P. take it over. A national Government
should take no interest in such gew-gaws.
* * • •
Said that Sydney Municipal Council will
have to take over the garbage-destructor from the
contractors, though it is claimed to be unsatisfac-
tory. The contract, it is alleged, was never
reviewed by the city solicitor, and under its terms
the contractors have the council by the hip,
whether there is valid cause for complaint or not.
Why is there always a smell about a garbage-
destructor contract ?
• • * *
The most pathetic feature of the Dill-
Macky agitation came to light in the kitchen
wenches’ organ the other Sunday. A waitress and a
Catholic laid bare to a pitying world the agony
she had to endure through hearing two diners cor-
dially agree with Dill, without, owing to her
position, being able to tell them what she thought
of them and their Sydney Calvin. For a staunch
sectarian, and a woman at that, to be unable to
express her opinion while the other side consigned
her particular prophet to Tophet is indeed misery.

Choice sarcasm wasted by a Gippslander
on the stodgy Minister Bent, whose trains are
running ever later and later:—
I would suggest, as a means of shortening the 8 hours
occupied by trains in covering the 128 miles between
Melbourne and Sale, that the carriages be provided with
an auxiliary mast and sails, so that advantage could be
taken of the generally prevailing strong winds on the
journey hither.

Some N.S.W. Wesleyan clergymen, it was
stated officially the other day, receive only £72 a
year. There must still be some people who
believe in religion—or are too poor to get out of
the re'igious mallee.
People in Melbourne have of late become
addicted to accusing themselves of murders which
they have never committed. A few weeks back a
girl told the detectives a circumstantial story of
now she had killed her new-born baby, and had a
dozen members of the police force digging up a
paddock for several hours, until a little girl came
along with a squalling infant in her arms and
asked if that was the kid they were looking for.
On top of this came a Carlton man, who strolled
into the local watchhouse and gave a most sensa-
tional description of how he had killed his wife
with an axe as she lay asleep on a couch. A
jaded constable took down several sheets of graphic
descriptive matter from him, while his comrades
went to look for the body; and he was
just finishing up the bloodstained story when
they returned with the “corpse,” who looked
quite indignant as she bade her husband not to
make a fool of himself but to go to bed. The
police, however, declined to give him up, and
charged him with insulting behaviour, or some-
thing equally vague. But the record in this
direction is still held by that Vic. up-country girl
who solemnly set out to blight her own character,
some months ago, by registering the illegitimate
infant she had never possessed, and thereby tried
to prove that she had “gone wrong” when she
hadn’t. Her attempt to get rid of her respect-
ability failed —still, she did her best.

A strange story of attempted but ineffectual
double suicide was told last week in West Mait-
land (N.S.W.) police-court, where the male would-
be (or wouldn’t-be) suicide was charged with
abduction. The parties were cousins, and very
young, the girl being only 16, and they decided to
die together for reasons that are not very clear.
This is the girl’s tale of their dying, on a certain
Friday night, put into dull, unromantic police-
court language.—
After staying together in the bush for some time,
Potter produced a bottle and said : “Drink this ; it will
kill you.” She drank half, and he drank the other half.
They lay on the ground for about two hours, but the
liquor had no effect. They went away then, leaving her
hat behind.
They went over to the railway line, Potter saying:
“ We will go over and put our heads under.the train."
They sat close to the rails, but she did not put her head
on the line.
They got up and went to Baker’s water-hole. Potter
got a large stone and told her to put it in an old skirt she
was carrying. He tied the skirt to her and to himself,
and both jumped into the water, which was up to her
neck. They remained in it a couple of minutes, when the
stone slipped off and they walked out.
Potter produced a razor and said : “ Come, to where we
took the drink, and I’ll cut your throat, then my own.
The pair afterwards walked to Thornton and thence
proceeded to Waratah, reaching the house of accused’s
mother on Monday night.
One hat and a valuable brick and probably the old
skirt were lost in these proceedings, and both

* • * *
Like Capt. M'Lerie, Inspector-General
Fosbery once set a trap for a Sydney black-mailer
of the park order. An ex-captain of the R.N.,
living at the Australian Club in Bent-street, was
the victim of a well-dressed _ scoundrel who
threatened to accuse him of a vile offence unless
he parted £2O. The old warrior, rather than fight
so unsavory a matter, paid up. Two months later
another demand was made for £2o—this time with
a promise that the ex-captain should not be
troubled any more. The money was paid but the
promise was broken; a demand for £2O more was
made, and this time the old man sought the advice
of the chief of police, who told him to meet the
man at the usual place in the Domain, and give
him four marked £5 notes. The appointment was
kept, the notes paid over, and the ’mailer was
picked up by two detectives who had been
watching the proceedings. Mr. Justice Hargrave
sent the ’mailer up for 14 years.
A former N.S.W. Chief Justice, when a young
man, was the victim of a ’mailer. He got a note
in a lady’s hand, in which the writer confessed to
an attachment for “Jimmy” and asked for a
meeting in the Domain. The susceptible lawyer
kept the appointment, and adjourned to a house
in Crown-street, Woolloomooloo; presently the
door opened and the woman’s husband appeared
on the scene. “Trapped, by 1” exclaimed
the learned counsel, in his characteristic way—
‘ ‘ how much ? ” That lawyer always went at once
to the heart of the matter. He had to pay £lOOO.
* * * *
Considering the many holes, orifices,
cracks, and discrepancies in that charge laid by
certain Chows (now awaiting trial for perjury)
against two engineers of the s.s. Chingtu accused
of throwing another Chow overboard in broad day-
light—it looks as if the was not the
capable and polished liar he is cracked up to be.
The same fact has often appeared before. The
Chinaman is a persistent liar, and he presents a
bland, unblushing, Joss-like front when found
out; but he is pitiably crude, and he is always
being found out. It is probably his immense
experience in being found out that makes him
so impervious. Compared with the Hindoo,
the Greek, and the miscellaneous Levantine the
Chinaman can’t lie worth 2d., and his persistence
in following a business for which he is quite
unfitted is lamentable.

When a very small case has money in it
flies are not a circumstance to the gathering of
learned counsel round the carcase. A perfectly
friendly suit in which all that was asked was the
Court’s opinion re the interpretation of a N.S.W.
will recently gathered together seven barristers, all
three-deckers in their profession, and all elabor-
ately instructed by solicitors. In fact, one bar-
rister was instructed by three solicitors, and
Heaven only knows how many solicitors the
other six were instructed by.

A returned warrior was charged at South
Melbourne, Nov. 24, with obscene language. He
was fined 605.; in default, 28 days’ gaol. When
it was subsequently elicited that he had fought
for his Cohentry in S’Afriea, the Bench sympa-
thised with him to the extent of reducing the fine
to 20s.
The Ideal GIRL
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
iONG had I sought for her, the fair.
Ideal maiden, tender, true,
With Marguerite-like flaxen hair,
And eyes as innocent as dew.
The daughter of the glowing South
I thought—though fair indeed is she,
With bosom bold, and scarlet mouth,
Too frankly amorous for me.
The maiden of my heart’s desire,
Of whom I dreamt by day and night,
I would not have her made of fire,
But, say, of incandescent light.
At last T found her, the divine
White incarnation of my wish—
Her breath sent shudders down my spine,
Her hand was like a frozen fish!
I yearn no more for such a pearl,
So pure and cold ; enough for me
A warm, pink, hundredweight of girl
To hug and nurse upon my knee.
O.R.

In reply to Premier Barton’s inquiry as to
whether the offer to hand over the Northern
Territory unconditionally to the Commonwealth
is still open, Premier Jenkins states that it is
suspended until the expiry of the period in which
tenders are receivable for the building of the
90,000,000 acre-grab railway. When the railway-
swindle tender is accepted, or when the 90,000,000
acre “cop” definitely fails, then Jenkins isopen to
negotiate afresh, but he states that he is going to
run no risk of the job being interfered with
by handing over the Territory now. This leaves
it still an open question whether Jenkins would
be willing to hand over the Territory, and drop
the land-grant syndicate foolishness, provided the
Australian Parliament pledged itself to build the
Transcontinental line as a national work within 10
or 15 years, and bought up the existing fragment
of railway that ends at Oodnadatta. If the
Jenkins Ministry is so wedded to its syndicate
that it won’t deal even on these grounds, then the
position is clear and definite. The only thing left
is for the Australian Parliament to make the
land tax a Federal impost, and so graduate it as to
slay Jenkins’ 90,000,000 acres syndicate dead.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 2, 1902.
My Dear Arini—
Concerning the Continental at Sydney Cricket
Ground, time and place were admirable. The
weather might have advantageously been
warmer. Grand-stands, band-stands, outlined
with soft, unwinking electric light of many
colors. The green basin of turf ringed
by the grey asphalt track. But the live
attractions, if the promoters hope to make
the departure permanently successful, will have
to have more life. They were tame and un-
varied. Between 4000 and 5000 persons were pre-
sent a number of them in evening-dress. All
fashionable Sydney “continented ” watching fire-
works, listening to the bands, looking at thp Cole-
manscarecrow, the Human Frog, and the very stout
lady on the wire-rope, in mournful silence.' The
quiet of those thousands of people in the half-light
made the scene somewhat eerie. Not a pushite
present. A party of Americans, with memories
of Coney Island hanging around them, went out
with a gun in each hip pocket. But there was
no row, except when tne fireworks soared up into
an amethyst, starless sky, and rained weeping
willows of flame and meteors of many hues. The
dresses, many of them, vied in beauty with those
of a race meeting.
An item for the Psychical Research Society is
the story vouched for by Mr. and Mrs. Leefson,
survivors from the Elingamite wreck, of their
two little girls dreaming simultaneously the night
before the disaster of its occurrence. Vanda,
aged four, was with her parents, and amused pas-
sengers, an hour or two before the Three Kings
were sighted, by telling how she had dreamt that
“this ship struckted on a rock and was breakted
up.” Cora, six years old, remained in N.S.W.
with friends, and awoke the same fatal morning,
crying, because she had seen her father and
mother and Vanda “ wrecked on the Elingamite.”
A remarkable sympathy exists between the two
children. Vanda, during the first hour of horror,
did nothing but call out for Cora, except once,
when she said, “Oh, my two dollies in the cabin !
they’ll be drownded.”
Annual meeting of Women’s Society for Pre-
vention of Cruelty to Animals seemed to me a
sample of cruelty to Lady Rawson. She was at
the Town Hall at 3 o’clock, and after Archbishop
Smith and several other gentlemen had spoken,
Her Ex. began to present the prizes to 500
children who had scored in the essay competition
on the humane treatment of animals. Stacks of
books were around the lady, she was barricaded
in them. “Aurelian Jenks,” said the Archbishop,
and the youngster trotted up the little ladder
from the body of the hall to the platform. “Alice
Maud Isabel Smith,” droned the Archbishop,
and then A. M. I. Smith hustled her way
from the back of the hall up to the steps and
climbed them in the last stage of exhaustion.
It was after 4 when I left, and Her Ex. was still
standing, looking dazed with weariness, and the
appalling barricade of books seemed as high as
ever.
Ex-Mayoress Harris and other ladies who sub-
scribed towards a wedding present to the Countess
Beauchamp are to be complimented on their
ability to keep a secret. A great cry of anguish
has gone up, high as the Beauchamp Towers;
“why wasn’t I also asked to subscribe?” from
those women who would, but were not given a
chance. _On the day of the Earl’s marriage, a
subscription cable of congratulation was forwarded
to him, and swift came a reply, under the stimulus
of which a present was" decided upon—a fan.
Ostriches at South Head furnished the beautiful
feathers; sticks of tortoiseshell, made-up ig
Sydney, bear a coronet in Australian silver, the
bride’s monogram, and a spray of jessamine. This
beautiful cooler and fly-frightener has gone on its
journey in a box made of Australian woods ; sub-
scribers’ _ names are written in a white vellum
book, painted with Australian flowers.
The picturesque-looking daughter, Christian, of
Mr. Hugh Dixson, of tobacco fame, was married
the other day to Mr. Rowe Thornett, an English
man whom she met whilst abroad. The bride
made her vows in a shimmering gown of white
lisse, flounced with rare lace, valued at 80 guineas
a flounce, and a Brussels-lace veil.
People _ who go abroad in the dawning see
strange sights, and quite recently an aristocratic
portion of Sydney, such of it as was up betimes,
was astounded to see its most fashionable cleric
coming down street in a milk-cart with a fair lady
holding on to him rather firmly. The cleric and
his friend had been out boating, and an unex-
pected southerly had made it necessary to pull
for their lives to a down-harbor bay. The only
conveyance obtainable was the passing milk-cart,
and as the driver kindly offered to see them right
home, pride was mastered by necessity.
Nora McKay, who fiddled at Amy Castles’
concert and later for Dolores at three concerts,
left for London and further study last Saturday
amid the usual rain of roses. At her own concert
in her birthplace, Maryborough (Q.), she was
given an opal and diamond ring and a purse of
£BO. Eva Mylott and the young Maonlander,
Soprano Nora Long, have returned from their
Northern Rivers tour with some money and many
freckles. When Miss Long was not on the plat-
form she was taking tickets at the door. Miss
Mylott, who goes hence next Saturday, was pre-
sented with the proceeds of her late concert, £290,
at the Australia Hotel.
Australian Bushman Captain Hedley Fitz-
patrick, son of a N.S.W. and Darling Downs
squatter, is returning to Australia with his Boer
bride, whom he married in the Transvaal. It will
be such a surprise and disappointment to Aus-
tralia if the lady does not resemble Tant Sanna (in
“ The Story of an African Farm ”) who drank so
much coffee and went to bed in her boots, and
called the rogue Englishman “a thief and a
Roman Catholic.” Captain Fitzpatrick leads the
Boer-seekers after farming knowledge who are
coming here at the desire of Lord Milner to study
our (lack of) methods of irrigation in the drought
areas.
Isn t delphinium a beautiful word, and isn’t it a
beautiful flower, and why don’t more people grow
it ?_ A hardy perennial, in color from the deepest
indigo to the faintest lilac and pink. Some lovely
one 3 are on view in flower-shops. Mr. Frank
Morris, of Summer Hill, is one of the best exhibi-
tors. He is a racehorse owner, whose leisure runs
to flower-cultivation.
Certain fashion writers and one medical man in
England are welcoming the new Trottoir, or
ladies walk skirt affectionately. Now, women
no longer burdened with and hobbled by a long-
tailed gown, “ will once more take to the graceful
and healthy exercise of walking.” But surely the
skirt has not so much to do with walking going
out of fashion as the shoe. I know women who
would “love to walk everywhere,” and who are
getting fatter and fatter for want of exercise, but
they won’t wear a moderate-heeled shoe or-one
that hasn t gunboat toes.
The tram-guard who shot off his girl’s ear at
Kozelle (Sydney) has proved the scorching quality
of his affection $ but the “ beloved ” damsel
doesn t seem to have taken the proof as did
Lizerunt” (in “Tales of Mean Streets”).
Luza Hunt smiled joyfully when her bloke beat
her head against a stone wall, for she knew
this w as love. The Sydney tramway men are
getting too forward in life’s picture ; there lies
much peril to damsel’s hearts in their brass
buttons and uniforms. For the sake of heartsease
the Commissioners will have to put their men
into moleskins and billycocks.
A six-years-old of my acquaintance, just re-
turned from London, had been admonished by his
governess that if he were not good he would never
go to Heaven. “ I’ve been to the Coronation, the
circus and the Zoo,” he replied, “I can’t expect
to go everywhere ! ”
Amy Castles will probably give one concert
apiece in the Commonwealth capitals before her
departure for Europe. These concerts will also
serve to introduce her sister Dolly. Prof. Peter-
sen, of the Melbourne Conservatorium, has said
brave things of the junior Castles’ soprano, and
it’s a rather good idea to let Australia hear it
before it goes on the songsmith’s anvil.
With a look of conscientious insincerity on its
face, the audience sat out the magnificent crash-
bang with which the Amateur Orchestral Society
closed its eleventh season last week. The bang-
crash was all Wagner—drums, horns, violins,
’cellos, trumpets, pugilistic harmony, Titania
music, funerals, weddings, shooting stars, rifted
clouds, starlight, storms, and galloping hoofs.
But the audience looked, even when it applauded
wildly, as if it had a headache at the base of the
brain. The Siegfried Idyll was a soothing inter-
val in the building of the tower of music. The
Ride of the Walkyries—well, as a local writer has
just remarked, “a vivid imagination is a curse,” for
when the Walkyries start their ride to and from
Walhalla, I don’t hear the music any longer, and
I must label the steeds by the names of the horses
immortalised by our own horse-poets, then start
them racing on the no-visible-means-of-support
track made of drums, trumpets, and screaming
violins. Old Pardon, the son of Reprieve, sud-
denly spreads his wings and jostles oiie of Ogilvie’s
White Horses, then—
Their Exes, of Cranbrook and suite were
present near the little round table with the posies
and extra gilt programmes. What a day they
had had ! Spent the afternoon, 91 in the shade
watching 12,000 unfortunate Public School children
demonstrating to the glory of Perry and Bridges
on the Cricket Ground. Then a tonic in the
shape of a Wagner programme !
That cricket match in aid of the Elingamite
fund between the girls of J. C. Williamson’s
Comedy Co., now in M.L., must have been comic
beyond all whooping. The athletic Blanche
Wallace making frantic dashes at run-getting
whilst Elsie Moore chased the ball, deserves
such recognition in poetry as McDougall got when
he topped the score. Misses Rose Musgrove and
Lane-Brown ran tea-tents and sold cigars. Some
of the girls of this co. have been taken to the
bosom of fashionable Wellington and entertained
.lavishly in its art-needle-work drawing-room.
On Dec. 13, whilst Melba will be Rigoletting
and Margueriting at Sydney Royal, Dolores will
be singing at Sydney Town Hall. The way these
two sopranos lyric in each other’s tracks ! Dolores’
concert will be a “ complimentaiy ” tendered to
Maestro Sykes, who first introduced Dimples—
then known as Trebelli-to the Australian public.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
* Melba, as seen at her second operatic per-
formance, lacked 6orae of the calm dignity of her
concert ‘form.’ An auburn wig, covering her
own dark hair, was partly responsible for the
touch of garishness. Then, two out of her four
toilettes were sleeping-suits, and a night-gown,
no matter how chic, -is not ideal wear for the
buxom Melba. As the gorgeous Violetta in the
banquet scene of ‘La Traviata ’ the star was
dazzling in languors of lace and rapturous roses,
while, without exaggeration, she seemed lapped
in a cloud of diamonds, as if the notes as they fell
from her lips had turned into brilliants.
“Melba, sharing love among the roses with
Arens, in rustic cot, looked stoutish, prosaic and
modern in up-to-date suit of pale blue and smart
walking hat. The Diva’s queer little bob-neck
bows and nods were as noticeable as ever, also a
non-patrician habit of bouncing in her chair to
express glee. Judging from her few sample
dancing-steps, our own prima donna would not
shine more brightly as ‘ Carmen ’ than Agnes
Janson was wont to do when she flounced round
as the light-heeled coquette.
“Thirty thousand pounds’worth of rainbow-
tinted gems weighed upon Melba’s shoulders in
her ‘ Traviata ’ role, and their massive groupings
gave an appearance of oppressive weight. The
unique bracketing of diamond armorials on the
bodice was matched by a necklet which had heavy
pendants all the way round. Diamond shoulder-
straps and a diamond fan were other small details.
The dress for the first act was of fine lace with
pink trimmings. ' All the ponderous diamonds
were dropped for the second act, when a blue and
white toilette was worn, and only one big, bright
gem sparkled at the waist. Not a glimmer of one
for the swan note scene. Two feverish eyes
supplied the only sparkle, and they went out in
the end.
“In his character of Gaston’s virtuous papa,
baritone Bensaude’s black gloves, undertaker’s
apparel, and sultry silk beaver were oppressive,
distracting attention from his impassioned strains.
Every time the hand that held the shiny ‘ topper ’
went out in action, one fell back from the crest of
a wave of emotion. That obstrusive hat put the
extinguisher on all that was not commonplace.
The baritone might have risen to some height but
for his hat.
“Old Mr. Mitchell shared a box with a
daughter and daughter-in-law, and never once
was so un-Scotch as to applaud his own lass in
public. Old Mr. Patterson booked his seat with
the gods. Melba and all her clan ‘ wanted to
smack’ Musgrove on Saturday night when a poor
house responded to his short-lived three-guinea
tactics.
“ Melba says she wants all Australia to hear
her. When she went to the home of her youth, at
Lilydale, t’other day, an old man walked in from
an out-back farm, hoping they might persuade
her to sing ‘just one song.’ That was tabooed,
she said, but she gave him two guineas, and told
him to book a seat and hear her in opera.
“ Melba in opera is like an illustrated poem.
In ‘Lucia’ the young, strangely beautiful voice
did wonders. The roulades were like fountain-
sprays glittering in the sun. The Voice enthralled
one completely.
“Berthed together in a box at the ‘Traviata’
performance were Miss ‘ Stonington ’ Clarke and
Miss ‘Beaulieu’ Buckley; the pair drove home
from the opera in a private hansom, unescorted.
Aide Browning looked after the rest of the family.
Surgeon O’Hara and co. held the stage-box with
Manager Musgroye, who folded his white-gloved
hands in an attitude of beneficent repose. ’Tis
said in places of accredited rumor that Mr. Mus-
grove has much occasion to soliloquise over the
sweet smiles of the Goddess of Fortune. He cer-
tainly looks very contented with himself.
“That Polo depends on fashionable support
alone, was proved by the very ‘ naice ’ gathering
that rallied to Keeyong Course, on Saturday, to
see the start of the season. ‘ Stonington ’ had a
special interest in the match, as Aide Browning
was one of the players. The cream turn-out of
Lady Silverlocks gained an air of smartness by
means of cornflower-blue borders, head-piece and
gamp. The sweet young hope of the house rode
to the.field of operations with an ice-cream escort.
Girls in bevies of twos and threes came unchaper-
oned in hansoms, and had for their reward a word
or two with the perspiring players while fresh
ponies were being re-saddled. Mrs. David
McCaughey got away from most of the muslin and
crash charmers in a racy frock of cerise foulard
with expensive lace fittings. Miss Lesly Madden
sported a theatrical dress of black with lilac.
“ The little State Ex. has a happy knack of
suiting the occasion. He contrives to fit into the
niche—whether it be social or official. At a State
ceremony his harness might be a coat of mail so
rigidly stiff he seems, so iron-bound, so preposter-
ously dignified. On the other hand, he drops into
Koonyong course, say, to have a look at Polo, as
mild and pleasant and inconsequential a little
chappie as ever stepped in shoe-leather. You
might pat him on the back with impunity.
His modest, walnut-shaped boxer would make
you feel pleasantly disposed to chaff the little al
fresco Excellency. When it’s working hours, he
works all right; and when it’s recreation time, he
plays, with an amusing effort to show playing
form. He’s really at heart a nice little fellow.
“ ‘ Majah ’ Johnston, who, after trailing his
sword in Africa, returns to his prosperous peram-
bulator business in a busy suburb of Melbourne, is
the coming polo champion of the season. At
Saturday’s match, when Kooyong and Melbourne
tried conclusions, the Majah’s fierce and brilliant
dashes gave the game its greatest impetus. A
smart pony, a good seat in the saddle, and a
swagger cut of figure all served to gain for the
perambulatah Majah special notice.
“There is talk of mixed bathing extending
beyond Carram this season, so the smart hands of
Melbourne’s big chiffon stores are preparing fancy
frilly gowns for the ii.ixed dip. The fluid that is
alleged to keep fringes in curl and dyes unharmed
in salt water is in wonderful demand. Dentists
should really supply their patrons with chains and
padlocks, for surely the greatest of misery of any
at the mixed-bathing meeting in the waters is the
fatality of drowned, stolen, strayed, or lost
molars ; and the missing plate is quite a common
sorrow.
“ Carrie Moore has an annoying habit of falling
into a convulsion of giggles, so that her words are
jostled in her mouth, and the ears of the audience
strained. Of course, her Yum Yum role in the
‘Mikado’ is necessarily gigglesome, but in the
‘Toreador’ show the laughing fits interfered so
much with the dialogue that the audience secretly
prayed for Carrie to move off. There is always
this to say of Maud Clietwynd, that her toes and
her tongue give equal point to any of her lines
and business.
“The home-coming of Cliveden’s mistress next
week brings more Clarkes into our ken, and
multiplies the difficulties of distinction. Also it
means that Society gets ready for a festival, for
widow Janet Clarke is hospitable and lavish. For
her active philanthropy, vanity, and popularity,
Janet Lady suffers from a constant buzzing of
appeals and a never-ending swarm of sycophants.
“A certain police magistrate was on a delicate
mission the other day—assisting the wife of a
Federal Minister to choose Xmas cards for
Joe Chamberlain, the Earl of Rosebery, and other
more or less known sons of the Empire. No secret
was made as to their destination. The matron
sought diligently for artistic pictures; it was her
companion’s duty to keep his eye open to see that
the cards were free from amorous messages or
saccharine sentiment—that, in short, Joseph was
not called ‘ Ducky ’ (which any Joseph would
resent). So the choice was a great work, and all
in the shop were much amused. At last pictorial
cards, depicting Australian sites, were agreed
upon, and all the shop assistants heaved a sigh of
relief.
“A kind, lovable, interesting man of many
parts passed away last week, Dr. J. J. Miller.
A book-lover, he hoarded up rare editions, and
choice volumes of the poets. An epicure, he was
widely known for his caviares , and he grew in his
own small garden-plots rare herbs, and was proud
of his salad recipes. He was also thoroughly
versed in music, and attended (with his very
charming wife) every good concert held in Mel-
bourne for years past. He collected special bric-
a-brac. Besides all of which cults he had the
military hobby (being a captain of artillery), a
taste for scientific research, and a fondness for
Goethe in the original. He was, withal, a modest
and retiring character, and was only 37 when he
succumbed to heart-failure, after a toilsome life,
working up a big suburban and Collins-st. practice
and fitting in a dozen hobbies.
“Dr. Miller leaves behind him a widow, two
children, and an old mother, whose only child he
was, and who had shared with her son the many
struggles which lie between a poor man and a
professional career. Young Miller took his
diploma at 24, and in no time had troops of
patients. His mother, a silver-haired belle,
blessed with a taste of blarney and a genius for
cooking, kept open-house for her son till he
married, and was famous for her Sunday-night
suppers, to which she welcomed as many as could
squeeze into their tiny cottage.
“Another death, recently announced, is that of
Mrs. Swinborn, an old Quaker identity, who in
her day had done heaps of practical good work.
She was an ‘old-school Friend,’ who believed in
work rather than words. Modern ‘ Friends ’ are
rather too evangelistic and sentimental to
altogether please the Old Lights. The proper
way is to sit mute in Meeting-House till the spirit
moves you to get up and deliver a moral essay,
almost secular in tone. Modern ‘Friends’ for fear
of driving away hearers by dullness, apparently
arrange that certain picked speakers are to hear
the call to sermonize whenever the pause threatens
to become painful. When some of the speakers
do get going, they call their hearers ‘ dear friends ’
and plead with them, which isn’t according to
Cocker—l mean Quaker. Even with these
modern tricks, a ‘Friends’ meeting is a bit eerie.
You sit there in awful silence till the trams and
street-noises seem to be gnawing your nerves.
Then some iron-gray patriarch gazing absently
over the meeting’s head orates impressively
‘peace, perfect peace, in this dark world of sin.’
Another awful pause, then he says the same
thing again, hissing the word ‘ sin ’ like an
accusing wind blowing through the key-hole of
conscience. ‘ Unfriendly,’ I call it.
“When Strong man Pagel goes into the lion’s
cage to pull the king of beasts about, the animal
crouches down and seems terrified, but his mate
‘ Doggie ’ welcomes the visitor with hospitable
tail-waggings and friskings. When Pagel leaves
the cage, the lion cheers up at once, indulges in
horseplay, and seems almost inclined to chew
‘Doggie’ in the re-action of his spirits. Pup
seems bored. Having sampled human society, he
can’t be bothered with stupid old Four-Legs.
Dogs are terribly uppish creatures. They fawn
on Man, and cold-shoulder their own kind, and
display a contempt for cats, however large.

_ “An extraordinary thing happened at a mar-
riage, the other day, at a church up about Eastern
Hill. The clergyman who was joining two hands
in holy matrimony discovered that the brides-
maids were bare headed, and didn’t even boast a
sprig of jessamine. He thereupon announced
that the bridesmaids must find some covering for
the head, according to the teaching of his church
concerning small matters matrimonial. The party
was distracted, till a practical person suggested
handkerchiefs. Three of a large size were forth-
coming, the maids submitted to the ludicrous
head-gear, and the marriage went on its way.
This is the holy edifice where romantic maidens
come in the twilight to confess the sins they are
inclined to, and all the faults they have a mind to.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

land clergymen in Victoria, one of whom began
life as a gaol warder, and the other as a policeman.

This is the consummation of my desires—something
that I had dared to dream of for many years past.

of the High Commissionership, and that the
Cabinet has practically decided., on Mr. B. R.
Wise for that position.
It seems that it was N.S.W. Premier John
See who was responsible for the Lord Mayoring of
Sydney’s (and incidentally Melbourne’s) civic
head. The Bulletin is reconciled to John See
being “the fount of honor”; it is a position that
just about suits J. See.

lar among politicians, and when Trenwith
entered Vic. Assembly, after the “contemptuous”
verdict for a shilling damages had been given

Premier Jenkins (S.A.), writes a well-
informed correspondent, must have it counted
unto him for righteousness that he did not think
twice before declining a proffered knighthood a
few months ago. He is a Yankee born, and is,
therefore, naturally averse to Imperialism and its
symbols. There was a suggestion that, as a com-
promise, the King might make him a “ Colonel.”

pieces on Manukau

few on board were
saved, and these
were all, a little
later, drowned in the
wreck of the London.

Prime Minister Barton’s eldest son, who
had been studying at Oxford, has returned to
Australia with his father. He intends ultimately
to practise at the Bar in Australia. Just now he
is acting as private secretary to his father, in place
of Mr T. R. Bavin, who has suffered a bereave-
ment in the loss of his little daughter. Mr. P. E
Flannery, the Prime Minister’s other secretary, is
recovering from an operation for the fashionable
malady, appendicitis.
The late Cecil Rhodes seems to have been
a man of strange language and curious schoolboy
English. He left a large sum of money to provide
scholarships, and explained that, in deciding which
boys were best entitled to them, three points were to
be allowed for “smug,” two points for “brutality,”
three points for “tact and leadership,” and two
for “unctuous rectitude.” And the matter isn’t
made much clearer by the explanation that
“smug” is much the same as “swot,” or else
“smug ”is the result of “swot.” It is necessary
to read such books as “ Stalky and Co.” to glean
even a remote idea of what the great man was
driving at.
Judge Moorhead, of Westralia, worried
himself to death at a comparatively early age.
Paralysis and complete mental collapse were the
visible causes of his death, last week, but an
anxious temperament, an immense capacity for
becoming anguished over small things, and a
conscience that was in over-good repair, probably
brought on the disaster. The charge of partiality
as a Judge of the Arbitration Court so got on his
Honor’s nerves that he defended himself in a 3-col.
speech from the bench, in which speech he related
nearly the whole story of his life. By next sitting
the semi-paralysed man had decided, by dint of
further worry, that in his speech he had been
unduly severe in some of his comments, and he
apologised to one of the persons commented upon.
Then he went home and gradually died.
The Irvine Cabinet is not a happy crowd.
M‘Kenzie and Bent never liked each other, and
since Bent has worked two or three points in the
House on his blind colleague in the matter of land
settlement and railway construction, the feeling
has grown in to hatred. The fat and jolly
Taverner, Minister for Agriculture, now on the
look-out for the Vic. Agent-Generalship, does not
get on too well with the lean and hungry Shiels.
Taverner wants to make believe that he
is spending a lot of money, whereas
Shiels sees that the popular ticket just
now is to pretend that the Ministry
is saving.
In the new Vic. Assembly,
Trenwith, Mackinnon, and
Watt are the clearest talkers ;
Prendergast, Duggan and
Peacock the fastest; Wil-
kins, Ward, and Ramsay
the loudest; Shiels the
most turgid; and Fink,
Peacock, and Maloney
the most involved. The
most difficult to under-
stand are Max Hirsch
(German), E. D. Williams
(Welsh), Mines Minister Cameron (Gaelic), and
Charles Forrest (Western District dialect). The
most Tory are Swinburne, M'Kenzie, and
honorary Minister M ‘Leod; the most humorous,
Andrews and Duffy; and the most humorless,
“Ben” Tucker, M‘Leod, and Premier Irvine.
Federal Ministers, as a rule, are poor, and
none of them has been made richer by the rate
session. By C. C. Kingston and Barton, both
poor men, the absolute sacrifice of any profes-
sional earnings must have been severely felt.
Kingston ought to be well-to-do, but he is
generous and Quixotically honest: when a brother
died leaving considerable debts, C. C. took them
all over and paid them. Barton wouldn’t save
money on £lO,OOO a year. John Forrest is
probably the richest man of the Cabinet.

Wragge’s Wragge gives the pleasant in-
formation that “we have a large batch of
aboriginal names for storms, and such we intend
to use in due course. We did not obtain them
from ‘Mestonus Superbus,’ but direct from the
blacks in the wild west.” Meanwhile we are
threatened with an “ elongated monsoonal depres-
sion called ‘ Leahy 1 (a rather appropriate name
for a disturbance just now), and an Antarctic
depression named ‘Airey.’” Note, by the way,
that Wragge (proprietor and editor of Wragge)
has now risen to the dignity of we.
The death of the wife of Melb. barrister,
John Fisher (widow of William Kerr) recalls
many incidents of the early days of Port Phillip
settlement. Kerr was Argus proprietor, and
from him Edward Wilson purchased the property
in the forties. He was subsequently Town Clerk
of Melbourne, and is remembered for a good per-
formance of Bailie Nicol Jarvie in the amateur
production of “Rob Roy,” by which was raised
the first portion of the fund for the erection of
Melbourne Hospital. Kerr was a live man, and
subtended a big angle in the young community ;
he was ably seconded in social matters by his wife,
who was very hospitable and popular.
Judge Moles worth last week declined to
deal with a County Court case at Ballarat, on the
ground that the defendant, Agar Wynne, had
been the Solicitor-General for Yic. and might be
a head of the Crown Law Dept, again, in which
case he (Molesworth) would be subordinate to the
Eolitician. “I once tried a case in which the
ead of my department was defendant, and I
afterwards was sorry for it,” said His Honor. If
Molesworth the outspoken continues to stick to
his point, the County Court Judges will presently
get emancipated from departmental control. At
present it is in the power of a Solicitor-General
to stop a County Court Judge’s salary when he is
sick.
“Jim” Halse, who, with two others, had
executive control of the co-operative stores that
were established during the long miners’ strike of
’92 at Broken Hill, died the other day. Unlike
most of the officers in that great row, Halse did
not have to move on ; he stayed and became a
shift-boss. That establishment of stores and
shops was the biggest idea jet worked out in con-
nection with any Australian strike: it put food
into the strikers’ mouths at absolutely cost price,
and it patched their boots for the cost of the
material, and not a penny of the strike-money
reached Bung, because it was all represented by
coupons, redeemable only at the stores. It wasn’t
Halse’s idea, but he worked as hard as any to
make it succeed.

N.S.W. Pastoralists’ Review, moved to
anger at the awful state of N.S.W. finances, con-
cludes an article: —
We do not know Mr. John See personally, and he may
be an estimable enough man in private life, but if he were
executed for high treason it appears to us that he would
richly deserve his fate, and that such an example would
do an immense deal of good throughout Australia and
Maori land.
No, The Bulletin wouldn’t cut off See’s head.
It would cut off his title. That would hurt him
far more.
One Elingamite survivor says that his
most vivid memory of the wreck is that of the
captain, when the ship was past hope, standing
in the water on two pieces of wreckage, a foot on
each, and submerged to the chest, calmly blowing
his whistle and giving orders just as if he were on
his vessel’s deck. He hurled abuse at Bill for
clumsiness and at Hans for slowness as he would
in the ordinary course —no more and no less. His
coolness was the salvation of many, by their own
admission, even women hushing their shrieks in
face of that example.
Sydney D.T.’s representative on Kingston:
There is no more persistent worker than Mr. Kingston.
It is to his disadvantage that he is slow and vacillating,
though his energy and his application are wonderful. At
present, he is seeking relaxation from Customs work by
drawing up his bills for next session. Mr. Kingston is not
only the best Parliamentary draughtsman in the Minis-
try ; he is, perhaps, the best in Australia. If it was said of
Mr. Kingston that he was one of the best draughtsmen in
the Empire, the statement would probably not turn out
to be an exaggeration. His really great powers in this
respect he is now devoting to an Industrial Arbitration
Bill, which is to be one of the earliest measures when
Parliament meets. Mr. Kingston has gone to immense
trouble over the work. He is said to have collected so
many papers and made so many notes and disposed of
them so carelessly that it takes him half a day to find any
odd scrap of paper that he requires. Mr. Kingston is a
curiously restless man, who prefers solitude to companion-
ship, and whose principal recreation is to sit on the front
seat of a cable dummy and ride along Collins-slreet. He
is a teetotaler, or practically so. He nevi r smokes in
public, and, except on special occasions, he wears a
tightly-buttoned faded sac coat, an old hat down hard on
his head, with the brim almost touching his ear. His
boots correspond with his hat. This quaint costume on
the back of a Minister in receipt of a handsome salary is
not an affectation. Mr. Kingston wears it probably
because he prefers it.
The Bulletin is still glad to remember that the
shabby, solitary toiler who rides on the front of
the tram was its nominee for the Australian
Premiership. In fact, as his clothes grow older
and his hat gets further down about his ears, it
feels more and more satisfied with its selection.
N.S.W. Chairman of Committees Cann is
threatened with a break-down, owing to nervous
strain. N.S.W. Parliament is very hard on its
officials, sitting, as it does, longer hours than any
other Australian legislature.
At a meeting of colonists of 1853, held in
Brisbane t’other night, Mr. John Byrne apologised
for non-attendance on the ground that he was
“ too feeble to be out after dark, having been born
in the year 1798.”
Suggested in London that the Jews should
testimonialise Pigott, “ the new Messiah,” in
sympathy for the persecution that he endures at
the hands of the Christians ! This is ironical, of
course, but so far the Jews have taken no part in
the demonstrations against this “ Messiah.” Once
bit, twice shy.
Worth while noticing that none of the big
Vic. wheat - buyers, who make much money
handling the Mallee farmers’ grain, has con-
tributed a penny to the fund for the relief of
those settlers. One of these wealthy wheat-kings
is in Vic. Leg. Council. He is reputed to have
made over £50,000 out of last year’s harvest, but
up to now his name has not appeared on any sub-
scription-list.
Certain quarters are much chagrined at
the developments in the career of the Fisheries
Bill through N.S.W. Assembly. As originally
drafted the measure provided for a Commissioner
at £lOOO a year, but the Cabinet, however, wasn’t
game, and only proposed £BOO to the House. In
committee, the billet was altered to a common
chairman (with a small “c”) at £5OO, and it is
hardly worth while engineering the Bill through
for such a small thing as that.
A Cable from Capetown to the English
papers :
Armed Kaffirs are dreaded in various districts of the
3ape Colony, and numerous attacks on women are
reported. The malefactors who retained their rifles
after the war, hold the settlers in terror.
These are the Kaffirs who, according to English
accounts, were not armed during the war.
Mr. George Barnard, a prominent Sydney
auctioneer who sells for one of the big pawn-shops,
and amuses his leisure by a lively interest in the
prize-ring, was the wrong sort to be a good mark
for a house-breaker, as a man recognised who
roamed into his house the other night and got
a shot through his hand and another through his
thigh. How many men are there in the worldr
who can register two hits in two shots at an
early-morning intruder ?
“Jaf.” : Government interference in the
constitution of the N.S.W. rabbit boards is much
resented. The chairman is usually the friend of
a doubtful “mimber.” Competence for the posi-
tion doesn’t count at all. The inspector is a Govt,
servant, appointed at a salary which eats up most
of the board’s funds, and not responsible to the
board in any way. The amalgamation of the
numerous stock, pasture, rabbit, &c., boards is
absolutely necessary, and Govt, interference
should not be tolerated.
MELBOURNE’S LORD MAYOR.
“His Majesty the King has been pleased to approve
of the title of “Lord Mayor” being conferred on the
Mayors of Melbourne and Sydney.”— DAILY PAPER.
The casual Australian female isquite eclipsed
by a reputed English specimen. She was a house-
maid anxious to be loved; the charwoman, in
whom she confided, invented a broth-of-a-boy
cousin in Ireland, and the girl (by correspondence
became engaged to him, and fixed a day for the
wedding ! All correspondence was carried on
through the go-between, and some nice presents
were sent to the phantom lover, who wrote affec-
tionately acknowledging them, but never sent any
in return. Finally the girl found money for fur-
nishing the happy home, whereon her lover fell
from a horse (by telegram) and “would never be
well enough to marry.” This non-delivery of the
matrimonial goods was a serious matter, and the
housemaid called in the police. The charwoman
is doing hard labor at present, but she ought to
make a successful novelist when she gets out.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — ln the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
“ Glenara ” : To be seen any day, on any
Victorian road ’twixt the Dividing Range and the
Southern Ocean, horses, cattle, sheep of all sorts
and conditions, carts of every description, men of
every brand, all heading in one long, unbroken
drift towards the coast. Some men riding, some
driving in bag roofed waggons, others (mostly
cattle-men) tramping, with their few belongings
packed on the footsore steers. By different tracks,
in different styles, they move, but all southwards,
fleeing from the northern horror, with its miles
upon miles of blistered desolation. Southern
Vic. railway-station. Sheep-owner (with 2000
starvers, just landed—soo of them dead—to a
passing teamster: “ Give you half the pelts to
take those dead ones away and bury them.”
“ Half the pelts ? Why, they’re only worth about
3d. each. ’Tain’t good enough.” “ Well, I’ll give
you all the skins. Must get them away from here
somehow.” “Chuck in another quid and I’m
willin’.” “Right y’ are.” And an hour later a
waggon, piled up with sheep-corpses, moves out
to a little-frequented spot amongst the hills.
Ararat Plains. New selector asks cattle-hunter
the price of one of his heifers. “ One of them?
We'don’t sell them in ‘ ones ’ nowadays. They’re
fid. to a bob a dozen, most of them. You can
take your pick for eighteenpence.”,.
• • * •
The man who was settled on the land—a
daily paper item from Ballarat: —
A sad illustration of the terrible straits to which
farmers in the Mallee have been reduced by the drought
was witnessed to-day in the city, when a once prosperous
farmer from Sheep Hills, near Donald, drove down Sturt-
street in a shattered waggon, the tilt of which was com-
posed of old cornsacks. This turnout and a dozen
starving horses, a crate of almost featherless hens, and a
few chairs and tables were all that were left of the fruits
of his 27 years’ toil. Accompanying the distressed settler
were his wife and children, who were perched on top of
the load. Scores of citizens assembled in the street to
gaze upon and pity the poverty-stricken family and their
pitiful turn-out. In conversation the unfortunate agri-
culturist said : “ We have travelled day and night since
leaving the famine region. It would make your heart
bleed to see some of the ruined farmers along the road I
have travelled—mothers wheeling perambulators and
carrying their children, and fathers walking behind
carrying blankets and a few necessaries.”
The three ages of agriculture in the dry country :
(1) Getting the people on the land; (2) helping
the people to remain on the land ; (3) getting the
people off the land.
* ♦ * *
The recent Victorian dust-tornado was the
last straw to the drought-cursed mallee. Water
channels, excavated at huge expense, have been
filled with sand and rubbish. Hundreds of miles
of these channels were made to tap the overflow
of the Wimmera River, but, as there has been no
overflow for years, there has been no water to
send along them. Vic. mallee water-schemes
have been an expensive luxury. There is scarcely
a hoof left in all the Wimmera district, as there is

“ Tonbur ” : Can’t see why there should
be such a fuss about the woes of the Vic. Mallee
people in particular, when many parts of Queens-
land and N.S.W. are in just as bad a plight. The
mallee never has been, and never will be, a wheat-
producing district. One crop in eight years is not
encouraging, and by supplying seed Government
is only prolonging the agony. But Vic. Govt,
has constructed such a number of useless railways
into the wilderness that it will do anything to g'
out of the difficulty.

Some landholders have made little fortunes
during the drought by agisting stock. A Kiandra

and 80,000 sheep for five months at 2s. a head,
which will pan out over ±I7OOO.

“5.0. C.” : Common now in Clunes (Vic.)
to see the mobs of half-starved stock passing
through to Gippsland. How they will exist
when they get there is a mystery. One mob, the
other day, was followed by a waggonette covered
with old bags, on the side of which was printed,
with tar or blacking, “ Root, Hog, or Die.” Next
day came anotlier mob, with an old man in a
waggon, his wife driving a spring dray, leading
three horses ; two daughters just ahead driving
cows and calves. “Fairly driven from home,”
the old man told me. “Going to Gippsland—or
anywhere—for feed.”
* * * *
“A.P.R.” : Be cruelty to animals (“Boko,”
B. 15/11/’O2). No one, if it can be avoided, will
allow £ISOO worth of stock to perish. Also, the
talk about “overstocking” is absurd. Four
years ago, the “overstocked” surplus of sheep
died off, and since then the Western flocks have
been almost exterminated. Last year decimated
the sheep in the State, and this year reduced the
remainder by half. When there is no grass, a
sheep to 1000 acres is “overstocking.” [lt may
?:reatly surprise “A.P.R.” to know that last year
1901) the sheep in N.S.W. increased by 1,800,000.
Horses, cattle and pigs also increased. For the
whole Commonwealth the increase of sheep in
1901 ivas over three millions. —Ed. B.]
* * * *
Another drought item. Down Queanbeyan
(N.S.W.) way, a mail-contractor (4 stages weekly,
64 miles in all) lost all his horses through the want
of feed, and, as he had no money to buy others, he
started padding with mails, etc., on his back. He
was found lying exhausted beside the track and
taken home, where he soon died. The home was
destitute.

In a disputed-will case lately heard at
Brisbane, the soundness of testator’s mind was
impugned on various grounds, one being that he
took a bath when on a “bender.” This happened
at thirsty Thargomindah.

‘ ‘ Oktis ” : Recently met eight Indian
hawkers in a body footing it from Melbourne to
the Richmond River (N.S.W.). Not one of them
could speak a word of English, their only guide
being a slip of paper on which were the names of
towns en route, with a request to direct the bearers
on their way.

“ Werrimee ” : Be snipe (B., 22/ll/’02).
The Australian snipe (Gattinago Australis) is a
bird of passage, being found in this country from
end of Aug. to the beginning of March, and in
Japan, where it breeds, from April to Aug.
Seebohm, in “ The Geographical Distribution of
Plovers, Sand-pipers and Snipes,” says it “breeds
in both islands of Japan, and passes the Philippine
Islands and the coast of China on migration to
winter (strictly, to summer) in Australia and
Tasmania. ” Mr. Alan Owston, of Yokohama, in
forwarding to A. J. Campbell, the Victorian
Ornithologist, the first eggs that reached
this country, stated: “ The birds breed on
the grassy moorlands at the foot of Fuji-
yama. . . . 2000 to 3000ft. above sea level.
Nest on ground, among grass. . . . Contained
four eggs. 17th May 1897.” The eggs are pear-

Though the snipe was described by Dr. Latham

* * •* *
In many parts of Australia it is considered
an honor to shoot the first snipe of the season. A
certain P. O. official on the Richmond used to belly-
warm the moorlands from midnight till sunrise to
catch the early bird. He gained the distinction
for three years in succession; but the fourth year
a man, bent on beating him, potted his bird the
day before, and hung it in a bush. A bare-legged
urchin, with a bridle on his arm, observed the
manoeuvre, and awaiting his chance, took the bird
and presented it to Stamps. The latter was out
early as usual next morning, deposited the dead
bird in a tussock, and then concealed himself
behind a log to watch. About dawn No. 2 came
along, fired at the bush, and, with a comioally-set
face, searched around for the game that wasn’t
there. Suddenly another echoing report rang out,
and Stamps came forward with a triumphant
smile. “ Beat you, Smithers, old chap! ”
“ Where?” asked Smithers, shamefacedly. “Fell
in that tussock.” Smithers rushed it; but at the
tussock he stopped, and his face became radiant.
Stamps pulled up a second later with a sickly
grin. The first snipe was stiff and cold, and
covered with a regiment of ants !

“ Rusty ” : Be the growling of the farmers
about N.S.W. Works Minister O’Sullivan attract-
ing workers from the country to Sydney. T’other
day, at Picton, I applied to a farmer who wanted
a man. Yes, he would give me the job—because
I was strong—at tucker and 2s. 6d. a week 1 In
same district another farmer pays his men 3s. or
4s. a week, while his own sons work on Govt, jobs
at 7s. a day. Small wonder the men make for
town in search of something like a fair wage.
• * * m
Victorian Government entomologist
French seems to have settled the grasshopper
question. His recent experiments with a grass-
hopper-destroying fungus near Warragul proved
very successful. The fungus is prepared in
water; bread or fruit is dipped in the water and
put in the way of the grasshoppers. They eat the
bait greedily, and farmers on whose property the
experiments were tried state that the hoppers died
in thousands.
• V • •
P.-M. General Drake has a strictly white-
Australia policy with regard to the letting of
mail contracts. The last man who ran the mails
between Birdsville (Q.) and Hergott Springs
(S.A.) had 13 colored grooms on the route.
Federal Govt, now stipulates that white men
must be put on, and no black labor employed in
any way connected with the carrying of mails.

Some of the far-back pastoralists—who
have sheep agisting at several widely-separated
places—have to “scratch gravel” now-a-days to
inspect their flocks. One man in the Gulf country
coached 950 miles, drove 280, and rode 550
exclusive of long rides and drives round
paddocks—to see how his much-divided herd was
getting on.

“ Wer.”: “Spinifex’s” distinction between
’coot and rat-’roo (B. 29/11/’O2) is an absurdity.
There are about 20 species of rat-’roos, and nearly
a dozen ’coots, all varying considerably in size
and color, so that no particular member of either
can be taken in this respect as representative of
the family. The animal he describes as a ’coot is
the black-striped wallaby. There is a striped
’coot, found in S.A. and W.A.; but the markings
are extremely light, and are across the body ; and
the animal when coiled up is about the size of a
man’s fist. Also, the average rat-’roo would make
three of the average ’coot; “ Spinifex ” puts it the
other way about!

Gerald ton, N.Q., reports the driest year
on record. Only 74 inches of rain since January.
Some of the roads quite dry. Bowen, 200 miles
south, has had 67 points in seven months. Char-
ters Towers, 100 miles west, is in such a state
that bathing is prohibited, and “soak” water
from the workings is being used for various pur-
poses.
» * * •
Supplementing “Frank S.’s” remarks
(8., 29/11/’O2) about milking-machines comes the
information that at Hawkesbury Agricultural
College, N.S.W., two machines, on the vacuum-
pulsator principle, “have proved an unqualified
success.” Mr. Suter, the college dairy-expert,
reports that after carefully testing the machines
on all points, he finds that the cows take kindly
to them, and there is no injurious effect. “The
maximum results in milk are obtained, and the
machines are cleaner and more expeditious than
hand-milking. They are also a great labor-saver,
as each machine will milk at least ten cows per
hour, and one man can look after three. The
cows do not show the slightest uneasiness under
the operation, which is very gentle, and an exact
imitation of the sucking of a calf.” Good news,
this, for those slaves of the milk-pail, the cookie’s
wife and daughters.
• * * •
Temora (N.S.W.) Independent publishes
some interesting figures to show the value of
manuring in dry seasons. One farmer has 520 acres
under crop, of which 400 acres cut for hay yielded
375 tons. Paddock No. 1 (80 acres), of sandy-grey
loam, was sown by drill with 22lbs. seed (of red
straw wheat), and 501bs. of superphosphates; it
yielded a ton of hay to the acre. No. 2 paddock
(80 acres) was sown by drill with 241bs. seed, with
251bs. superphosphates and an equal quantity of
screened stable manure; it yielded nearly a ton of
hay to the acre. A third paddock (100 acres) of
heavy, gritty, clay soil, treated as the first, gave
equally good results. Crops in the same vicinity
not manured were almost total failures. The
rainfall for the year was B's4in. from January to
Nov. 17.
• * * •
“ Chass. ” : Be manuring orops (8.,
22/11/’O2) :
The advent of drills and the even distribution of fer-
tilisers has practically revolutionised farming. Land in
S.W. Victoria will average two bags of wheat and four
bags of oats more to the acre by using lcwt. of super-
phosphate. Thomas’ phosphate, a by-product of steel-
works (until recently considered as much ip the way as a
gold-mine mullock-dump), ground fine, gives the best all-
round results. The effect of this fertilizer is marvellous ;
a strip left unmanured can be seen the moment you get
a good view of the field. Manured crops grow faster in
the winter, stand dry weather better, ripen fully a fort-
night earlier, and grow from 1£ to 2ft. higher than un-
manured crops on the same land.

23 HUNTER STREET. SYDNEY.
Again the paradoxes of Bananaland. The
S.W. corner of Q., usually regarded as the most
hopelessly barren and drought-stricken corner of
the continent, has had several excellent rains in
the last few months, and on some stations
(particularly Kidman’s properties) stock are
thriving. This way, messieurs the pastoralists—
relief country on the Cooper !
* * • *
About rain fell last week near Cun-
namulla, and small boys ran out, caught the
wonderful stuff in a bottle, corked it up, and took
it to the old folks, who put a date on it and
preserved it as a curio. In one paddock, the bones
of hundreds of cattle are just now an inch or two
under water. Nature’s little ironies !

‘The late H. J. M'Cooey told this yarn : At
Wilson's Creek, nearMullumbimby(N.S.W.) there

myself among then!) have been violently struck

who have repeatedly witnessed these myste-
rious happenings, but will not publicly say
an> t img about the matter for fear of being
ridiculed. Ihe owner—a German—lias been
obliged to abandon the property, and can’t
i f > though it cannot be worth less than
*2IOO. After his wife had died of shock
(owing to the visitation of the ghost) while
1 eing c< nveyed to Lismore Hospital, the

* * * *
Salisbury, a village near Adelaide, has an
astonishing strip of land in an old river-bed (now
nearly dry) that grows unsurpassable oranges.
There are but 150 acres altogether of this alluvial
soil, a bit of which has just changed hands at
about £230 an acre.
* * * *
H. : Nothing unusual in crows
following a traveller. Have had a couple of hun-
dred of the black devils almost within arm’s-
length when bathing in Cootamundra district.
Also, crows know well when a man is sick. Have
known them in Bogan district follow for miles an
invalid who had just left Warren hospital. The
crow is the craftiest of birds, and all other birds
know it and dread him accordingly.
* * * V *
“ W.E.H.” : On 12th Nov., in N.E. Vic.,
we were clearing a paddock, when a dust-storm
came up, so thick that you couldn’t see an object
50yds. away, and suddenly all our fires turned
white, burning exactly like the electric light,
while everything 10 or 20yds. off became magnified.
Jencing-wire appeared inches thick, and almost
microscopic insects loomed as large as blow-flies.
This stated of things lasted about an hour (4 to 5
p.m.); then a thunderstorm settled things down
again.

“Walnic'’: The much- discussed bunyip
is probably a survivor from a past geological age,
just as the platypus and echidna. In the rocks
about Sydney are found the fossil remains of a
labyrinthodont, which was probably exterminated
by the submergence of the country beneath the
sea or* by the disturbances accompanying the up-
heaval of the mountains ; but much of the country
west of the Divide is much older than the coast,
and it is quite possible that some of these crea-
tures survived in the Western swamps and rivers,
and are only now dying out, owing to the country
becoming drier as the hills decrease inheight. Their
aquatic and (probably) nocturnal habits would ac-
count for their being so seldom seen.
* * * *
E. S. S« >rensen, in the Commonwealth
Annual, relates how an ingenious female caught
snakes by erecting a 3ft. square wire-netting en-
closure and setting a saucer of milk inside. The
reptile would crawl through far enough to be able
to drink the milk, and when turning to withdraw
would invariably poke its head through a different
mesh each time until it rove itself into such a
tangle that there was no hope, of escape. It seems
to have been reserved for an Australian woman
to get home after all on the enemy of Eve.
* , * * *
“Walnic”: Probable that some stations
out Cobar (N.S.W.) way will go back to cattle.
“ Workers ” out there are kept in good condition
on scrub, and scrub that sheep won't, as a rule,
eat. But Meryula, which has plenty of mulga,
has 30,000 sheep in good condition, and not a
blade of grass. Mulga is worth cultivating.

In the early sixties a mob of cows from
Twofold Bay were landed on Kapiti Island, five
mile 3 from the mainland of Maoriland. A bu”
was bought from a settlement on the coast opp<
site the island, and was swum across the strap
between two canoes. Next morning the man
from whom lie had been b. ught found him outs: Jo
the cattle-yard once more. He had swum back the
five miles during the night.
“Kanuck”: Re flesh-eating ibis (B.
30/10/’O2). I have often seen black ibis feeding
on dried blood at Meteor Downs station (C. Q.),
when there was no drought and no lack of other
food.
• * * *
Luck 1 At a quarry at Thornleigh (N.S. W.)
recently, a horse, with a tip-dray, backed over an
almost perpendicular embankment, 200 ft. deep,
but yet escaped all injury, the only damage done
being a broken dray-shaft! The horse was found
rlying on his back on a portion of the dray, and,
when released, proceeded philosophically to eat
his dinner.

“ Jimmy S.” : I know an old miner who
was born (in the days of woman colliers) in a
Scottish coal-mine. Lying on a heap of small
coal, wrapped in various pit clothes, the light of a
pit-lamp dazzled his eyes before he saw daylight.
How was that for “to the manner born” ?

Running-in the last beast.
‘ Giro”: Those up-country N.S.W.
children who went on fishing after their elder
sister had drowned (B. 22/11/’O2) must take rank
for casualness behind a couple of Melbourne
youngsters. One night some years ago a Colling-
wood milkman entered the house where his wife
lived apart from him and chopped her, his
mother-in-law, and an elderly boarder to pieces.
His son, aged 10, witnessed the massacre, and,
being satisfied that his father meditated no
violence towards him, went back to bed and slept
until the usual hour. A few months later the son
of a solicitor’s wife at Hawthorn—a youngster of
about the same age—went to the house of a neigh-
bor_ and asked for lunch. When the boy came
again in the afternoon and asked for tea, inquiries
were made. “ Well,” explained the child, “when
I came home at lunch-time I found muvver had
cut her froat.”
* * * *
Specimens of an extinct species of huge
beetle, recently found in Stewart Island (M.L.)
are now in Christchurch Museum. The thorax is
missing in each case, but the head, ljin. broad,
the horns lfiu. long, the wing cases and the two
forelegs indicate the size. The curator of the
museum estimates that the live beetle measured
sin. from horn-tip to tail-tip. The existence of
such a giant coleopter is taken to indicate former
tropical climatic conditions in Maoriland.
* » * *
“ W.M.8.” : Some foolish people are
advocating the introduction of prickly-pear into
Vic.—heedless of the fact that in S. and S.W.
Queensland this plant has simply overgrown the
country, and will cost millions to eradicate—if,
indeed, eradication be now possible. The Govern-
ment offers £5OOO for a means to this end. As a
fodder, p.p. is an utter failure, as the absolutely
indigestible fibre forms a ball in the intestines,
and kills the beast. Too much publicity cannot
be given to this fact. What with hares, rabbits,
sparrows, drought, and rust, Vic. has surely
enough curses on hand already.

“ Wyom” : A few years ago at “The Lime-
kilns,” 30 miles from Cooma, N.S.W., I saw the
body (or rather skeleton and hide) of a brumby in
a reared-up position, held so by one forefoot caught
in a tree-fork. It is said that the animal was a
stallion which, in fighting with another, hacl
swung round and got his hoof thus caught.

Sin Thickhead: Interest on our foreign debt has to
be paid in gold coin in London. The Treasuries here
have to export the gold to pay it (unless they pay out of
new loans raised in London). The sovereigns they send
are included in our exports. Therefore, unless our
exports exceed our imports by an amount sufficient to
cover interest on our foreign debt (public and private) we
are not paying that interest honestly out of our earnings..

‘7,0.” SUPPLIES still another version of the
Mallee story:—
As showing that the Vic. Mallee is worth saving, and
in contrast to the drought-ruined farmer, Melb. Argus
gives particulars of a few Mallee men who have done well
-exceedingly well. Barber, of Birchip, one of four
brothers, started 23 years ago with very little money and
a tew poor teams among them. They now have 10,000
acres, much of it freehold ; crop SCOO acres annually, all
cleared fenced, and tanked, with good homesteads. All
tnm is tree of debt, and has been done with the returns
land—on an average rainfall for the last 16 years
or i4T6in. I Last year Barber sold £BBO of wheat, kept
back seed for 1000 acres, and had 300 bags of oats and.6o
tons of hay in addition. An even stronger instance
°f I, 0 Possibilities of the district is afforded by Ferguson,
of Beidah, who came from S.A. in ’B9. He holds 5000
act es, and, by fallowing and manuring, gets good returns
in ordinary seasons. In ’96 he had a complete failure,
lie spent that year £3OO in horse feed, kept on fallowing,
and planned for next year. It was also a failure, as he
only got back 225 of the 250 bags of seed wheat, and he
had to spend another £325 in horse feed. In ’9B, how-
ever, he reaped 3000 bags of wheat, and got back all his
previous outlay and a profit besides. These are, possibly,
exceptions. Mostly the Mallee farmer drives a five
or six furrow plough, or, in alternate years, a
disc over his land, sowing at the same time and
dragging a heavy log behind—the whole in one opera-
tion ; then he harvests with the stripper (at 25.6 d. to 3s.
per acre), and his whole outlay runs to ss. or 6s. per acre,
so that, at present prices, a yield of half-a-bag pays. But
tli e farmer in the Mallee (like the men mentioned above)
muL ; h (iVUI '- v y ear > and drills his crop in with a
seea-drill, putting in superphosphates also. The Mallee
soil is light, rich in lime and humus, and uses artificial
manures efficiently ; and this year, with a less-than-sin.
rainfall, men who have farmed their land thus are getting
vvw fu Tr 'n yit i l l^with wheat at ss. to 6s. a bushel,
vv nat L he Mallee farmer wants more than an irrigation-
scheme is (1) enough water for stock and household :

tarimng , (4; preservation of shelter-strips. If the land,

A WONDERFUL VISIT.
“Excitement has been caused among the farmers
residing on the banks of the batrobe River, Victoria, by
the appearance of a strange animal. Several of those
who have seen it describe the beast as being about 3ft. in
height, black hair, short neck, long tail. It travels along
panting, with its tongue out, nearly touching the ground.
Several shots have been fired at it, but none have
apparently taken effect.”—DAILY PAPER.
farmers were to get seed-wheat for 150 acres, and
were also to have their credit strengthened by the
extension of their cheap leases for five years,
indignation meetings were promptly held, and
individuals complained bitterly that they wanted
to sow GOO and 800 acres, and the State was
using them shamefully in refusing to lend them
enough to buy seed for the lot. Then the icy
Premier Irvine thawed a little, and said the Govt,
hadn’t fixed an absolute maximum of money to be
advanced, and would probably consider tl e matter
further later on, for the benefit of the extreme
pauper who is able in ordinary seasons to cultivate
800 acres of wheat. The mere fact that Premier
Irvine, Taverner, and one or two of the other
Ministers, are mallee representatives, of course
has nothing to do with the policy of robbing the
Public Service Peter and the charities vote to pay
the eternally-snivelling Paul of the North-west,
and to carry out huge irrigation schemes for the
benefit of that agricultural ingrate.

Redfern suburban council (Sydney) has
done something sensible—sunk a well in its own
park, where it has secured a good water supply.
This makes it in a way independent of the
Prospect supply for street-watering and so on.

Allport and Potter, Sydney. Sole proprietor, W.

A shocking accident lately befel a young
girl at Glen Innes (N.S.W.). While in the dairy
getting milk, her hair became entangled in the
separator, and the whole scalp was torn off in a
couple of seconds. After being seen by local
medicos, she was trained to Sydney for treatment.
