The Imperial Obsession.
Writes “ M.”
A more-or-less prolonged condition of obsession is not
uncommon in the records of British statesmanship. Pitt
was under obsession of a ghastly sort when he bled Eng-
land white, and loaded her with debt in an effort to force
back on France the Bourbon, the priest, and the renegade
aristocrat. Gladstone and Disraeli were under obsession
of Cobdenism when, during the U.S. civil-war, they could
neither see nor feel the cosmic foroe of human right
beneath the Northern arms. And Chamberlain and
Balfour and Rosebery, with all other natural leaders of
the people, are in such obsession to-day as render
them blind to right and wrong, to justice and injustice ;
to put all reasonable pleas out of their mouths in any and
every cause i and force them back on loyalty, a thing of
their own invention, whenever action is questioned, or
proposal challenged. They dare not let any item of their
programme stand on its merits. The Education
Bill, which surely might be argued apart from
any question of the merits or demerits of its
authors, is thrust before Parliament, arbitrarily as
Meg Mkph.it,t.bb’ porridge-ladle before domine Sampson.
“ Gape, sinner, and swallow,” not because it is good, or
necessary for you, but because its rejection will involve
“our” condemnation. “Our” condition of obsession
renders ourselves and our acts taboo. Touch the Com-
mons (Doctors’ commons), says Proctor Spenlow, “ and
down comes the country.” Touch a Government
measure and down comes Brummagem Joeism and
Oecildom, which is about the same thing. So Parliament
believes, so the unled “ Liberalism” of England believes.
The obsession overshadows, if it does not dominate,
them all. The Education Bi'l does not matter
much. It is one of the familiar Tory self-denying
ordinances which, though worthless in themselves,
serve often as stepping-stones to ultimate achieve-
ment ; but when the same principle is dominant
throughout the policy of the time, there is need for
those who are awake and unbewitched to make a noise
and show a light. Obsession is the horrible antithesis of
inspiration. The obsessed is as the hypnotised. Is not
the obsession of the “ Empire ” in S’Africa new as
apparent as the results of any hypnotic suggestion ? Is
right or wrong more seriously considered now than in
the inception of the war? On the other hand, is any of
the thoroughness which has characterised world’s con-
querors discernible ? The war was fought that the Rand
might be worked according to the notions of British and
Semitic capitalists. The Rand, freed from Boer control,
is spoil of war. The Rand is estimated by some
experts to be capable of yielding £25,000,000 worth
of gold per annum for an incalculable period. But the
obsession of the Empire rulers renders the Rand taboo.
What 1 Stretch forth a hand against Cohen and Levy,
De Beers and Robinson, or Barnato or Rhodes’ execu-
tors? Obsession forbids. They are Imperial. Touch
them, and must not the whole Imperial fabric
tremble? Yet one-fourth of the estimated Rand
revenue would pay interest on the cost of the
war and provide a sinking-fund to clear the total
within 50 years. The unprejudiced view of the un-
hampered statesman, or the resolute will of the fearless
conqueror would instantly recognise and grasp the
opportunity ; but imperialism obsessed turns to the land,
as did “ mediaeval chivalry,” in any and every strait. The
land, and the people on the land must some day and
somehow pay up the cost. Beyond that it is impossible
to go. It is like enough to breed obstruction chill as
death, to surrender S’Africa once more to the Africander
Bond and arrest all the desire of colonization which had
been felt amongst the enterprising peoples of the world.
Apart from the Rand there is not one note of legitimate
progress heard, from Capetown to Buluwayo. Nor in
Britain is one voice of the old Liberalism raised in protest.
The nation is obsessed of a jingoism that has created an
idol of taboo, and declared the sin of heresy to be as the
sin of rebellion. Is not that condition reflected in Aus-
tmlia ? Are we not also, to some extent, obsessed of a
spurious Imperialism which demoralises, degrades, lets,
binders, be-devils us? Is there not throughout our
administrative and executive life a pitiful lack of com-
prehensive grasp and direct purpose, which savors of
spoiled statesmanship, of obsessed faculty? Australia
never asked more of Australians than now, and never
promised more for loyal and heart-whole service. But,
owing to the partial, the reflected obsession she asks
and promises in vain. When the true liberalism, the ele-
ments which should constitute the the pre-
sent, will awake, it is indeed difficult to say. One clear call
from one true leading voice would suffice; but no call
comes. Liberalism is leaderless, democracy anaemic, and
there is no present sign of deliverance on the horizon.
Humbug reigns, or rules behind the reigning power. The
Empire, puerile in the backwaters of her loudly-vaunted
triumphs, invites the contempt of mankind, and the
colonies bid for a share of the same contempt, though in
a callow and babyish style.
4
“F.”: Chief Justice Madden, summing up a
case of alleged “ impropriety ” —the sort of impro-
priety which ever moves the Waxellent person to
eloquence—told the jury to.remember that ‘‘the
law was for the protection of young girls who
yielded too readily. This girl had shown herself
to be immodest in a marked degree; she rejoiced
in sensuality, and was untruthful. The law must
be given effect to if a jury were satisfied that an
assault had been committed; even if the girl
forced it they must find the man guilty.” Yet,
there are women (of the aggressively virtuous
order, as a rule) who continue to clamor for
Rights, and demand to be put upin an equality
with man, the oppressor. Do they propose to
exchange old rights for new, or do they hope to
run legal equality in double harness with their
legal in-equality ? Lovely woman doesn’t help to
keep law’norder on sea or land as a sailor, a
soldier, a police constable, or a chucker-out;
nevertheless she considers herself entitled to vote
shoulder to shoulder with the man whom she can
charge with a criminal offence if he fails to resist
her amorous advances. Surely the enfranchised
female will relinquish her right to wreak legal
vengeance on the victim she has seduced with
violence. But, seeing that her intentions are
doubtful, it would be well to amend the law
relating to assault before the Suffrage Bill is
passed. Let us, O, let us have equality in assaults.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Claimed by N.S. W. Labor leader
M‘Gowan that Reid was ejected from office by
his party simply because he refused to introduce
an Early Closing Bill.
Thomas Bent’s new railway “ inspectors ”
(spies the employees call them), whose duty it is
to watch the staff, are all constituents of Brighton.
Their previous occupations were hotel-keeper,
book-maker, market-gardener, bricklayer, wood
merchant and laborer.
If Barton resigns the Premiership for the
Federal High Court, the more democratic of the
Cabinet’s followers would plump for Kingston as
his successor. He has the reputation, with the
Labor party especially, of being the strongest and
most sincere member of the Ministry.
The Jenkins Goverment in S.A. is finding
relief work (stone-breaking for rations only) for
the unemployed, who ivere by now to have been
all drawing good wages out of the Snow tram-
grab. To rub it in further, the rations supplied
in some cases seem to be unfit for use.
The Federal Govt, has definitely decided
to drop, in the new Defence Bill, those clauses
rendering the permanent forces liable to be called
upon for service outside the Commonwealth. It
was a matter of the clauses going out or the
Government going out, and the clauses went.
Last year N.S.W. State Labor Bureau
found work for 5389 unemployed—4sl2 got
Government work, mostly out of loan money, and
private employment was found for 877. The
proportions show a lamentable superabundance of
loan money and very little self-supporting em-
ployment.
The thin end of the wedge ! At the
Commonwealth Trades Union conference in
Sydney last week, in which Australian and State
M’s.P. formed a big proportion, it was moved and
unanimously carried “That this congress is of
opinion that the office of State Governor should
he abolished.”
THE CHURCH AS INFIDEL-MAKER.
THE CHURCHES AND LABOR. -Discussion AT
MELBOURNE TRADES HALL.
“Mr. Elliott protested against such a disintegrating
subject being introduced. The churches had no sym-
pathy with labor; their sympathies' were with the
enemies of labor.”— NEWS REPORT.
“ Don’t you think you could induce your husband
to attend church, my good woman?”
“ No.”
“Why not?—he believes in God I suppose?”
“No! that he doesn’t —says the Almighty's a hope-
less conservative.”
Premier John See deftly scratched the
back of N.S.W. and Australia generally, last week,
in a speech wherein he stated that the country,
despite its present bad condition, would pull
through triumphantly by reason of its immense
resources and the indomitable pluck and self-
reliance of its people. Their indomitable 1.0. U.
and reliance on the English money-lender was
doubtless what John really meant.
Stated that Premier Barton and Home Sec.
Lyne asked for a special train to take them from
the recent Maitland meeting and were refused
unless they paid for it. The alternative was to
hook their sleeping-car on to a slow goods-train.
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners acted quite
rightly ; there would have been no justification in
wasting £5O on a special to give a little extra
comfort t<> two politicians who look after them-
selves so well.
If the Irvine Government has half the
nous it claims, and really wants to do the farmers
a good turn, it will secretly and swiftly buy up
enough Victorian seed wheat to supply at least
half the State. If it doesn’t do this the farmers
will presently be forced either to use non-acelima-
tised, imported wheat, or mortgage their souls to
bulling speculators.
The Queensland sugar-industry returns
make interesting reading taken in conjunction
with the wails about the destruction of the
plantations which was to follow the disuse of
Kanaka labor. Up to Oct. 31 82,091 tons of
cane-sugar had been grown in the State by white
labor ; while, of the £18,918 granted for rebate,
no less than £15,000 had been paid to N.Q.
planters.
Defence Minister Forrest—after threaten-
ing the scalp of the Adelaide militia lieutenant,
who, at a meeting to form an Australian I,aval
League, characterised Admiral Beaumont’sgeneral
belittling of an Australian navy as “ utterly flip-
pant and wrong”—found that a man who joins
the unpaid volunteer forces for his country’s
defence doesn’t forego his right to individual
opinion and free speech. And Bigjohn promptly
backed down. There’s some reasonableness in
Bigjohn after all.
A discovery made by Andrews, the Vic-
torian Government’s most caustic critic, badly hit
Irvine on the raw. Andrews went back to the
time of Irvine’s first election when he found that
he stood against and beat a Patterson reform
candidate. Patterson’s reform was on exactly the
same lines as Irvine’s present proposals for saving
the blessed country. But in those days Irvine was
a, land-taxer who believed in getting the money
required to keep the State affairs in a decent way
by a scientific impost on land values.
The new N.S.W. Liquor Bill proposes to
retain the present licensing benches —usually com-
posed in the country of the local P.M. and the
two J’s.P. who have the least to do or the biggest
hunger for places where they can either exercise
power or pretend to. The lay-member is not
paid ; and, though no doubt he often does his
duty, and stands the loss that results, the chances
are that he isn’t always built that way. And
even if he always was built that way, it would still
be a mean thing for the public to ask men to do
that sort of work out of their own pockets, so to
speak.
The Westralian Assembly decided, by 20
votes against 14, not to reduce the salary of the
State Governor from £4OOO to £3OOO, because, as
Premier James put it, the reduction “was calcu-
lated to minimise the importance of the State and
to threaten State rights.” Query : How does the
importance of the Canadian province of Quebec
get on—Quebec which has 10 times Westralia’s
population, a Governor with £2OOO a year salary,
and only one House of Parliament ? The import-
ance of a State doesn’t depend on paying the
biggest gubernatorial salary, or having the biggest
Parliament House, or the biggest debt—or even
the biggest garbage-tip.
The S.A. Attorney-General thinks he has
found a hole in the Australian constitution by
which the State can claim to fix the polling places
at Senatorial elections, and with that small mean
desire to be nasty in a small mean way towards
their own Australian Parliament, which charac-
terises so many State functionaries, the Jenkins
Ministry is working up a disturbance over the
matter. Logically, on the same ground every
State constituency should fix its own polling
places at State elections. The cases are exactly
parallel, for at Senatorial elections S.A. is simply
a constituency, and Jenkins and Gordon are, by
comparison, about equivalent to a mayor and an
inspector of nuisances.
What may happen to a man who professes
Liberal views and supports a Tory Ministry was
well illustrated in Vic. Assembly last week.
W. A. Watt, who since his defeat for N. Melbourne
by Laborite Prendergast, has been all shades of
political opinion, and who managed to get back
into Parliament under the wing of the Irvine
Government, was forced to tell the House that he
intended to vote for the Ministerial proposal to
give the public servants separate representation
and to speak against said proposal. With A.N. A.
fluency. Watt remarked that such a position was
“perfectly defensible, altho’ somewhat para-
doxical.” “In what a labyrinth of nonsense,”
writes Junius, “does a man involve himself who
labors to maintain falsehood by argument.”
The weak “Lord Mayor” Gillott is the
melancholy jade of Vic. politics just now. Since
his lordship’s puerile desertion of Peacock he has
been cold-shouldered by his former colleagues,
and hasn’t succeeded in ingratiating himself with
Ministerialists, who can stand an ordinary turn-
coat, but not the kind who goes back without
warning on a colleague who had done so much for
him as Peacock did for Gillott. His lordship has
wilted away into the furthest seat in the Govt,
corner, where he sits with his invariable plug hat
down over his eyes, and nobody notices him.
Although so recently Attorney-General, he now
has no more political significance than a house-fly,
and if he wandered out of Parliament and never
came back, it is doubtful whether anyone would
be the wiser.
This is a cold country for a Ministry which
tries to make ends meet, and Tasmania is by no
means the hottest part of it. This, for instance,
is the moan of about the most influential daily in
Raspberry-land re the new income-tax :
What would be thought of a British Cabinet which, in
its last stages, and with a narrow majority, attempted to
impose new taxation to the extent of £20,000,000 on the
taxpayers of the United Kingdom ? Yet the two amounts
are proportional.
And the British Cabinet, quite lately, put on
£31,000,000 a year of new taxation —this on top of
much heavier previous taxation than Tasmania
paid—and what was said of it was mostly
“ Hooray ! ” The kind of Government they would
soeak badly of in Britain is the one which had a
£20,000,000 deficit in a time of peace, not one that
put on £20,000,000 to cover the deficit. Whatever
bad qualities Bull may have, he isn’t always
weeping about being expected to pay his way.
THE DROUGHT FIEND TAKES UP A COLLECTION.
Distribution of seed wheat in the Mallee and extension of lease to settlers;
being an honest attempt in the hot weather to illustrate current events.
Cigar importers in Australia still hold to
a hope of the modification of the Protection given
to local industry in the tobacco trade, and intend
a big lobbying campaign “ when the Federal Par-
liament is moved from Melbourne.” It is thought
that it will be easier to “influence” members
when they are got away from a big city.
Two months ago, Q. ex-Speaker Cowley
was severely condemned by the Philpocrats for
having suggested, as a remedy for the financial
trouble: “Sell the land; sell the railways;
abolish free education ! ” The first is now being
done as far as possible ; the second is proposed,
and the third is not far in the distance.
Previous Q. land Acts have contained
ample provisions against dummying and aggrega-
tion. The Administration has neglected to
enforce them. Noio the Administration argues
the said provisions were a dead letter—hence
useless. And now dummying is to be legalised,
aggregation to be legalised, and monopoly exalted
into a system.
The London financial institutions in-
terested in the passage of the present Queens-
land Land Bill have magnificent nerve. The
other day they wired out to the Brisbane
Press that they considered the measure inade-
quate, and that they were seriously annoyed at the
opposition offered to the Bill by the Q. Labor
party. Not cohtent with wagging the Philpocracy
they want to stifle the Opposition also.
The new Q. Land Bill, which contains
material for three months’ fair discussion, has
been brought in at the fag-end of a session, and
the Philp Govt, has made an attempt to rush it
through in a week’s time. A stonewall of some
24 hours, however, brought Philp and co- to
reason —and this scandalous measure may now
get some semblance of deliberate consideration.
“ H.L.” writes ;
Re that Queensland secession movement. Philp never
made a bigger mistake in all his existence if he thinks
that he could get a majority vote for disunion at a
referendum. Writer personally knows three, and has
heard of many more democrats who, though plainly
seeing the good points of the measure, voted against
Federation, solely because the Philp crowd were barrack-
ing for it. They argued that the traitorous, axe-grinding
crowd which had brought this State to its then level
would never support the Bill unless they saw something
in it to their own advantage and the undoing of De-
mocracy. Now the anti-piebald Bills have been passed,
likewise the Adult Suffrage Bill, and there is joy in the
tent of the Democrat. And the sight of the angry,
powerless Philp stuttering out his wrath against the
Australian Government gives the said tent-dwellers a
gratification which almost surpasseth understanding. By
all means let Philp have his referendum.
. Thus C. de Thierry in the Empire
Review :
Colonials are Protectionists by reason of necessity. . . .
The colonies care nothing for the abstract virtues of
Protectionism or Freetrade. What they do find is that
Protection suits them and Freetrade does not. . . . Not
only is it necessary as a means of revenue, but as a means
of defending themselves in the commei'cial war carried on
against the British Empire by foreign count ries. Finally,
they have found it answer well in developing home indus-
tries. Colonials being hard-headed men, naturally pay
more honor to experience than they do to Cobden Club
pamphlets. National politics may be, and are, based on
sentiment, but they are always carried out upon prac-
tical lines.
Which statement just about sums up the fiscal
situation, and explains why the policy of the
M‘Millan-Reid-Hirsch crowd has become such an
object of derision in Australia.
The Seddon Government came out on top
at the Maoriland General Election with a strong
majority of at least 28. The Local Option poll,
taken at the same time, would have come very
near to abolishing the drink trade in Maoriland
but for the fact that it requires a three-fifths
majority to carry Prohibition ; if a bare majority
had been sufficient, the great experiment would
have come off over a very large section of the
country. The Government party is divided
between the followers of Premier Seddon, who
has leanings towards a policy of economy, and the
believers in Treasurer Joseph Ward, whose idea
is to borrow all the money that anybody will lend,
and have a great debauch, and let the future take
care of itself. And the razzling and dazzling of
Joseph during Seddon’s absence has set the
country’s feet on a path that is not easily retraced.
The decision of the N.S.W. Parliamentary
Conference that public works should be inau-
gurated with extra energy, to provide relief for
drought - stricken agriculturists, has started
Minister Owe’Sullivan on a whirl of new loan-
money expenditure. Four new railways—Cobar
to Gehenna, Temora to Sheol, Bogan Gate to
Tophet, and Manilla to the Bottomless Pit —are
to be started immediately, and water conservation
works, roads, bridges, artesian-well sinking, the
snagging of rivers, and the wholesale repair of
public buildings are to be commenced. The very
day after the conference recommended loans and
public works as the usual remedy for distress, the
Owe’Sullivan was ordering railways and such-like
wholesale. The Owe’Sullivan’s idea goes into
two propositions : In good times, borrow lots of
money because the country can afford to pay the
interest; in bad times, borrow lots of money
because the country must have relief at any
hazard. Always borrow lots of money.

wealth Constitu-

George Reid finds a scrap of consolation :
England’s real trials would begin when the United
States threw aside protection. Then would she have to
look out for her industrial supremacy. It was well for
her that that giant of enterprise, America, was kept in
the menagerie of protection.
Query : Why didn’t England’s trials begin
years ago when America practised Freetrade?
And seeing that the U.S., with If times Bri-
tain’s population, already manufactures times
as muoh as Britain does—s ay £1,200,000,000 a
year more—has Freetrade Brit ain any “ industrial
supremacy ” left to look after? Even in its trea-
sured cotton, woollen, and linen industries the
“ mother country ” has now dropped into the rear
and is toiling along in the dust-cl oud at the back
of the waggon.
“ An Ex-Teacher ” writes :
Is there any other N.S.W. Public Service department
wherein an officer becomes such a mere creature of cir-
cumstance as regards his financial condition as that of
Education? A teacher in any school below Class IV.
never really knows, under the present back-and-forth
system, what his salary may be in three months’ time. I
know a teacher up North—a 3B man—who, in ’99, was in
charge of two half-time schools. The aggregate atten-
dance when he took charge was about 25, and he was
paid 9th class salary accordingly. Being young and
ardent, he soon had the aggregate up to 40, his salary
rising to Bth class, or £24 better. Things remained thus
for 12 months, and then the bigger of his two schools was
declared a 9th class public, or “ full-time ” ; whereupon
the teacher, though he had achieved almost unique
reports, was ordered to take charge of the one school,
and his salary was lowered to 9th again (£24 less)- and
all this for excess of zeal. The officer’s classification
(3B) fully entitled him to the Bth class pay. lie was,
moreover, a married man.
George Reid says that if his party can
only win four Victorian seats at next elections for
the Australian Parliament “Freetrade will be
assured.” And if Reid’s party will only practise
Freetrade, The Bulletin almost hopes it may win
these seats. A year,, or two years, of genuine
Freetrade would end the Freetrade party in
Australia for a century. Hitherto it has called
itself a Freetrade party, which was a lie ; it has
said it was practising Freetrade, which was another
lie ; it has protected a large number of industries,
and assured the people that the benefits thereby
arising were the blessed results of Freetrade,
which was a third lie; and it has assured the
public that it believed Freetrade to be the best,
policy for the country, which was a fourth lie—•
unless it really believed what it said and was wil-
fully injuring the country by practising partial
Protection. And all the days of its life it has
been a coward and a liar —or else a traitor.
Extract from speech by N.S.W. Opposi-
tion-leader Carruthers :
In duties alone, N.S.W. would have to pay £250,000 in
one year on breadstuffs. A quarter of a million is taken,
in the name of Federation from the pockets of the people
who were practically in a state of famine ! The test
whether it was advisable to remove the duties was to.
suppose that the Government, with wheat at ss. a bushel,
and bread at 3|d. a loaf, and meat at famine prices, were
to come down with a proposal to impose duties on bread-
stuffs to the extent of £250,000 a year.
Also, in duties on breadstuffs alone N.S.W. will
receive £250,000 a year in the name of Federation,
and with all these beggared farmers (who don’t
want the fodder duties taken off, because they
have no animals left to feed) crying for costly
relief works, who but a fool would throw away
£250,000 ? As for bread being at the famine price
of 3Jd. a loaf, it was 3Jd. when Parkes, M‘Millan
and Carruthers were running the show on Free-
trade lines —it generally is 3Jd., or more, in Syd-
ney, and only in 17 years out of the last 81 has
it averaged less than that figure. It has been llid.
per 21b. loaf under Freetrade for that matter.
The organ of the Sydney kitchen wenches 1
plunges into politics are indeed amusing. It is.
trying to influence the Balmain election by shriek-
ing “sanctity of trial by jury,” which, it declares,
was endangered by the action of the Labor party-
(assisted, by the way, by Mr. Law) in the Fried-
man case. Trial by jury is designed to prevent
innocent men being gaoled by tyrants, not to see
that men about whose guilt there is doubt are
shut up. When trial by jury fails to safeguard
the liberty of the subject, it is time for it to be.
abolished. There is nothing sacred about the
system itself. The Sunday Times quotes some
barrister who declares that the Judge must abide
by the jury’s verdict : that barrister is as wise
as the Times. The Judge has the power at any
stage of the case to withdraw it from the jury
altogether and order an acquittal, and in some
cases he can direct the jury to find a verdict of
not guilty. He can do any of these things to,
liberate a man, but none of them to convict him ;
and is only bound by the jury’s verdict when it
is one of innocence. Juries are to see that a man
gets the benefit of all the doubts, not to ensure
convictions in spite of the Judge and the Crown.

Dear Sir—l consider it my duty to inform you of the
marvellous cure by WEBBER’S VITADATIO of my
daughter, aged two years and nine months, from a com-
plication of diseases. Twelve months ago she became
very ill, suffering from diseased Lungs, Peritonitis,
Marasmus, Measles, and Abscess in the right eye, which
reduced her to a shadow, the local doctor in attendance
stating he could do no more for her, and thought it was
impossible for her to recover. At this time I was strongly
advised to give her VITADATIO, arid as a last resource I
did so. lam happy to say that she soon began to im-
prove and rapidly regained her health, and has remained
so ever since, thanks to VITADATIO, and in my opinion
it has been the means of restoring her to health. I am
well-known in Elsternwick, having resided here 12 years,
and all my acquaintances know of my child’s illness and
cure, and can testify to the truth of my statement.
I can confidently recommend it to others suffering from
any kind of disease after the cure of my child. As I am
removing to Perth, W.A., my address is—O/o Veale and
Co., Newcastle-street, Perth.

W. H. ORANSWICK.

The “Citizens’ Reform League,” which has
(in the newspaper columns) been taking such a big
part in the Sydney municipal elections, is a
gorgeous joke. Its creation is mostly due to a
smart D.T. reporter, and its meetings—well, has
it had one genuine meeting this year ?
Though, according to the official reports,
the last layer of water in Broken Hill reservoir
will not be dug out until the end of Feb., already,
by the end of Nov., it demands a strong constitu-
tion to take a shower-bath, let alone a drink, of
the highly-perfumed stuff. There is a bit of a
hill over which the street trams run; yet so
difficult is it to turn the alleged water into steam,
that the pull sticks up two motors with a load of
only two cars. And to give a good drink of it to
the poor wilted things that up there pass for
flowers is to condemn them to a rapid death.
Dear Bulletin. —Your par. re Kenniffs
(8., 11/22/’O2) is wrong. “ Kenniff, sen.” was not
on trial. [There were two brothers: one was
senior.— Ed. B.] Consequently, he did not “pro-
test his innocence,” nor did he “ curse the judge
with the bluest curse on record.” Neither did
anyone else. Both accused averred their inno-
cence, after the jury’s verdict of guilty was
recorded. Jim Kenniff, the younger of the twain,
said that the real murderer was a pastoral
magnate. It was this allegation, made from the
dock, and before sentence of death was passed,
that Chief Justice Griffith requested, not ordered,
the Press to refrain from publishing.—Yours,
Chee.

A few weeks ago, Pierpont Morgan
loomed across the world as the man who had
brought about the decline and fall of British
maritime supremacy. To-day he is comparatively
small potatoes, and is a cheerful example of the
powerlessness of Private Enterprise when it runs
up against the State. By lending a little
assistance to the Cunard co. the British Govern-
ment has just about pulverised the Morgan com-
bine, and that gigantic; corporation at once made
haste to come to terms. In all probability the
combine will go to bits. It bought up existing
lines at a tremendous capitalisation, and by virtue
of its monopoly, and its reduction of expenses,
and having only one ship leaving England and the
11. S. A. every day, all sorts of channels of waste
of energy were to have been closed up. It may
still achieve the saving, but it has not secured the
monopoly, and the business has been so over-
capitalised that nothing but an absolute monopoly
could possibly make it pay. Morgan will go
down in history as another of the splendid
failures around'which hang almost as much glory
as if they had been successes.
“Malle” writes re the waste of public
funds (mostly borrowed from Bull), caused by the
various N.S.W. Govt, departments working dead
against each other:
Most departmental heads fancy themselves tin gods
and the other fellows mere unpleasant circumstances. If
a private person owned, say, a mine and a farm, and the
manager of one would not allow the other the use of any
gear, etc., he was not using himself (thus putting the
owner to a lot of needless expenses), both these swell-
headed persons would soon bo minus a job.
Most of the country along the coast from Long Bay to
La Perouse is, for some unaccountable reason, a military
reserve. The Roads Department opened a fine white-
metal quarry on this area, and now the military authori-
ties, finding a hole ready excavated for them, have
installed an electric plant therein, and refused the
common road-people access. Here is the only decent
metal procurable for miles, and it means a very heavy
extra annual expenditure to buy and cart blue metal
to the locality. Also the Railway Construction Depart-
ment, after much trouble, got leave to remove a few loads
of turf from the said reserve. More was wanted, but,
although no use is being made of the ground, and the
couch-grass would inside 12 months be as thick as ever,
a haughty warrior came along and objected. In cases of
this kind some tribunal should be empowered to direct
that the people’s property can be taken for public pur-
poses when there is a manifest advantage to be gained
thereby, and no toney public servant should be allowed
to run away with the idea that public property, which
happens to be vested in his Department, ceases to be
public property ; nor should any such superiah person be
allowed to override the interests of his employer—the
public. »

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Lady Grafton-First Consul duel, ’tween crack
jocks Barden and Smith at Randwick, Saturday week,
was repeated at Warwick Farm last Saturday, when
former, up on Dady Grafton again, and latter, on Bar
One, had the finish of the big thing to themselves. Smith
just landed the money, but not by quite as narrow a
margin as Barden succeeded with the week before.
Apropos Barden. Figured out, anyone putting £1 on his
first mount at Randwick, t’other day, and “playing it
up” on the other two would have handled £336, but a
fourth investment meant disaster, as he finished third in
the last race.
» * * •
Adelaide Tatt.’s summer meet, Saturday, run
off in wind and dust, was only fairly attended, but
£6550 went through the machine. Depping races were
unlucky for riders and nags. Eight boys were fined 10s.
for being late at the post in hurdles, and £1 each for a
like offence in steeple. First of these events resulted in
horses Conference and Buccleuch being killed, and Harry
having his shoulder put out, while ’chase saw Lightfoot
break his back and rider Joskin’s collarbone. In Young-
ster’s Handicap, Bombala and First Fleece dead-heated.
Bombala’s owner refused to whack the stake, and that
prad won easily, but Bombala’s jock failed to pull the
weight and First Fleece got the money. There’s a moral
in this ; it, to an extent, recalls the Vanity Fair-Eleanor
dead-heat for Williamstown Cup a week or two since.
* * * *
The Abbess, winner of Sandhurst Cup, is half-
sister, by Bill of Portland, to Seclusion, recently pur-
chased by French brandy man Ilennessy, and was bred
by Mr. “Jimmy” Wilson, jun., who, estimating her by
no means a top-notcher, took £lOO for the mare some
time back. Since then she has been on the job con-
sistently. After running second to Rescued at last
Williamstown Cup meet she, next day, closed on Fare-
well Handicap, and, two days further on, Aspendale Park
Handicap. Then she finished second to Sans Peur in
Mentone Handicap. But ’tis told now that The Abbess
should not have won, for jock (F. Dunn) on Godwin is
alleged to have mistaken a starting machine 40 yards
from the judge for the winning post, otherwise, he claims,
the race would have been sure for his mount.
• * * *
The Abbess, who won Sandhurst Cup (a 20 to 1
chance), is seemingly a better mare than the stable
reckoned her, but like many of the Bill of Portland stock,
she is not easily ripened. Burn, who made a good finish
on her, was to have ridden stable-companion Cluster, but
couldn’t get within 41bs. of the latter’s weight.
**■ • *
Winner of big thing (Bendigo Handicap) of
Sandhurst Cup meet, second day, turned up in Rupert
Clarke’s 4yr.-old gelding, Fairy States, by Hova from
Judy Mo. ’Twas Fairy States who recently appropriated
Maribyrnong Cup, beating easily, among others, The
Victory, who seven days later scooped Melbourne Cup.
* * * *
Seaport, by Portsea-Opera, won a handicap
at Caulfield the other Saturday, his first victory since
July, ’99, when he was a promising 2-year-old. Ever since
he was backed for the Australian Cup as a 3-year-old,
little Seaport has been spoken of as likely to win a great
race, but somehow he was never ready when wanted.
Now that they have got him in something like condition,
the 5-year-old may go on and prosper. He looks as good
as his sire, Portsea, who won a memorable champion race.
* * * »
The question of stamina in The Persian’s case
has still to be settled in public. Fielder was confident
about the tall gelding’s ability to go the Melb. Cup
course, but many good judges wouldn’t have him as a
2-miler, even with his lenient weight, 7.8. Melb. Cup
was The Persian’s chance had he been given a fair passage.
If he is to win a long distance race in the future he will
have to carry a lot more than 7.8.
* * * •
Possibilities for coming Yilliers and Carrington
Stakes were seen out at Warwick Farm, Saturday, in
Cecil Rhodes and Pendant, both of whom evidenced
unusual and unexpected galloping ability. Pipe-
opener at Randwick a week before evidently did Pendant
a heap of good, for she absolutely romped away from her
field, giving well-backed first-class up-country performer,
Gmceton, who ran second, absolutely no hope at any
time. Balance of the field was spread-eagled. Pen-
dant was weightily supported in city “shops” and on
the course till it wasn’t possible to back her under 6 to 4
“on.” She is by Far Niente from Necklet, by Musket
from Docket (imp.), dam of Bangle, Chain Shot and
Medallion.
• * „ * _> *
Likelihood of same nag winning Ak’J.C. Yilliers
and Tatts. Carrington Stakes Xmas time, as has occasion-
ally occurred heretofore, is minimised now through first-
named being extended to one mile.
* » * »
A coincidence ! Racehorse Aerolite was killed
by lightning recently atTamworth (N.S.W.)
• « • *
An office-bearer of a Melbourne suburban
Anglican church has been called on to resign because he
drew a horse in ‘ ‘ Tattersall’s.”
* * * *
A claim at Flemington (Vic.) Co'irt lately by a
bookmaker’s clerk for 5 per cent, on his employer’s
“ Carnival ” winnings, as part of his wages, revealed that
said boss —an “ inside ” Ikey—cleared between £3OO and
£4OO during Cup week, ’Ol, and over £4OO this year from
Caulfield Guineas to Williamstown Cup. That is one
Ikey among hundreds. No wonder he combines with the
church to block the clean, honest tote which doesn’t
make a hundredth of such profits, and needs no runners
or tick-tackers, and doesn’t care tuppence whether every
horse in a race is stiff or not.
* * » »
When the weights came out for last Caulfield
and Melb. Cups every sporting writer in Australia gave
Lieut. Bill and Flagship for Caulfield and Victory for
Melb. Cup as being likely winners ; and yet, when the
day for final selection came, Bill was discarded for his
race, and only two papers mentioned The Victory for the
Cup, and the “ mentioning” was somewhat feeble at that.
The stars and the tipsters in their courses fight for the
chosen.
* • * *
The mighty fallen. Great old Splendor, sire of
winners of 400 races and something in excess of £45,000,
has come down to the position of an ordinary country
stallion. Mr. Frank Mack bought him at dispersal of
Tocal stud, and a day or two ago parted with his pur-
chase to a Cobbora (N.S.W.) settler. Splendor is 22
years of age, and by Speculum.
' * * « *
Narrabri (N.S.W.) races, t’other day, discovered
something worth watching. Five-year-old gelding
Weetah, by Mozart out of a Gozo mare, gathered three
handicaps easily (humping 9.7 once), and shaped well
enough to indicate that even in metropolitan company
he’d be worth an investment.
* » * »
M.L. jock., C. Jeukins, was up on 6 winners,
1 second, and 2 thirds in 9 starts at M.L. Cup meeting.
Hewitt got very close to this, scoring 5 firsts, 2 seconds
and 1 third.
* » * *
Unquestionably one of the best mares ever bred
in M.L. was Lady Zetland, who succumbed while foaling
to imported Obligato, t’other day. She was by Perkin
Warbeck 11. out of Forget-Me-Not, and never did much
good till turning 4yrs. ; then she won frequently and
consistently for perhaps five years subsequently. She
finished up sensationally by winning M.D. and Canter-
bury Cups. Dady Dilian is the best of her progeny so far,
but a yearling by Obligato promises handsomely.

Tas. Govt, proposes in its Stamp Duties Bill to
impose a penny tax on every five shillings put into Tatt.’s
sweeps. Probably Tatt. won’t protest seriously ; it will
be but another bulwark to his business.

The Australian cricketers found the S’African
teams quite worthy of their mettle. Regular matches
are talked of, and Major Wardill hints at a S’African visit
to Australia in 1903.
* * * *
N.S.W. cricket fatalities recorded last week :
At Sans Souci (Sydney) a wicket-keeper was struck in the
neck with the ball, and died soon afterwards. Tarana
(N.S.W.) saw Charles Mudge struck on the chest with the
ball and die two hours later. Nobody arrested, no police
court proceedings. How different if a boxer dies as the
result of a fall or lack of.condition 1 The police make
arrests and issue summonses, the unco’ guid elevate their
hands in holy horror, and the press publishes articles
on the “brutality” of the thing, yet only one fatality
occurs in years through boxing.
* * * *
English critics are discussing Saunders. Their
brethren of Australia discussed him similarly before he
went ’Ome. Most Dondon cricket scribes agree that the
Victorian left hander sends down a ball occasionally which,
at least, isn’t exactly what it ought to be. One of them
puts it—“ Saunders has one undoubted ‘ wrong ’un,’ and
one of the wonders of the year has been that not one of
the umpires has called him.” Many capable judges who
have seen Saunders at work will experience little difficulty
in coming to the conclusion that at any rate English
umpires haven’t been harsh.

In second-grade cricket-match, Paddington v.
Central Cumberland, at Parramatta, two batsmen pasted
a bowler unmercifully, getting 1,1, 5,6, 4 off five succes-
sive balls, and drove the sixth straight back, flattening
the bowler out for several seconds as clean as freckle-
fisted Fitzsimmons might have done it with a punch on
the point. Case of adding injury to insult.

“Renard”: At Clermont (Q.), last week, in a
match between two local cricket teams, a bowler named
Kenyon took 5 wickets with 5 consecutive balls. The
last ball of the over just missed the bails, and with the
first ball of his next over he scattered the stumps—s con-
secutive wickets, and 6 wickets in 7 balls. In another
match, played at the Springs (near Clermont), in the 2nd
of the visiting team, the first 3 batsmen were
caught off the firsts balls bowled, and the last 3 balls of
the match each took the middle stump. The innings
thus began and ended with a “ hat-trick.”

“B.B.”: There is a bitter feeling in inner
N.S.W. cricket circles that Victoria will probably have a
majority of reps, in the proposed Rest of Australia v.
Australian Eleven fixture. The exceptional form shown
by Graham, Stuckey and M’Alister with the bat, and
Laver and Collins with the-ball, cause such players to be
regarded as certainties. Then there is old Geo. Giffen,
Dake and Gehrs, of S.A. —which leaves but three reps, for
N.S.W. if the match is to be 11 a-side.
* * • •
The l.uck of cricket l . In a competition match at
Adelaide last Saturday, West Torrens v. East Adelaide,
former won the toss, and F. Jarvis (one of its best bats)
being absent, sent East Adelaiders to the wickets,
whereupon they smote to such purpose that at the end of
the day’s play 377 runs were on the board for but one
wicket —Gehrs getting 196 and Dake 171 not out. They
are not taking any “ sending opponents in ” at Adelaide
now !
* * * •
Bowlers pretty well owned the situation in
Sydney first-grade cricket, Saturday, through rain that
morning and previous day. Sydney District declared its
innings—six for 299, made a week before—closed straight-
way, and aboriginal Marsh treated the Central Cum-
berlanders to a lively time. Sending them in a perfect
length he gathered six for 23, and, with luck, might have
done a heap better, for close shaves were frequent. The
innings closed for 45. Second effort produced 153, leaving
Sydney winners by an innings and 101 runs. Farquhar
and PyeL in C.C.’s second turn at wickets, hit brilliantly
for 58 and 35. Waverley walloped ’Varsity by 146;
Howard did most of the damage for former, scattering
seven for 34 in a total of 106. With Redfern’s 188 and 112
to go up against, Deichhardt-Balmain compiled 144, and
lost by 44 on the first innings. E. Hume (Redfern) bagged
seven for 57, and Sullivan (Deichhardt-Balmain) seven for
53. Glebe man Duggan, opposed to Paddington, was 92
not out previous Saturday, but only managed to add one.
Glebe “ declared” with seven for 282,'and then sent Pad-
dington pavilionwards for 122. For the latter, veteran
“ Alick” Bannerman accumulated 27—one less than that
other old ’un at the game, Iredale. This is Glebe’s first
triumph present season. North Sydney bested Burwood,
with eight wickets to„the good. Waverley now tops the
list (6 points), Sydney, Redfern and North Sydney are
next (4 each).
* * * *
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:—
“ Albert Trott, ‘Bobby’ Abel, and others playing cricket
with tennis rackets at Dords was a peculiar and funny
spectacle. The new game—a Yankee-Canuck one—is
called ‘Vigoro,’ and is practically tennis mm cricket.
The wickets are M-shaped, with the V towards the server,
and of net frames 2Jft. high by 3ft. in breath. Seven a
side, or less, every soul with a strong racket. The
‘bowler’ serves the soft rubber ball with his racket, the
‘ batsman ’ hits at it with a racket, and the ‘ wiket ’
(as it is termed) keeper stops with a racket, as do the
fieldsmen, though they may use a hand, but must
• throw in ’ with a racket. Altogether a ‘ racket-ty ’
game and very lively. You are ‘caught out’ on, and only
by, a racket, and this takes some doing. The match was
Players v. Pros., and the latter were whacked in an
innings. P. F. Warner, George (Ethel Haydon) Robey,
and Eustace H. Miles, the European tennis champion,
took part, ‘A.E.’ scored top twice for the losers, and
said the ‘ bowling ’ gave his arm—well! ”

Looks as if 50 miles within the hour is possible
on a bike. Oct. 24 saw French paced rider, Contenent,
traverse 46m. 1595 yd. inside 60min., and thus wipe out
world's champion (Michael’s) best for same term, achieved
a few months back, by 250yds. Latest advices are that
Michael purposed having another cut, and vowed he’d do
the 50 ere present season ends.

The Canada cycle and motor company received
a cable on Saturday advising the departure of the world’s
champion Major Taylor, who is to ride at the Cycling
Carnival to be held in January. The Major adds another
to the long list ot Massey Harris riders.*
* * * *
The disqualification of Don Walker is a hard
knock on the head to the nascent cycling boom. It was
crookedness of crack riders that damned the sport in the
eyes of the public a year or so back ; now the cycle-
meeting promoters, who were depending almost altogether
on the Vic. flyer to awaken enthusiasm in coming events
with the imported Major Taylor, will have a tough job to
make the public “ bite.”
• , • »
A case heard at Carlton (Melb.) Court last
week offered a solemn warning to bike-race punters.
Prosecutor, a clerk, charged a “ cash fielder ” with steal-
ing 155., inasmuch as accused had laid him 15s. to 2s. 6d.
against a winner at the Exhibition bicycle sports, and
didn’t meet the obligation. The “cash fielder” pleaded
innocence of larceny, on the ground that he had refunded
the backer’s 2s. 6d. and given an acknowledgment that
the 15s. was owing—for an indefinite period. The Bench
dismissed the case, remarking that bike-race bookmakers
ought not to be permitted to bet at the Exhibition-oval.
* » * »
The stage having worked the religious dodge so
successfully, the prof, cyclist now pastes his posters on
the churches. Nigger Major Taylor, the bicycle sprinter
whose visit to Australia is being extensively boomed, is
advertised as a consistent respecter of the Dord’s Day
who will not tolerate Sabbath racing.
• * * •
Entries for Austral wheel meeting (initiated in
Melbourne, Saturday, before 12,000 spectators) topped all
totals since ’99. Unlike previous meetings, there was
absolutely no open betting on the ground, and Mo didn’t
want to do extra much beforehand —he had painful
recollections of last year. Don Walker gathered in £95
during the afternoon, spite of going under in two and
three mile events of Commonwealth Stakes ; but after
his appropriation of the five miles, stewards investigated
his riding in the three miles final just before, wherein
he finished last, with the result that they disqualified
him for a month. This means he won’t figure in the
Austral—a fact that brings much joy to the Chosen, for
Walker was favorite. Explanation is that Don got
“ pocketed ” through no fault of his own. Intervention
of the Deague’s appeal board is possible, but hardly
probable.
* » * *
Mr. H. B. James’ 460£ miles on a motor bike in
the natural day of 24 hours, from Melbourne to Hamilton
via Geelong, Oamperdown, and Caramut, and back to 3
miles on Melbourne side of Dara, last week, is a world’s
record of its kind. The heavy drawback of incessant
severe vibration through both engine and road has scared
riders of the petrol two-wheelers from tackling the task ;
it calls for much grit. Stoppages consumed 4h. 12mins.,
and actual riding 19h. 40m. —at an average pace of 23|m.
an hour throughout. One section of 8 miles was traversed
in 15mins., and another of 82m. in 3h. sm. The run from
Melbourne to Hamilton (204 m.) was done in better time
than the mail-train makes.
* * * *
H. B. James, of the Dunlop Tyre Co., has just
ridden 460 J miles over Victorian country roads, on a
motor bicycle, in 24 hours inclusive of all stoppages and
delays. The tyres used were Dunlop moter, and they
needed no attention during the trip.*
* • * •
Twenty-five-year-old Bill, the second hope of
the Heckenbergs, met “ Bill Smith ” at Sydney National
Sporting Club, Monday night. Heckenberg stood over
6ft. and scaled 14st. Smith, almost as tall, looked about
ljst. lighter. Bill Smith used to be known as Oliver
Williams, the lepper, skipping-rope dancer, &c., known
in the backblox as “ the Bounding Bushman.” His party
offered twos and threes freely, spite of the fact that
against Maori Ruenalf, Heckenberg showed much the
better. Heckenberg was remindful of Goddard, but has a
left lead and quick right cross that Goddard knew nothing
of. From the first the scrap was savage, and midway
through the first round Smith floored his man with a heavy
bump, and the Diverpool giant crashed down under the
ropes w'ith his head on his arm like a model for the Dying
Gladiator. People were preparing to leave, it seemed
such a thorough knock-out. But at 10 he was on his feet
again, assumed the aggressive, and with a right cross
knocked Smith in a huddled heap. In the second round
he w'ent through Smith like fire through dry grass, and
spread-eagled him four times in quick succession. Then
Smith lay still, and his side skied the sponge in despair.
• » * *
Judging by Tim Hegarty’s fight against Paddy
King, at Sydney Gaiety A.C., Monday, ’tis no wonder he
was licked so often in ’Murka. All the skill and pug.
power claimed by Hegarty’s friends is apparently mythi-
cal. He crouches too low, brings himself down inches,
hasn’t got a good solid punch any time, and often
couldn’t get away from stumpy-stat.ired King’s right,
despite that it threatened seconds before launching out.
Hegarty notched several lefts and a few right hand body
punches. King operated mainly on ribs, and with
plenty of force. Tim tried frequently—almost always—to
get the right home, but it was either well-stopped
•or went round neck or shoulder as King came in. A
straight jab early in the tussle marked Paddy’s right
eye and another on the same spot later added to the
trouble. Bar this there was no best in it, and the battle
looked either man’s till the fifth round, when one on the
jaw worked up King’s “ Irish ” and while they were close
together Paddy threw Tim—whether accidentally or
otherwise deponent knoweth not —but Hegarty was
thrown, and hard too, which caused referee “ Snowy ”
Sturgeon to declare the man on the floor the winner.
Most of the crowded house hooted but all the same the
verdict was right. For satisfaction’s sake the same pair
should “get to it” again, though ’tis more than likely
champion lightweight Bob Turner will be Hegarty’s next
vis-a-vis. On the whole the thing was tame and there
was no championship form on either side at any stage.
* • * *
“Old Timer”: Considering that he put ’em up for
thefirsttimeagainstSmith lastweek, this scribe likes wood-
chopper Bill Heckenberg’s prospects among Australian
heavies. Though somewhat muscle-bound, punches from
Heckenberg need only get home for t’other chap to know
he has struck something. With good tutoring—say from
past-master D. Foley—the newcomer ought to do well.
That he is game is sure, for 19 out of 20 would have
staved down after that steam-hammer blow in the first
round. Heckenberg is matched with Booth, the Banana-
land heavyweight, whose operations have mostly been
confined to tent work at country shows and race meets,
when he opposed all comers, undertaking to knock them
out within a given time. Those who know Booth best
(boxers principally) assert that he’ll surely win ; but it 13
to be borne in tuind that he has been a long time away
from the game, also, like jockies with horses, boxers are
poor tipsters of fight winners. It would certainly pay in
the long run to always bet against their views.
» • * *
First note of a possible sculling boom was
struck, Saturday, when Harry Pearce, of Double Bay,
and Newcastle Dick Tressidder were matched in Sydnev to
row March next. Chris Neilson will train Tressidder,
while returning world’s premier Towns may interest
himself on Pearce’s behalf. Tressidder has for long had
his mind set on a match with home-coming Towns, and
waited years for him ; he is built on the lines of old Bill
Beach—big, heavily set, and strong as a lion, scaling
12.12—something near Beach’s racing weight when fit.
Dike the ex-world’s top-notaher, too, Tressidder wants a
lot of training, and can stand a draught horse’s share of
it. Pearce is asonof “Footy” Pearce—above the average
in Sydney sculling boom times, but never a champion.
Last good rowing match in Australia was six years ago,
when Towns squelched Nielson on the Hunter. If Tres-
sidder downs Pearce, which the writer tips as likely,
he’ll come at champion Towns.
* • • *
Vic. 4-oared championship rowed, Saturday,
over Dower Yarra 2'.m. course, went to Albert Park crew,
which led all the trip, and finished half-a-dozen lengths
to the good from Ballarat (only other competing boat) in
13.471, the fastest recorded for the race. This was the
15th struggle for same honor, and eight of the best oars-
men Victoria (the home of the game) owns sat in the two
boats. In the race’s history there are five successes down
to the “ Mercs.,” four to Yarra, three to Albert Park, and
two to Wendouree.
» * * *
Swimming supremacy (300 yards) of N.S.W.,
contested at Bronte (Sydney), Saturday, went, as most
people expected, to Dick Cavill, who wasn’t at any time
pressed, and finished with a gap of 16yds. between him-
self and the next man, Healy, in 3min. 45sec. —4 l-ssec.
outside the Australasian best, and 10 l-ssec. worse than
the world’s record, both of which are Cavill’s. Dittle
better than three weeks have elapsed since D.O.’s home-
coming from England, hence he could hardly be expected
to show extra well; besides, there was no need to do
anything better. Another month will surely see him near
his top. Other Australian champions (Read and Dane)
recently in England, are aboard the incoming Medic.

The Arch-Fiend Journalist.
The most noticeable feature in the slander action
against Trenwith, M.P., in Melbourne last week,
was the wholesale manner in which the functions
of a journalist were misunderstood by all con-
cerned. This arose partly from the ignorance of
Judge and Bar, partly from the endeavor of
various pressmen mixed up in the case to preserve
the tradition that they belong to a specially noble
and elevating, if underpaid, profession. It resulted
finally in Judge Hodges making some painfully
silly remarks on the subject when directing the
jury. The defence, he said, had urged that jour-
nalists—
were men who wrote the opinions which they were
ordered to write for the money they put in their pockets.
If that were true, journalists would be too honorably
described as the vilest creatures that encumbered the
earth, because they would be not only encumbering the
earth, but they would be poisoning every bit of real,
genuine, honest, mental growth in this world .... men
who were devoting the best talents that God had given
them, and that had placed man above the brutes, to
advocating by day and night causes they did not believe
in, and inducing them to adopt courses by false repre-
sentations, were fitting themselves for a companionship
with the arch-fiend, who was described as a deceiver from
the beginning and the father of lies.
All this is absurd judicial flapdoodle, and every
brain-worker who lets himself out for hire knows
it. It is the same in most professions : the lawyer
pleads as he is paid to plead; the clergyman earns
his living by a nice regard for the peculiarities of
his congregation; the soldier fights for causes he
doesn’t believe in; the politician speaks for
measures which he thinks will be popular even if
he doesn’t consider them advisable. The working
journalist is in exactly the same box. It has become
the tradition, however, for pressmen to talk in
vague terms of the great public services they are
rendering, in a more or less obscure fashion. The
paid journalist, if he were candid, would admit
cheerfully that he writes things that he doesn’t
believe in. If that were not the case, the news-
paper staffs throughout Australia would have to
be quadrupled in order to be tolerably sure of
having men at hand who could see eye-to-eye with
their employers on every topic. It is a huge
absurdity to pretend that the two or three leader-
writers on any particular paper_ (to only one of
whom the local political writing is entrusted, as a
rule) are indissolubly wedded to all the opinions
they express. Sydney has one notable example of
a slashing Freetrade writer who left his work at one
time to stand as a Protectionist for Parliament, and
Melbourne is said to contain a Foreign-trader who
does excellent work as an advocate of a high tariff.
And so long as they content themselves with
writing these opinions as the opinions of an em-
ployer, an editor, or an organisation, that is
journalism, and has nothing to do with the
“arch-fiend” Judge Hodges speaks about so
familiarly. There are several men on the
Australian press who honestly proclaim that they
have no opinions whatever of their own. They
are the ideal journalists, if they can write, but
Judge Hodges would exclude them from employ-
ment on any paper, unless, indeed, they went to
the S.M. Herald and wrote “neutralities.”
Our late visitor, violinist Max, Schliiter, has arrived in
’Frisco, U.S.A., and by now the Chanson Triste, which
Max plays like an angel, has been heard by the Friscoites.
At Honolulu, Franz Wilczek, who, whilst in Australia,
kept his Strad so obstinately in its case, joined the
Sonoma, and the two artists played duets and solos at the
ship’s concerts. Some sea-voyages have their compensa-
tions. During his stay at Honolulu, Mr. Wilczek had a
splendid concert, which was attended by the ex-Queen
and other dusky royalties, as well as all the principal
local citizens of the nation that bounced Lily-o’-Killarney’s
diadem away from her.

South Australia’s stock census shows that
that State has now 1900 camels.
Sydney Municipal Council elections re-
sulted satisfactorily. Most of the blatherskites
and humbugs were rejected; the aldermen
with good records were in all cases returned, and
the “ new blood ” is generally of high quality.
Dr. Camac Wilkinson, who headed the poll for
Belmore, is a distinct gain to the Council —a man
of great ability, high scientific accomplishments,
and much public spirit. Mayor Hughes was,
happily, returned in spite of his Lord title. The
rejection of Isaiah Cohen, Eden George, George
Perry, David Eealy, and a few others of the same
sort, is a matter on which the citizens are to be
congratulated.
The Veterinarian.

Tom Mann bearded some of the Melbourne
clergy in their lair at St. Paul’s Cathedral chapter
house, the other day. The wicked agitator had
undertaken to talk awhile on the subject of the
Church’s attitude towards labor politics, and
whilst stating the things which all parsons know,
but many of them prefer to ignore, lie mentioned
incidentally that the shareholders in the great
sweating business of Bryant and May, Ltd., were
largely clergymen. Whereat the Bev. Bust (fine de-
scriptive name) called the lecturer a liar, and shortly
afterwards withdrew to discover his mistake. The
foolishness of Bev. Bust served to get a half col.
report of Tom Mann’s lecture in next morning s
papers, also it warmed him into eloquence.. The
parsons sat abashed more or less (no parson is ever
quite abashed) whilst Mann told thern that the
church deliberately defended an industrial system
which prevented society being established, on a
moral basis. “If,” said he, “an honest, simple
statement of the facts were made by the church
concerning the production of wealth and those
who were entitled to such wealth, and those
who got wealth without working in any
way, it would do more good than had
been accomplished in this generation.” Possibly
Mr. Mann’s plain, hard talk to the gentle
shepherds will have an “ awakening ” effect here
and there. The fact that only one of them called
Mann a liar, and was not morally supported by
the rest of the gathering, may be taken as a nega-
tive indication of repentance all round. In fact,
the least promising Kerristian present was not a
parson, but an old lady. She Wanted to. know
where the landlord would be if nobody paid any
rent, to which Tom Mann replied that the land-
lord in that case might need to work for a living.
The elderly lady was too agitated to put any
more questions after that.

Biliousness
KNOCKED OUT BY
Chamberlain’s
Stomach and
Liver Tablets
Price, 1/6. Just what you need.
Biliousness is a condition characterised by
a disturbance of the digestive organs. The
stomach is debilitated, the liver torpid, the
bowels constipated. There is a loathing of
food, pains in the bowels, dizziness, coated
tongue and vomitings first of the undigested
or partially digested food and then of bile.
Take three Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets to begin with and then
one tablet each following day for three or
four days until the bowels become regular.
If one tablet is not enough to cause a free
movement of the bowels, take two; or if one
acts too freely, take only one every other
day. Chamberlain’s Stomach and Liver
Tablets should be taken immediately after
supper. Eat sparingly and of easily digested
food, such as boiled rice and milk toast.
Drink no coffee.

Selina Sangal was reprieved at a meeting
of the Vic. Cabinet on Monday night. With tho
pose of a weak-strong man, Premier Irvine said
that the reason for the reprieve was not the one
that The Bulletin has urged, but some other
reason, which he is not going to disclose. But so
long as Mr. Irvine is reasonable, no one will
trouble as to what mental processes he goe3
through to arrive at his conclusions. As a matter
of fact, no Australian Ministry could very well
dare to hang anybody (not guilty of deliberate
murder for the sake of gain—say poisoning for
insurance-money) who has been kept under sen-
tence for any considerable length of time. I hey
didn’t even dare to hang Bertrand in the old days,
after his doom had been long suspended by an
appeal to the Privy Council. Were a murderer
to be always kept in gaol three.months under sen-
tence he would very rarely indeed be hanged.
(And yet any sentence which won’t stand the test
of a few weeks’ calm consideration should surely
be esteemed a poor one.) Hanging is a thing that
a civilised community has to do in hot blood, or
not at all.

TO PLAY YOUR PIANO
The Price of the Pianola is £65 on moderate
monthly payments, or £6O for prompt cash.
ĥ
The Pianola is an instrument which will aid you in playing your piano.
It does the hard, technical work, leaving the player to infuse his own
personality into the music.
The performer does not play the Pianola. With the Pianola’s assistance
he plays the piano, though he may not know one note from another.
By a slight movement of a lever, the nimble little finger® of the instrument
that are pressing the piano keys are made to vary their attack,
A touch on another lever, and they hesitate, stop, and bound forward
again in their marvellous play. |
No mechanism is apparent, and the player, forgetting t'he Pianola, for-
getting the keys and complicated action of the piano, is absorbed in the
wonderful effects he is producing from the instrument before him.

ask for Catalogue “B.”
Sole Agents for Australasia:—
HENRY M. BIRGE & CO.

Edjudina (W.A.) is in the doldrums over the
projected abandonment by "Boulder” Hamilton of his
option on the Wallaby. Opinions regarding the possi-
bilities of the district are very divergent but, whatever
happens ultimately, it is now quite certain that it is not
to have a meteoric career—up like a rocket and down
like a stick. There is no chance of foreign capital find-
ing its way to the spot till the horny-handed son of toil
proves that he has found more than a block of scrub land.
South Kalgurli (W.A.), in exploiting the
ground with a diamond drill, has located two fairly
promising finds. Too much importance need not be
attached to these discoveries, as a drill core is so small
that it may prove misleading. This has happened in one
or two notable instances. Most of the gold now being won
comes from the 300 ft. level, and the mine needs very
careful handling to maintain the yield. Shareholders
are grumbling greatly at receiving no return on their
money, and are likely to grumble much more before they
get anything really substantial. Up to date £376,000
has been spent. Of this £188,500 was provided by the
shareholders, and £187,500 from the 54,000 tons treated.
Despite this enormous expenditure the ground is, ad-
mittedly, not half prospected, even at a comparatively
shallow depth, though the area is only 13 acres.
• , • * •
Victory United, Cuddingwarra (W.A.). has
been a great deal before the public of late, and scrip has
fluctuated erratically. 00. owns a 10-head mill and
cyanide plant, and, like most Adelaide-bossed shows, its
returns, like its market movements, are most uncertain.
On its capital of 80,000 shares three divs of 6d. each have
been paid—one in 1898, and two in 1899, the last in June
of that year. Since then calls have been necessary. The
upper levels are depleted, but recently the reef was cut
at 530 ft. A few crushings may eventuate, and, by
Stretching a few points, a div. may be declared. But
punters should be very careful, or they may be left to
nurse the baby.
• * * *
It transpires that the purchaser of the Lake
View Extended lease (W.A.), sold in London, by auction,
in Sept., was the Lake View South Co. The peculiarity
of the deal is the fact that both go’s were floated by
Horatio Bottomley, and were oflicered by the same indi-
viduals. The Extended co. had originally £35,000 work-
ing capital, to which was added £18,750 on reconstruc-
tion in 1899, so that in all £53,750 was expended on
plant, machinery, and development, and yet the lot was
knocked down for £3400. Is it any wonder, after this
transaction, that W.A. shares stink in the nostrils of the
British public ?
* * * *
Perseverance (W.A.) has at last been thrown
open to the public. So far as one can judge from a trip
underground there is absolutely nothing to conceal, and
why secrecy was so long enforced is a mystery. The
mine has been opened up in a masterly manner, and
there is practically nothing to be seen at which the most
captious critic could cavil. As some of the stopes are 30
to 40ft. wide it is easy to imagine how the large output
is maintained. The surface plat t is a picture, and it is
kept in the pink of condition, quite a contrast to most of
the plants on the field. Manager Ralph Nicholls, who
only returned to the mine two months ago, is leaving
immediately for London, but as his understudy, Flynn,
really holds the reins, Nicholls’ absence will not be
seriously felt. No stoping has been done below the 500 ft.
level, and, as there are three lodes to operate on,
including Tetley’s, the amount of ore in reserve i 3
enormous.
* • * *
The Queen Margaret (W.A.), after sinking the
shaft to 400 ft. on its new property, the S. Gippsland,
at Vosperton, started a crosscut at 370 ft. The reef was
penetrated 2ft., but no results have been published, and
the water has been allowed to rise to prevent spies giving
the show away. A crosscut is now being put in at the
280 ft. level. The co. is controlled by Melbourne people,
and it is generally anticipated that, when in the humor'
they will make things hum. It is stated that the 50,000
tons of tailings on the Bulong property were sold for
£2OOO, or 9|d. a ton. The purchaser must be hugging
himself over the deal.
• * * *
Mr. Sticht, general manager of Mt. Lyell, has
demonstrated that pyritic smelting is something more
than a theory. For over a fortnight he has run the nest
of furnaces in No. 2 plant without using any coke what-
ever, the sulphur in the ore being utilised as fuel. There
was no hitch whatever, in fact the furnaces seemed to
run faster than they did previously, and Mr. Sticht is
quite satisfied that this can be looked upon as “ a per-
manent and valuable feature in the reduction process.”
This is the largest scale on which the pyritic process,
pure and simple, has been successful, and the economy
of it is likely to have an important bearing on
the financial result of Lyell operat ons. Various
changes made by Mr. Sticht last half-year showed a
reduction in the cost of production of over £3 per ton of
copper, and with the pyritic process at work still further
reduction will be shoivn. It is stated that the hot-air
blast has been discarded, Mr. Sticht having found it more
effective to apply a considerably increased cold-air blast.
This is a technical feature of much importance to metal-
lurgists, and doubtless, when he has complete data, Mr.
Sticht will publish details. This achievement adds
another feather to the cap of Mr. Sticht, who has rightly
earned the distinction of being the most accomplished—
as he is the least assuming—general mine manager and
metallurgist who ever came to Australia.
* j * * *
London shareholders in North Lyell-have shown
their disapproval of the past management by rejecting
the old board of directors, which included some men
of high professional attainments in the metallurgical
world. The challenge thrown out by ex-Manager Trent
has evidently been accepted, as the new Board has com-
missioned Mr. Rich, former superintendent of the Rio
Tinto mine, Spain, to make an exhaustive report on the
• mine and methods of treatment of ore. It is reported
that the co. has recently made some discoveries of high-
grade ore in the deeper levels. If this be so and a
vigorous policy of working, with a suitable system of
treatment, be adopted, there is still hope that this mag-
nificent mine will be saved from ignominious collapse.
■» * * »
As predicted, the fly in the Briseis ointment
was that the overburden was not being removed to the
extent calculated upon in the original estimates, and the
directors have given instructions for increased activity in
this direction. The whole of the available water forces
are to be turned on to the overburden, in the hope, as
Mr. Fraser, the new' manager, anticipates, that it will all
be shifted in 12 months.
* • * *
Brilliant Extended (C.T.) makes its first appear-
ance in the div. list with a substantial distribution of
£6250. A welcome relief to shareholders, who have spent
over £50,000 in developing the mine. The gold was won
from a depth of over 2100 ft., and it is understood that
there is a long succession of divs. in sight.
* » * •
Charters Towers will make a div. record this
year, eclipsing any previous year by about £60,000. The
total will reach close on £400,000, and be £300,000 in
excess of calls. The improvement on last year will be
over £150,000.

Deputy Commissioner of Taxes Searcy (S.A. \
rules that if a man endorses a cheque drawn for ’ a
larger amount than £2 he is legally bound to put a Id.
- stamp on the document under the provisions of the new
Stamp Act. Many Adelaide business firms, which once
took a receipt for each employee’s wages, now dispense
with that formality in order to save their subordinat es
the weekly impost. An office boy at 10s. a week, being-
paid monthly, has to stamp the voucher for his pay.

M anager Sutherland’s run to Europe to consult
his Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) board is mainly with a view
to re-starting the Fremantle smelting works.

At Poverty Point.
Melba, it is said, at first objected strenuously to
appearing in opera, and said she wasn’t going to make
herself tired on what was practically a holiday trip. She
must be glad now, however, that Musgrove persuaded
her, as Melba in opera is a far greater artist than the
same person on the concert platform, and her Marguerite
was necessary to show Australia what solid foundation
there was for her reputation.

Strong man Pagel laughs at all who say that his lion
will one day make a meal of him. The big German’s
confidence in his strength is, perhaps, the secret of his
success with the brute. Pagel claims that he is absolute
master of Leo by reason of the hold he gets on him—a
grip on one paw and an iron elbow against the lion’s
throat, leaving him powerless. He explains that the lion,
young as he is, has tried “ funny business” already, only
to back down speedily and apologise in abject terms.
This seems true, for the lion is anything but docile towards
strangers. On the day of Pagel’s private exhibition, half
the medical men in Melbourne were scared out of their
senses. At each arrival in the tent it jumped with roars,
striking the side of the cage four feet at once, glaring
hard at the discomfited surgeons. Also the lion, during
a performance last week, had to be severely clubbed by
an attendant for expres ing a desire to eat the mastiff in
full view of the audience.
People differ strangely in their estimates of the age of
Dolores. The unkind guesses remind one of the late
Richard Stewart’s eoi"'plaint: “It seems that my
daughter Nellie was born some years before I first met
her mother.” Dolores made her debut in London in ’B9,
and was engaged for a Scandinavian tour the same year.
Her mother, the original Trebelli (Gillebert spelt back-
wards with the G omitted) was only 21 when she made
her first appearance in grand opera at Madrid in 1859 ;
so it goes without saying that daughter Antoinette
(now Antonia), an artist from her childhood, was a mere
girl when she started public life.
The “ star ” pupils of Mrs. Green’s academy are prepar-
ing special “ turns ” for her forthcoming fancy-dress ball
at Melbourne Town Hall. Miss Florrie Green will do the
shadow dance from “ Dinorah ” ; also, Miss Duckett and
Miss Irene Dillon, both of them high-sr hool dancers, are
expected to rise to the occasion on their educated toes.
Miss Dillon, by the way, has already had the offer of an
engagement from J. O Williamson, who takes a natural
interest in encouraging amateur students of the stage
arts. Mrs. Green has his permission to use any of the
dance music in his operas for teaching purposes. So the
young amateur gets more or less the training of a pro-
fessional, and one may say of the comic opera ballet that,
like charity, it begins at home.
One musical artist now in Australia came with a well-
developed delusion that the star with whom he was to
appear was madly in love with him. It required not
merely friendly remonstrance, but also managerial
strategy to keep him from too pronounced an exhibition
of his devotion. At one time it was the rule of an
operatic tenor to regard the prima donna as one of his
“ perquisites,” but those good times are past.
Charles Arnold writes from near London to his old
friend and manager, L. J. Lohr : “ I have bought a place
with 10 rooms, billiard room, and J-acre of fruit and
flowers. I don’t expect to play again for some time —not
until I get another ‘ Jones,’ and they don’t grow on every
bush. Had a very bad time with gout since arriving in
New York and England—so bad that if I had had my
company I would have been forced to close. This is the
result of suppressing many previous attacks without
proper treatment. Am under the care of a specialist,
who hopes to get the acid out of my system by
means of electric treatment, and once rid of that I can
more or less guard against gout in the future. I suppose
you heard of the Nance O’Neil fiasco. The co. received
but half a week’s salary for three weeks work. They had
jumped from S’Africa to Cairo, then jumped to London and
rested for six months, and wound up by getting J week’s
salary in 7 months. Sharp (Sharp w r as Arnold’s original
old professor in ‘ Jones’) has left the stage and is study-
ing law, and Thomas is playing his old part in 1 Charley’s
Aunt.’ Mrs. Thomas is nursing a fat little boy, now two
months old. Miss Bensusan has the promise of an
engagement in New York.”
McKee Rankin, who has recently had so disastrous
an experience with his Nance O’Neil season at London
Adelphi, was the first American manager to take an
American co. to England. That was in ’BO, when he pro-
duced Joaquin Miller’s “Danites” at Sadler’s Wells.
“To preserve youth drink gin” is the advice of one
Australian actress, who is certainly juvenile enough in
figure.

Piggotfc, the English fraud who claims to
be Christ, was challenged by a clergyman “to
debate the question.” The answer came from a
secretary: “ Any communication addressed to
‘the Rev. W. H. Smyth Piggot’ will be dis-
regarded, as that person has ceased to be. A
request addressed to ‘ the Messiah ’ will receive
attention.” The sec. added, getting in one for
himself, that “he was not secretary to Mr.
Piggot, but was simply as John the beloved
disciple was to Jesus Christ.” Which distinctly
“ lays over ” even our old friend Dowie.
S.A. Parliament, which has just ended
one of the maddest sessions on record, had, in its
last moments, one flash of intelligence, to the
disgust of the Government, which wanted to g°
on its course of insane riot to the end. Among
Ministers’ wild proposals was one to carry a rail-
way into the Pinnaroo sand-desert, on the pretext
that it would open up rich agricultural land.
Whereupon a cautious Scot set going the idea,
finally adopted, of putting the country to the hard,
practical test of cultivating three typical patches
of 200 acres each for three years, for the guidance
of Parliament.
