The Station-Master, Bay of
Dogs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was the last of the 15 railway cadets who had
started in January of the year to learn telegraphy
in the big gallery of the operating room at the
General Telegraph Office. One by one I had seen
them despatched to little wayside country
stations where a dreary station-master, and per-
haps a half-looney, morose porter, made life a hell
upon earth. They would be called in the official
returns “ Railway Officers,” but wheni I had been
a few months at the Bay of Dogs station I learned
they were the most miserable of drudges,
The Bay of Dogs was bad enough, but a country
station, where the sole connection with the express
trains consisted of holding a white flag while said
trains thundered through the station at 30 miles
an hour, and a brutal travelling public threw
orange-peel or decayed fruit at the “ officer,” Was
simple Sheol. For the rest, the duties would
consist of serving out letters, collecting dog-taxes,
sheep-rates, Govt, life-assurance premiums, and
the scraping clean of two sets of points which
gave access to the station sidings.
Bay of Dogs was the first suburban station of
the city of Romahapa ; and there were only two
sidings, and no brutal cockatoos loaded up with
hides and grain and potatoes. I believe in
democracy, but I disbelieve in the cockatoo.
The station was built on piles which rose out of a
corner of the bay, all the solid land being required
for the permanent way, and my first duty every
morning was to let myself down among the slimy
cross-beams and piles, and with a long pole engi-
neer into the oozy current the carcases of three or
four dogs that invariably lodged there during the
night.
The station buildings consisted of a lean-to,
with the office at one end, the ladies’ waiting-
room at the ot er, and the ordinary shelter-shed
between them. We had no goods-shed, as our only
heavy traffic consisted of coal loaded inwards for
the suburban coal-dealers.
The Station-master at the Bay of Dogs was a
grim and terrible personage named Kierney. He
had the finest flow of language on the whole
llurunui-Bluff section. With the exception of
Hooper, the sub-station-master, everyone was
afraid of the grim old man. The only people not
afraid of him were the drivers ; there is nothing
that the railway driver is afraid of, barring a baby
or a woman on the line. Kierney’s hair and
pointed whiskers were grey, his eyes red coals
that glowed out of a face that always reminded
me of a crumpled bank note.
The Bay of Dogs was a place of long hours, for
trains ran 21 out of the 24, and being a block sig-
nalling station, someone had to be on duty all the
time. The morning shift began at 4.45 a.m., and
ended at 1 p.m., when the “ evening man ” came
on, and continued till the block signalled “all
clear.” The cadet, who, for the time being, was
me, started at 8 a.m. and left at 6 p.m., and half
that time was in a state of mental agony and tor-
ture, for, as with all the rest, the very presence of
Kierney filled me with terror.
Hooper, the sub., tried his best t.o make the
worm turn, but the worm was too utterly crushed,
and the cadet at the Bay of Dogs was a wan -
faced, timid boy with a hunted look in his eyes.
Kierney used to make me sit on a high stool with
my back turned to him, and every time he caught
sight of my face through shifting about the office,
he would break out into a torrent of frightful
abuse, which always ended with him hurling me
off the stool, with a blood-curdling yell of “Out
of my sight, you blanky caw-caw ! ” Then I would
vanish outside and shiver in the wintry wind that
blew across the loathsome mud flats till the awful
voice of Kierney would be heard shrieking to
know what the Maoriland Railway Department
paid me for. Still, the old man taught me how
to work the block signals, and he ground the why
and wherefore of every book and form in the
station into my inmost soul, and when I left the
Bay I could balance up the accounts of any
station quicker than many clerks twice my rail-
way age.
* * • *
Kierney was a drunkard. Half the time he was
in a state bordering on delirium tremens, and it
was only his unaccountable intimacy with the
chief traffic clerk at Romahapa that kept him in
his position. But, let him be ever so drunk,
his gait was never affected. He would always
march off duty at 1 p.m. with the stride and gait
of a Guardsman. The sun always seemed to shine
brighter when he went off duty, and the harsh,
unpitying wind that blew across the malodorous
mud-flat seemed less keen and only half as
poisonous when his grey-clad, bent shoulders dis-
appeared through the little garden gate of the
station-house.
All things come to an end, and slowly it began
to dawn upon me that Kierney’s shouts and awful
cursings did me no more real physical harm than
the soft, effeminate lisping of Hooper, the sub. I
still lived; and on the few occasions when
Kierney tried to do me physical violence I easily
dodged him. My terror of him began to fade
rapidly. As it did, the Bay seemed a less hideous
place. In fact, there were times when I almost
loved it. In the mornings, when the long plat-
form glittered with frost, and there was no stench
from the mud-flats because they were covered by
the tide, and away through the canopy of smoke
and haze the big city loomed indistinctly, there
was something about the Bay that appealed to
me. I was beginning to feel my feet, and used to
ogle, in a very mild fashion, the girls who came
from further-out suburbs to attend the Ladies’
College, the gables of which were just visible
through the tree-tops about a quarter-of-a-mile
from the station. ,Then, in the evenings—pro-
viding it was flood-tide, when a little breeze
used to sigh across the water and wave the tiees
that hung over the big bluff around which the
northern line vanished, and bring sounds of music
from great steamers going down the Victoria
Channel, and the lights from the buoys would
flash and gleam, and great bars of carmine burned
dully through the grey and white clouds that
were always piled over the distant peninsula—
then the Bay would look really picturesque.
Still Kierney, though no longer terrible, was a
source of nervous irritation, and I felt, somehow,
that some day, when he was pouring out a torrent
of abuse, I 'should hit him right on the big
wart that stuck out blackly through the grey
whiskers on his chin. That wart got on ray soul.
I was in a state of constant desire to hit it with
both hands. Even Kierney seemed to realise that
the old order was changing. He left off bellowing
at me, and sometimes, when things were slack
and he hadn’t been drinking, he would talk
quietly and ask me the reasons for certain book-
keeping entries and the wherefore of the existence
of the GB4 book itself ; and I began to realise that
I owed the old fiend a debt of gratitude for a lot.
of knowledge of railway accountancy.
• * » *
There was drought in the North, although the
rivers were bankers. The long, hot, burning
summer and autumn had melted the glacieis from
which they sprang, but the rivers of that country
run between giant terraces, sometimes 20011.
below the level of the surrounding plains, and up
on those plains the land was naked for want of
rain. Pastoralists were trucking all sheep and
stock south, where the clover was knee-deep and
the grass like a Persian carpet. The Railway
Department was taxed to its utmost to cope with
the rush. All day and all night the stock trains
thundered past from the north, and all day and all
night the long trains of empty trucks went rolling
back to bring more and more. As soon as one
train cleared the section another was switched on,
the block-bells would call—one, two, one ; there
would be answering bells, and away over the bluffs
and hills would come the weird, unearthly screech
of the “ Bull-Yank ” engines. I seemed to live on
the platform those days, with a white flag in my
hand for our station had no semaphores, and our
block system had no interlocking apparatus, and
was a thing of shreds and patches, and an utter
delusion as a safeguard. The roar and rush ot
those crowding trains through our station was a
moral tonic, and I began to lose the desire to
hit Kierney’s wart. . T
Kierney seemed very shaky that night. I was
about to go off duty when the old man approached
and offered me a packet of “ Welcome ” cigarettes,
and in a low, strange voice, especially for him,
asked me would I mind staying on duty with him
till this infernal rush was over. He said he had a
va»ue fear, a premonition of coming disaster, and
he would take it very kindly if I would stop on
with him. ....
It was so unusual that I could only gasp out that
I would be delighted. 1 got down the monthly
returns, and began to work at them. Kierney
kept popping across to his house, and coming back
with an anxious query as to whether everything
was going right. The long twilight had deepened
into gloom, and the trains rounding the northern
bluff could only be distinguished by the great,
flaring headlights, and the screeching whistles
would blow till the waving of the small white light
told that' the line was clear. The returns did not
get much forrader, for the trains came so fast that
all my time was spent between the two block
instruments and the platform, waving the white
*
There were two engines on the train, and both
their whistles sounded at once. I reached for the
Romahapa block : One-two-one ! Quick as light-
ning came the replv : 41 Line blocked. I rushed
out on to the platform and waved the red light,
and across the glinting waters came the screech of
brakes; and, as the long, heavy train came
roaring down the slope on to the viaduct, I saw
the wheels streaming with sparks. As I rushed
out Kierney rushed in, but I did not notice him
particularly. Again Romahapa block sounded :
“Line clear!” and I darted back to give the
answering signal, and secure right of road for the
big train. Kierney was down on his knees,
groping under the desk. Out again on to the
platform, a wave of the white light, and the
screech of the brakes suddenly ceased and the
rhythm of the sounding rail-joints became regular
and rapid again. She ripped past at 20 miles an
hour; and, as she went thundering through,
Kierney came out on the platform on all fours and
gaped stupidly at the flying train. Suddenly he
sprang to his feet and shouted: “Jack ! Jack!
where are you ? ” Then as suddenly he went
down on all fours again, and darted back into the"
office. He could certainly travel at a respectable
rate on all fours. As another train was signalled
on to the section I saw Kierney groping about,
and his voice was a whine of entreaty but it
made my flesh creep.
“Come on, Jack, there’s a good boy; come to
your poor old father. Come on, you young devil !
I’ll get angry with you if you play this tomfoolery
any longer. Good God !my boy, I thought you
were under that train till I heard you laugh.
Come on, let’s go tmme now, little son ; come—
come on.” Then he waited for an instant, and
burst out savagely : “You young pup, I’ll break
every bone in your body if you don’t come
out.” Then he suddenly recoiled and covered his
face with his hands, and commenced calling
piteously to Jack to “ step"into it —to fill it up.”
The whistle sounded across the bay. I signalled
on to Romahapa, and going outside waved the
white light. When the train was just at the end
of the platform, Kierney rushed out and tried to
snatch the lamp from me, “ Stop her! stop her!
for God’s sake! the child’s hiding under the
platform—he’ll be cut to pieces;” and as I
struggled with him he tried to leap down between
the rails. I gripped him round the waist, while
the lamp flew across the track. The whistle gave
a tremendous screech. Kierney went down on
his back and I on top of him. He tried to roll
over towards the rails, and got one hand on the
edge of the platform. I hung on desperately.
Heavens ! Would that accursed train never pass !
It seemed to be miles long, and my strength was
ebbing, and the old man with his purchase of
a grip of the edge of the platform slowly hauled
me closer and closer, and I was wondering how I
would know when it was time to let go. Suddenly
I saw the red tail-lights, and a glimpse of the
guard in the van making the last entries on his
running-sheet, and as I released my hold the old
man rplled in between the rails.
Fearing he would get under the platform and
be perhaps cut up by the next train, that was even
then waiting to come on to the section, I jumped
down after him. Another struggle, and then I
managed to drag him into the office and secure
him in a chair, and lash him and the furniture
securely to the leg of the big fixed desk. Then I
attended to the signals.
He raved there for a while, and then lapsed
into a kind of troubled sleep. The bells rang on,
train after train went rushing by, and in the
intervals I wrote a report to the Traffic Manager
that the Station-master at Bay of Dogs was mad. I
left it lying there till 1 signalled the t rains through.
Two hours later the grey dawn stole in through
the little win lows, and when I came in, after
passing-the night goods through, the station-
master, awake and in his right mind, had his eye
glued to my maiden report to the Traffic Manager.
He looked up calmly. “ Have I been very
violent, my boy?” I nodded. When I had
finished at the signalling instrument the old man
declared he was all right and begged to be
liberated that he might go home to bed. I cut
the cords, and he staggered weakly to his feet.
“ Wait till the last one goes through, and I’d help
you across,” I said, throwing some coal on to the
tire. It blazed up and threw a warm glow across
the little room. Kierney sank down in his chair
and held his hands out to it. 1 noticed how thin
and wasted and wrinkled they were. All the
vile abuse was forgotten, and I looked at his
bruised face and wasted, wrinkled hands and
began to snivel for pity of him.
He heard the sob, and turned his haggard,
wasted face round to where I stood at the block
instrument. “ Yes, my boy, let what you’ve seen
this night be a warning to you never to play the
fool with woman or child. You can play the
devil with anything else—health, wealth and
reputation—but the female and the kid, deal
gently with them, or they’ll find you out. When
your money has gone, and your health is wrecked,
and even the dogs slither over to the other side of
the street, you can end it—simply tun off the
track.
“I’m a haunted man, my boy, and I’m afraid
to end it. Don’t know what’s waiting for me in-
side the Home Signal. When I was young I got
tired of being always without a cent, and the
sight of other fellows spending money on oysters
and drinks got me down. 1 was married to a
delicate wife with a delicate baby—and 1 never
knew what it was to have a decent pair of boots.
So one day I bolted.
“ Little Jack died from sheer want. I learned
of it years afterwards when I met my wife in' a—a
low wine-bar. She flung it at me across the
glasses, and the crowd of drinkers threw me out
into a world that has never been the same since,
for remorse got hold of me, and ever since I’ve
been haunted.”
' I felt that I must say something. “ How ? Do
you see the ghost of the little boy ?” I asked. He
turned on me with a burst of the old fury.
“No, you blanky caw-caw ! If I saw a ghost
I’d be all right; I’d be satisfied ; I’d know that
Jack still lived in some other state of existence.
But he doesn’t. He’s blotted out, and everywhere
I go I see the empty, gaping vacancy where my
child should be. The sunlight shines round that
black emptiness and never fills it. I never see W
group of children playing or standing but that
empty blackness is there, and I know that Jack is
annihilated, gone, obliterated, nowhere —like the
last signal you sounded on that cursed block —
Gone, Gone-GONE ! ”
And his voice went up to a yell, and when
Hooper rushed in ten minutes later he found me
holding down a hopeless lunatic, while the block
bells called in vain; for the Station-master at
Bay of Dogs was mad.
HERMES.
A candidate for the House of Commons, (
addressing a bucolic meeting, was so vague and
guarded in the expression of his views that one of
his auditors asked him what on earth he was
driving at. “I, sir,” was the reply, “am driving
at the Amelioration of the Illimitable Perspec-
tive.” Which “ lies over ” even Dan O’Connor.

BILIOUSNESS is very rife in Australia.. The enervating- climate and
dietary habits directly tend to foster it. This is especially notice-
able during the summer months, and there are few individuals who
at this season do not experience an occas'onal attack of BILIOUSNESS.
The excessive heat of an Australian summer has a debilitating l effect on the liver, causing that
organ to become sluggish in its action, with the result that it fails to regulate the supply of bile in
the system, and Biliousness is the natural outcome. BILE BEANS, a purely vegetable
production, have been especially prepared to counteract those debilitating climatic effects, and hy
stimulating the liver to increased action have gained world-wide praise as an undoubted specific for
this distressing complaint.
Mrs. J. S. Pawlson, a dressmaker, for-
merly residing at Wolfran-street, Broken
Hill, and now at Fremantle, W.A., was
severely troubled with Biliousness, she
consulted several doctors without result,
at length Bile Beans for Biliousness cured
her, and she writes as follows: —“For
about eighteen years, previous to about
two years ago, I was a sufferer from
extreme Biliousness, The attacks, were so
severe at times that I fell down in fainting
fits. Of course you will understand the
effect these fits had on my general health.
I consulted several doctors with regard to
my complaint, but their prescriptions had
little beneficial effect, and that only for the
time being, I also tried many different
brands of patent medicines but without
effect. About two years ago I happened to
see an advertisement wherein Bile Beans
had proved efficacious in curing Biliousness,
and decided to purchase a box. After a
few doses I,felt first-rate, and continuing
with them I was completely cured, and have
had no recurrence of the fainting fits for
twelve months, and the biliousness has
entirely left me. I also give Bile Beans great
praise as a blood purifier. Previous to taking
them my face was very sallow and covered
with brown spots, but now, thanks to Bile
Beans, I have a fairly good complexion. I
have recommended them in many cases,
and the verdict has been, they are a
wonderful medicine.”

The Drums of Battersea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They can’t hear in West o’ London, where the
• worst dine with the best—
Deaf to all save lies and laughter, they can’t hear
in London West—
Tailored brutes and splendid harlots, and the
parasites that be—
They can’t hear the warning thunder of the
Drums of Battersea.
More drums ! —War drums 1—
Drums of Misery—
Beating from the hearts of men—the Drums of
Battersea.
‘ Where the hearses hurry over, and where man
lives like a beast,
They can feel the work-drums beating—men of
Hell! and London East.
And the far-off foreign farmers, fighting fiei’cely
to be free,
Found new courage in the echo of the Drums of
- Battersea.
More drums I —War drums !
Beating for the free—
Boating on the hearts of men—the Drums ’of
Battersea.
And the drummers ! Ah ! the drummers ! —stern
and haggard men are those
Standing grimly at their meetings; and their
washed and mended clothes
Speak of worn-out wives behind them and of
grinding poverty—
But the English of the English beat the Drums of
Battersea !
More drums ! War drums!
Drums of agony—
The big bruised heart of England’s in the Drums
of Battersea.
Where in fields slave Englishwomen, oh 1 the
sound of drums is there :
I have heard it in the laughter of the nights of
Leicester Square-
Sailing southward with the summer, London but
a dream to me,
Still I feel the distant thunder of the Drums of
Battersea !
More drums ! War drums !
Drums of Liberty-
Rolling round the English world—the Drums of
Battersea.
Oh ! I heard them in the Queen’s Hall —ay ! and
London heard that night—
While we formed up round the leaders while they
struck one blow for right !
And the old strength, that old fire, that I thought
was dead in me,
Blazed up fiercely at the beating of the Drums of
Battersea !
More drums ! War drums !
They beat for victory
When above the roars of Jingoes rolled the Drums
of Battersea.
And where’er my feet may wander, and howe’er
I lay iny head,
-1 shall hear them while I’m dreaming—l shall
hear them when I’m dead !
For they beat for men and women, beat for
Christ, and you and me :
There is hope.and there is terror in the Drums of
Battersga !
More drums ! War drums !
Drums of destiny :
There’s -hope I—there’s hope for England in the
Drums of Battersea.
HENRY LAWSON.
A Sermon in Maremma.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a long white road through the wheat-
fields, which are ablaze at millionons points with
poppies. So many poppies are there that their
changing in the breeze gives you the suggestion
of the night on these foot-hills, when the air is
sown and shot with fire-flies.
These little fields of thirty furrows wide,
The little crops of sixty paces long.
Why, that statement has cut itself into verse-
lengths without my aid or wish. It’s hard to write
prose if the words sing. Above is Massa Marit-
tima perched on the high cliffs and trying to look
young in the new spring sun, although it
was old before Joseph took Mary to Egypt to
escape Herod the King. The ox-waggons come
down from Massa—their two great mouse-colored
bulls clashing wide and degenerate horns as the
lazy driver drags on the iron bit in their nostrils.
The waggons go to Follonica. The weeding girls
who are working on the half-grown wheat, their
wooden shoes stacked by the roadside (for wooden
shoes wear out, and feet do not) are oblivious of
the waggons and Follonica; and so are we—you
and I.
Loaf along an inch on your elbow—it is a
Titanic struggle, I know—and see the shimmer of
the indigo-blue of the Mediterranean beyond
Follonica; and on the sky-line is a belt of our
expatriated countrymen, the Eucalypti, brought
hero to bring health to the beauteous swamp of
the Maremma. Do not look upon the gum-trees
—we are here exiles also.
You remember the first gum we talked under,
kissed and quarrelled under. No, you don’t.
.1 ust like a woman—forgetting the trivialities and
clutching the essentials.
Ifor lunch we have sausage of Genoa and sweet
peas in the pod ; a bottle of sweet Capri for you,
a big bottle of Barolo for me, and a little bottle—
a pocissimo—of Bousaquie. Now, there’s a liqueur
brandy for you. Fill a glass, empty it, and wash
that glass in hot water, and the glass is still
sticky.
For eating there are two fowls—a fowl-and-a-
half is sufficient for me. If a man is stronger he
should be considerate, and you may have the
other half. Some cold veal, salad, oranges,
peaches, and loquats.
Pearly beloved, —There is but one religion, and
that is the religion of love—not Heavenly love
(whatever that is)— not love that gives up all—but
love which is just sufficiently renunciatory to
give away that which it does not want in order to
secure in return that which it does want. (Pass
the salt, O, my beloved). The love which is
worthy of a religion is certainly not all ideal; and
it is certainly not all material. Absolutely
Heavenly love dies because it is starved—abso-
lutely material love dies because it is surfeited.
And when the love (any love) dies, the capacity
to love begins to die, too, and with the capacity
to love, the lover also. Experience is indelible—
we can never again get the psychological flush of
youth, although we may secure a return of the
physiological—by exercise or wine ; but that great
trustfulness —that freshness of first love—can never
come to us again. Therefore, are we very neces-
sary to one another. (You may have all that veal.
I don’t like it.)
Any religion not based on common ordinary
humanity—that is not really a reflection of it—
dies. A really heaven-inspired religion would
have no more support in the market than a “ bull ”-
operation in ice at the North Pole.
See, for instance, how the hymns of the Angli-
can church reflect the desires of the people they’re
intended for. The favorite hymn of the male
religionist is “ Onward, Christian Soldier.” For
myself, it affects me slightly in the same way as
the Marseillaise does. When I hear the Mar-
seillaise played I want to go out and stab a Jingo
and see all the sawdust run out of his heart.
We’ve only one glass. You drink first. . . .
Now kiss your side of the glass, and keep your
finger on the place while I pour out the wine. . . .
May you live a thousand years, and then die of
love for me.
Dearly beloved—As the man who has religion
is kept up to the mark by strident militant hymns,
so is the woman-convert encouraged to aspire to
the glory of submission. See how sincerely the
women sing the hymps of apparent renunciation
—it isn’t renunciation ; it’s submission, and sub-
mission with a woman is conquest :
Oil ! to be nothing,
Only to lie at his fret,
A broken and empty vessel
For the Master's use made meet.
Now that’s all of earthly love—real, human
love. It has nothing more to do with the Chris-
tian ideal of renunciation than you and I have to
dp with the modelling of that awful Michael-
Ange o Adam and Eve in Pisa Cathedral.
Dearly beloved ! Did you feel when we first
met that you wanted to lie at my feet? —Now,
don’t throw the fruit at me ; you might want it
before you die —or maybe after. Fruit, I’m told,
is very cooling.
Pass the Bousaquie, please.'
Dearly beloved ! —ln conclusion. There is but
one religion, and it is love ; but one heaven, and
it is a heaven we may have every day ; and but
one sacrament —a kiss.
See those prematurely-aged Women carrying
the great flat wine-barrels full of water on their
heads. It is a very courageous fine, a Cupid
used to knocks and bad housing, who comes to
stay with them. In the interests of love for all,
poverty should- be legislated off the earth.
To the mountain or the sea. Up the mountain
to Massa be it, then. And let us praise Love
with the hymn of Exhortation as we go.
Go not happy day
From the shining fields ;
Hasten not away
Till the maiden yields.
And it is a pity that a day is but 24 hours long.
FLORENCE.
RANDOLPH BEFORD.
The Motions of Mipper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nipper got spliced yesterday, and last night he
invited “the herd” to a “darnce and general
jollo.” Towards 10 o’clock some of ’em mixed
matters, and Nipper left ’em to it and came out-
side with a few of his cobbers.
“Yersee,”he says, “when a bloke breaks up
over ’is girl yer don’t want ter go near ’im, ’n’ yer
want ter keep ’ er away from ’im, too, coz when
she starts squarin’ ’im ’e only gets worse and
finishes up by gettin’ drunk. Then next mornin’
’e fetches 40 bob for heavin’ ’is science about ’n’
tearin’ a uniform. ’N’ another thing : when yer
takin’ yer donah to a darnce yer don’t want ter
tell ’er not to darnce with any certain bloke, coz
she’ll promise yer she won’t orlright, but d’reckly
she gets to the ’all, strike me pink, he’s the very
fust bloke she looks for. Don’t / know ’em !
" My little bit o’ heliotrope use ter break me up
that way once, but I tumbled to a thing or two.
W’en I'd feel that sinkin’ feelin’ below me belt
coinin’ on I use ter say to meself, ‘ Here goes ! ’
Then I’d start doin’ a tale to every girl in the
room ’n’ givin’ ’em garden stuff. Well, my little
lot’d stand it for a while, then she’d get sulky an’
ref use to darnce ; then I know’d I ’ad ’er, ’n’ when
we’d be goin ’ome she use ter rouse on me quietly ;
then I know’d she was mine ’n’ I was ’ers, coz
when yer girl rouses on yer fer a thing er that
sort, it’s a moral cert she gone on yer. One night
though (don’t tell her I sezso) she was so broke-up
that she started cryin’. Well, blime, that broke
me up, too; so I says, ‘Well, will yer splice it?’
‘Ribuck,’ says she, so I sells my new ’arness an’
‘ ‘ Wot! the one wid all the brasses and red
morocca on ? ”
“ Yes.”
“Strewth ! ”
“ Yes, I sells it ’n’ borrows a fiver on my cart,
’n’ ’ere you’s are to-night. But, I say, don’t none
o’ you’s go away ; there’s a spread after. Mag’s
old woman made the blue monges.
“No beer, Nipper? ”
“Whips—but don’t give Micko none till ’e’s
quiet.”
ROBERT HAP.
The new Russian law re illegitimates is
rather advanced. The father is obliged to give
the child all that is necessary to support it, ac-
cording to the social position of the mother. Ille-
gitimates inherit their share of the father’s personal
property, but have no claim on what is entailed.
A father is allowed to legitimise an illegal child
without marrying the mother, but if he has a
lawful wife, the consent of his legal children, if
any, must be given, and if they are minors, their
mother may consent for them. Whether she is
likely to consent in Russia it is hard to say, but
the chance of her consenting in Australia doesn’t
amount to much. This law considerably (and
justly) diminishes the marrying value of a man
with a lurid Past. After all, the good woman is
entitled to look on man (if she likes) as a money-
making machine whose worth as a husband is
diminished if his income has to be shared
with three or four previous blunders. A small-
incomed man, under the Russian law, is fear-
fully handicapped, and generally has to take
somebody whose teeth overlap.


A Stringof Complaints
Languor, Depression, General Undoing, Loss of Appetite, Weakness,—
A String of Complaints Due to Hot Weather.
lUS
■■ \
Mr. G. J. Plenty, of Bowden, South
Australia, sends us his photograph
and says:
“I have a regular string of com-
plaints every summer. The excessive
heat prostrates me. I feel languid
and depressed and have a general un-
doing of my health. A great many
of my friends complain in the same
way. Lately I have found that Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla overcomes all these bad
effects and enables me to go through
the hot season without any trouble.
It revivifies my system, purifies my
blood, and gives me an unusual de-
